### [High-fidelity iSWAP gate with Double Transmon Coupler](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27080v1)

**Authors:** Tarush Tiwari, Sudhir K. Sahu, Guilhem Ribeill, Michael Senatore, Matthew D. LaHaye, Raymond W. Simmonds, Daniel L. Campbell, Archana Kamal, Leonardo Ranzani  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27080v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `QC/QI experiment` **4/5**

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates a high-fidelity 99.827% iSWAP gate between two transmon qubits using a double transmon coupler. By using parametric driving, the researchers achieved a fast 40ns gate while effectively suppressing unwanted static interactions and crosstalk. This approach provides a scalable and resource-efficient method for implementing two-qubit operations in superconducting processors.

**Why it may be interesting.** The use of parametric gates to achieve frequency-selective activation and the suppression of crosstalk is highly relevant to the control of open quantum systems and the development of scalable quantum architectures.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Achieving high-fidelity entangling gates between superconducting qubits while suppressing parasitic crosstalk and static interactions.

**Main result.** Demonstration of a parametric iSWAP gate with 99.827% fidelity in 40ns using a double transmon coupler.

**Method.** The researchers used a parametric driving scheme on a double transmon coupler (DTC) and employed robust phase estimation to calibrate non-commuting error terms.

**Model / system.** A superconducting circuit architecture consisting of two transmon qubits coupled via a recently developed double transmon coupler (DTC).

**Key observables.** Gate fidelity and gate duration.

**Important parameters / regimes.** 99.827% gate fidelity, 40ns gate duration.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of parasitic crosstalk, describes the DTC architecture, details the parametric iSWAP implementation and calibration via robust phase estimation, and presents the achieved fidelity results.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Entangling operations are at the heart of all approaches to quantum information processing. Parametric gates, in particular, offer a versatile solution to strongly couple off-resonant superconducting qubits with suppressed parasitic crosstalk to spectator qubits due to frequency-selective activation. In this work, we demonstrate a parametric iSWAP gate between two transmon qubits using the recently developed double transmon coupler (DTC). The DTC supports robust internally-defined cancellation point (``off'' state) for static interactions, while simultaneously mediating a fast parametric coupling between data qubits that can be deployed for high-fidelity two-qubit operations. We use robust phase estimation to calibrate non-commuting error terms in the parametric iSWAP gate, and achieve a 99.827% gate fidelity in 40ns without any numerical optimization. The circuit architecture and calibration techniques developed here are extensible to other gate implementations and qubit modalities, paving the way towards resource-efficient quantum information processing.

</details>

### [OAM-mode sorting with a wavefront twister](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27739v1)

**Authors:** Suman Karan, Swati Chaudhary, Harshwardhan Wanare, Anand Kumar Jha  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27739v1>

(a) A conventional wavefront rotator such as a Dove prism, oriented at angle θ0 rotates an input wavefront by angle 2θ0. (b) The proposed wavefront twister rotates an incoming wavefront by an angle θ(ρ) = aρ that varies linearly with radial location ρ. Con- sequently, the wavefront is not simply rotated but twisted. (c) The wavefront twister maps the point (ρ, ϕ) in the inci- dent wavefront to (ρ, ϕ + aρ). (d) The proposed OAM sorter scheme consisting of the wavefront twister followed by a lens of focal length f maps the OAM modes l1 and l2 to annuli of distinct radii in the focal plane, leading to...</figcaption>

**Summary.** The paper introduces a 'wavefront twister' optical element that sorts OAM modes into concentric rings of different radii. By increasing the twisting strength, the researchers demonstrate that inter-mode crosstalk can be almost entirely eliminated, offering a scalable path for high-dimensional OAM-based applications.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant to quantum optics and photonic quantum information, as it proposes a scalable method for managing high-dimensional OAM states, which are essential for high-capacity quantum communication and encoding.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The challenge of efficiently and unambiguously sorting high-dimensional Orbital Angular Momentum (OAM) modes while minimizing inter-modal crosstalk and diffraction loss.

**Main result.** The proposed 'wavefront twister' maps OAM modes to distinct annular rings at the focal plane, where increasing the twisting strength can reduce crosstalk from 15.45% to 0.05%.

**Method.** The authors use the Fresnel-Kirchhoff diffraction integral and paraxial approximation to calculate electric field intensity, employing power series expansions and hypergeometric functions for analytical evaluation.

**Model / system.** An optical system consisting of a novel 'wavefront twister' element—which applies a radially dependent rotation theta(rho) = a*rho—followed by a convex lens, acting on Laguerre-Gaussian (LG) beams.

**Key observables.** OAM index (l), mean radial position (mu), radial spread (sigma), and inter-modal crosstalk percentage.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Twisting strength (a/w0), OAM topological charge (l), and beam waist (w0).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes the paraxial approximation for LG beams and focuses on the p=0 radial mode.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the conceptual design of the twister vs. a standard rotator; Fig 2 and 3 show 2D/1D intensity profiles at different twisting strengths; Fig 4 plots radial position and spread vs. l; Fig 5 shows crosstalk vs. twisting strength.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the wavefront twister concept, provides the mathematical framework for the coordinate transformation and diffraction, presents numerical simulations of mode separation and crosstalk, and discusses scalability and limitations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We propose an OAM sorter based on a novel optical element that we refer to as a wavefront twister. It is a generalization of the conventional wavefront rotators such as the Dove prism. However, unlike a Dove prism, which simply rotates a wavefront, the rotation generated by a wavefront twister varies linearly with radial position, resulting in the twisting of the wavefront. We demonstrate that the wavefront twister, followed by a lens, maps each OAM mode to an annulus of distinct radius at the back focal plane of the lens with negligible inter-modal overlap and preserves the circular symmetry. Thus, the proposed wavefront twister offers a scalable scheme for high-dimensional OAM mode sorting, with important consequences for the practical realization of OAM-based applications.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27053"></a>
### [Resolving spurious topological entanglement entropy in stabilizer codes](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27053v1)

**Authors:** Peilun Han, Zijian Liang, Yifei Wang, Bowen Yang, Yingfei Gu, Yu-An Chen  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27053v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5**

**Summary.** This paper addresses the issue of inaccurate topological entanglement entropy measurements in stabilizer codes. By identifying the source of spurious errors, the authors propose a new calculation method that provides a clean measurement of the total quantum dimension. They further demonstrate the utility of this method by revealing topological frustration in specific code geometries.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Topological entanglement entropy (TEE) can suffer from spurious contributions that lead to an overestimation of the total quantum dimension in 2D gapped phases.

**Main result.** The authors identify the microscopic origin of spurious TEE and introduce a new concave partition method that is rigorously proven to be free of these spurious contributions.

**Method.** The study utilizes the framework of translation-invariant stabilizer codes of prime-dimensional qudits and analyzes bivariate bicycle codes on a bipartite cylinder.

**Model / system.** The work focuses on translation-invariant stabilizer codes of prime-dimensional qudits and bivariate bicycle codes on a bipartite cylinder.

**Key observables.** Topological entanglement entropy (TEE) and entanglement entropy dependence on cylinder circumference.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Prime-dimensional qudits and cylinder circumference.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The proposed prescription is specifically proven for translation-invariant stabilizer codes of prime-dimensional qudits.

**Paper structure.** The paper identifies the origin of spurious TEE, introduces a new concave partition prescription, provides a rigorous proof of its accuracy, and applies the method to study bivariate bicycle codes on a cylinder.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Topological entanglement entropy (TEE) is a key diagnostic of long-range entanglement in two-dimensional gapped phases of matter, but it can suffer from spurious contributions that overestimate the total quantum dimension of the underlying topological order. In this work, we identify the microscopic origin of spurious TEE and introduce a concave partition for computing the Levin-Wen TEE of translation-invariant stabilizer codes of prime-dimensional qudits. We rigorously prove that this prescription is free of spurious contributions. As a complementary probe, we study bivariate bicycle codes on a bipartite cylinder and show that the entanglement entropy depends sensitively on the cylinder circumference, revealing topological frustration of the underlying anyons.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27858"></a>
### [Geometric complexity in thermodynamics](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27858v1)

**Authors:** Tan Van Vu, Keiji Saito  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27858v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27858_figures/2604.27858_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Geometric framework for reset maps. (a) A state-agnostic reset map T clearing probability from a target subspace. This unified description encompasses diverse ther- modynamic operations, including cooling, information era- sure, and state copying. (b) The geometric complexity of T is defined as the minimal geodesic length between the iden- tity map 1 and T on the space of physical maps, as induced by a suitable Riemannian metric.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a new way to measure the difficulty of performing physical operations like cooling or state erasure using geometric complexity. It proves that as you try to make these operations more precise, the required physical resources (like time, energy, or control) must become infinite. This result provides a universal, dynamics-independent version of the third law of thermodynamics for both classical and quantum systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a unified, geometric version of the third law of thermodynamics that applies to both classical stochastic processes and quantum open systems, linking thermodynamic costs to information-theoretic complexity.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The authors seek a universal, dynamics-independent principle to quantify the difficulty of implementing state-reset maps, such as cooling or erasure, which currently lack a unified measure beyond specific protocols.

**Main result.** The paper establishes a fundamental complexity-error trade-off relation, e^{C} * epsilon >= 1, proving that achieving zero error in a reset operation requires diverging geometric complexity.

**Method.** The authors employ a Riemannian geometric framework, defining complexity as the minimal geodesic length on a manifold of physical maps using a tailored log-barrier Hessian metric.

**Model / system.** The framework applies to both classical stochastic maps (Markovian and non-Markovian processes) and quantum channels (CPTP maps, Lindbladian dynamics, and unitary-based protocols).

**Key observables.** Geometric complexity (C), execution error (epsilon), Shannon/von Neumann entropy, and the number of sequential protocols (N).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Dimension of the state space (d), transition rates (w), operation time (tau), and the metric parameter (alpha).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation assumes smooth paths in the space of probability distributions and focuses on maps where the output distribution is strictly positive.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1(a) illustrates a state-agnostic reset map for cooling and erasure, while Figure 1(b) defines geometric complexity as the geodesic length between the identity and a target map.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of state-resetting, develops a Riemannian geometric framework for complexity, proves the complexity-error trade-off, demonstrates universality across classical and quantum regimes, and provides analytical bounds and scaling relations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The third law of thermodynamics forbids cooling a physical system to absolute zero in a finite number of operational steps. Although this unattainability principle has been quantified for specific state-to-state transitions, a universal, dynamics-independent bound for implementing a state-agnostic reset map remains elusive. In this work, we unveil the fundamental limits of physical map implementation by deriving a trade-off relation based on geometric complexity. By analyzing continuous paths of maps on a geometric manifold, we prove that the geometric complexity of any classical stochastic map or quantum channel is bounded from below by its execution error. As a consequence, we show that achieving zero error in a state-reset operation requires a divergent geometric complexity -- a unified measure that naturally incorporates disparate physical resources, including infinite time, energetic cost, or control bandwidth. This unattainability principle holds universally across both classical and quantum regimes, establishing a strict geometric limit on the physical realization of reset operations in thermodynamic control and quantum computation.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.28003"></a>
### [The Synergistic Route to Stretched Criticality](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28003v1)

**Authors:** Lorenzo Lucarini, Sandro Meloni, Pablo Villegas  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.28003v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `scars & prethermalization` **2/5**

**Summary.** The paper shows that synergistic interactions can create a new pathway to extended criticality and Griffiths-like slow dynamics. This mechanism works by using complementary pathways to reinforce activity, and it is robust across both higher-order and nonlinear pairwise networks.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is interesting for many-body dynamics as it identifies a novel mechanism for achieving slow, non-exponential relaxation and extended criticality without relying on traditional quenched disorder.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating whether extended criticality and Griffiths-like slow dynamics can arise from mechanisms other than quenched disorder or frustration.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that synergistic interactions, which reinforce activity through complementary pathways, provide a new route to non-conventional critical phenomena and a broad distribution of relaxation rates.

**Method.** Theoretical analysis of spreading mechanisms and nonlinear dynamics within network-based models.

**Model / system.** The study considers networks featuring synergistic interactions, including systems with explicit higher-order interactions and purely pairwise systems with nonlinear dynamics.

**Key observables.** Relaxation rates and distribution of activity/spreading dynamics.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Synergistic interaction strength and network topology.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the concept of synergistic interactions, demonstrates the emergence of Griffiths-like dynamics, and shows the robustness of this mechanism across different network types and interaction orders.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Griffiths phases are typically associated with quenched disorder, while frustration gives rise to multistability and spin-glass behavior. Whether extended criticality can arise in other contexts remains an open question. Here, we show that synergistic interactions provide a distinct route to non-conventional critical phenomena. By combining spreading mechanisms that reinforce activity through complementary pathways, we uncover a broad distribution of relaxation rates, leading to Griffiths-like slow dynamics and extended criticality. We demonstrate that this mechanism is robust across networks and emerges both in systems with explicit higher-order interactions and in purely pairwise systems with nonlinear dynamics.

</details>

### [Derivation of the Born Rule and Operational Quantum Formalism in the Accessibility Framework through Boundary Reduction](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27125v1)

**Summary.** This paper proposes that quantum mechanics is an epistemic consequence of an observer's limited access to a more complex, deterministic algebraic reality. By applying a 'Balance Principle' to spectral triples, the authors derive the Standard Model's gauge group and spacetime dimension, while simultaneously deriving the Born rule as a result of an information bottleneck at the observer's interface.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for researchers in open quantum systems as it provides a structural, algebraic derivation of non-Markovian dynamics and the emergence of probabilistic, non-unitary evolution from an underlying deterministic, unitary ontology.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper seeks to derive the fundamental operational features of quantum mechanics (the Born rule, state updating, and interference) and the parameters of the Standard Model from a single, deeper algebraic principle.

**Main result.** The authors show that the Born rule and quantum formalism emerge from an 'Aperture'—a structural information bottleneck created by boundary reduction in Accessibility Theory—while the Standard Model parameters are uniquely selected by a 'Balance Principle'.

**Method.** The study employs Noncommutative Geometry (NCG) techniques, specifically using spectral triples, boundary reduction of algebras, Gleason's theorem, and Diophantine analysis to solve for complexity constraints.

**Model / system.** The framework is 'Accessibility Theory' (AT), which models an observer interacting with a deterministic, unitary 'Continuum' through a commutative, three-outcome 'Aperture' interface derived from a real graded spectral triple.

**Key observables.** Aperture record process (sequence of sector labels), transition effect operators, and context-free records.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Spacetime dimension (n=4), number of fermion generations (Ngen=3), algebraic complexity (A_disc=144), and the trace reduction ratio (xi=1/4).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes the validity of spectral triple axioms, the Principle of Universal Accessibility Balance, and specific observer-level coherence commitments such as noncontextuality and linearity.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by establishing the Accessibility Theory framework and the Balance Principle to select the Standard Model algebra; it then uses boundary reduction to define the Aperture; subsequently, it applies Gleason's theorem and algebraic constraints to derive the Born rule and quantum dynamics; finally, it discusses the emergence of non-Markovianity and the resolution of the measurement problem.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We show that the operational quantum formalism -- the Born rule, Lüders state updating, quantum interference, non-Markovian effective dynamics, and Bell inequality violation at the Tsirelson bound \(2\sqrt{2}\) -- arises within Accessibility Theory (AT) from the Aperture construction together with explicit coherence and locality assumptions stated in the paper. AT is a framework built on real graded spectral triples and a single algebraic selection principle. The Principle of Universal Accessibility Balance requires three independent measures of the complexity of a spectral triple -- its algebraic, gauge-theoretic, and geometric content -- to be exactly equal and minimized, uniquely selecting the algebra \(\C \oplus \Hbb \oplus M_3(\C)\) and with it the Standard Model gauge group, particle content, four-dimensional Lorentzian spacetime, three generations, and gravitational dynamics. Restriction to a codimension-one geometric boundary reduces this algebra to its commutative center \(\C \oplus \C \oplus \C\) -- the Aperture -- which defines a permanent information bottleneck for any embedded observer. Coherence conditions on inference through this bottleneck, together with Gleason's theorem on the 48-dimensional internal Hilbert space, uniquely determine the Born rule; the remaining operational features follow from the same observer-level framework under the stated assumptions. At the ontological level the theory is deterministic and state-realist, while the operational quantum formalism appears at the observer level as a consequence of structurally limited access to the underlying algebraic reality.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27459"></a>
### [Galilean boost invariance does not survive the trace: symmetry breaking in open quantum systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27459v1)

**Authors:** Leonardo F. Calderón, Esteban Marulanda, Santiago Morales, Leonardo A. Pachón  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27459v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27459_figures/2604.27459_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Dimensionless boost-violation parameter ℏγ/kBT across representative open-quantum-system platforms in the high-temperature Ohmic regime. Markers indicate representa- tive values quoted in the text; horizontal bars span the experi- mental ranges of γ and T [17, 31–35]. The shaded band delin- eates the crossover between the decoherence-dominated regime (left, covariant maps serve as an excellent approximation) and the dissipation-dominated regime (right, where Eq. (8) attains quantitative significance). Cold atoms in dissipative optical lat- tices span the crossover and enter the violated regime; ultra- cold molecules reach its lower edge. Bars cap at ℏγ/kBT = 1, beyond which the...</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper proves that tracing out a Galilean-invariant environment breaks the boost covariance of the resulting reduced dynamics due to the dissipative term in the master equation. It establishes that this symmetry breaking is unavoidable in any system obeying the fluctuation-dissipation theorem, though it can be suppressed through parametric driving. The work provides a scale, defined by the ratio of damping to temperature, to predict where this violation becomes experimentally significant.

**Why it may be interesting.** It identifies a fundamental kinematic obstruction in open quantum systems, showing that dissipation and boost invariance are mutually exclusive in standard models, which has direct implications for the validity of covariant approximations in cold atom and optomechanical experiments.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates whether Galilean boost invariance is preserved in the reduced dynamics of an open quantum system after tracing out the environment.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate a 'no-go' result: for bilinearly coupled systems, Galilean boost covariance is broken by the dissipative term of the master equation, and this violation cannot be avoided if the fluctuation-dissipation theorem holds.

**Method.** The study uses an operator-level analysis of the exact Hu-Paz-Zhang master equation, algebraic testing of symmetry transformations, and stochastic decomposition of the influence functional.

**Model / system.** The analysis focuses on the Caldeira-Leggett model, featuring a system particle coupled to a bath of harmonic oscillators via bilinear-coordinate interaction.

**Key observables.** The dissipative anticommutator term, damping coefficient Gamma(t)f(t), and the boost-breaking phase.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The dimensionless ratio hbar*gamma/k_B*T, which defines the crossover between negligible and substantial symmetry violation, and the squeezing rate mu.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes a factorized initial condition and focuses on the quadratic regime, though extensions to non-quadratic potentials are conjectured via stochastic decomposition.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 provides a survey of the dimensionless boost-violation parameter across various quantum platforms, showing a crossover from covariant regimes (e.g., trapped ions, nanoparticles) to violated regimes (e.g., cold atoms in optical lattices).

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining the global Galilean-invariant model, proceeds to an algebraic analysis of the master equation to locate the symmetry breaking, explores the fundamental conflict with the fluctuation-dissipation theorem, and concludes by discussing experimental platforms and potential restoration via parametric driving.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Tracing out a Galilean-invariant Caldeira-Leggett environment breaks Galilean boost covariance of the reduced dynamics, while spatial translations and rotations survive intact. An operator-level analysis of the exact Hu-Paz-Zhang master equation localizes the violation entirely in the dissipative anticommutator term, scaling with the damping coefficient $Γ(t)f(t)$. The fluctuation-dissipation theorem ties this coefficient to the absorptive bath response that drives equilibrium momentum diffusion, so for any non-trivial bath spectral density bilinear-coupled Galilean invariance, the fluctuation-dissipation theorem, and reduced boost covariance cannot hold simultaneously. The stochastic decomposition of the influence functional extends the mechanism beyond the quadratic regime. The dimensionless ratio $\hbarγ/k_\mathrm{B} T$ delineates the crossover: cold atoms in dissipative optical lattices and ultracold molecules sit at its edge. Parametric driving offers a one-directional escape: the squeezing rate that protects nonequilibrium entanglement above the standard quantum limit also suppresses boost-breaking over a driving cycle.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27838"></a>
### [Heisenberg-limited Hamiltonian learning without short-time control](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27838v1)

**Authors:** Myeongjin Shin, Junseo Lee, Changhun Oh  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27838v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27838_figures/2604.27838_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1. Comparison between previous short-time control approaches and our framework. Previous Heisenberg- limited algorithms rely on stroboscopic control and Trotterization, requiring many ultra-short evolution segments with small 𝑡min. Our framework replaces these short-time queries by long-time evolutions interleaved with learned auxiliary operations, ensuring that every query to the unknown Hamiltonian has duration at least 𝑇. Thus, the short-time bottleneck is removed while Heisenberg-limited scaling in total evolution time is retained.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27838_figures/2604.27838_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 2. Schematic overview of our iterative learning protocol. At iteration 𝑗, the current estimate 𝐻𝑗 defines a residual Hamiltonian Δ𝐻𝑗= 𝐻−𝐻𝑗. We first learn an auxiliary Hamiltonian 𝑊𝑗using only long-time evolutions under 𝐻, and then use 𝑊𝑗to emulate the residual-control primitive 𝑒−𝑖Δ𝐻𝑗𝑡. This emulated evolution reduces coefficient extraction to sparse pure-state tomography. After estimating Δ𝐻𝑗, we update and truncate the estimate to obtain 𝐻𝑗+1, preserving 𝑚-sparsity and bounded norm while halving the error.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that optimal, Heisenberg-limited Hamiltonian learning can be performed even when experimental constraints prevent the use of very short-time pulses. By using a method to emulate continuous control through longer evolution periods and learned corrections, the authors show that high-bandwidth control is not a fundamental requirement for efficient quantum system characterization.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for many-body dynamics and quantum control because it proves that the experimental difficulty of generating ultra-short, high-bandwidth pulses does not fundamentally prevent achieving the most efficient possible learning of a system's underlying physics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Determining whether Heisenberg-limited Hamiltonian learning (achieving 1/epsilon scaling in total evolution time) is possible when access to arbitrarily short-time dynamics is restricted by a minimum evolution time T.

**Main result.** The authors prove that Heisenberg-limited scaling can be achieved without short-time control, resolving an open problem. They establish that for logarithmically sparse Hamiltonians, any constant T is sufficient, while for polynomially sparse systems, a quantitative tradeoff exists between minimum evolution time and total evolution time.

**Method.** The framework emulates continuous quantum control by replacing short-time Trotter steps with long-time queries to the unknown Hamiltonian interleaved with learned correction unitaries. The task is then reduced to sparse pure-state tomography.

**Model / system.** An n-qubit, m-sparse, traceless Hamiltonian H expressed as a sum of Pauli operators with unknown real coefficients.

**Key observables.** Pauli coefficients of the Hamiltonian, total evolution time, and error in the Hamiltonian estimate (l-infinity norm).

**Important parameters / regimes.** m (sparsity), T (minimum evolution time), epsilon (target accuracy), and n (number of qubits).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The Hamiltonian is assumed to be m-sparse in the Pauli basis and traceless; the algorithm assumes the ability to simulate the known part of the Hamiltonian and perform sparse pure-state tomography.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares previous approaches relying on stroboscopic control/Trotterization with small minimum evolution times against the new framework using long-time evolutions and learned auxiliary operations.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the short-time bottleneck problem, defines the Hamiltonian model and sparsity regimes, presents the long-time emulation framework and correction generator learning, provides mathematical proofs for logarithmic and polynomial sparsity regimes, and concludes with the implications for quantum learning.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Characterizing quantum systems by learning their underlying Hamiltonians is a central task in quantum information science. While recent algorithmic advances have achieved near-optimal efficiency in this task, they critically rely on accessing arbitrarily short-time dynamics. This reliance poses severe experimental challenges due to finite control bandwidth and transient pulse errors. In this work, we demonstrate that Heisenberg-limited Hamiltonian learning can be achieved without short-time control. We introduce a framework in which every query to the unknown dynamics has duration at least a prescribed minimum time $T$, and show that this restriction does not preclude Heisenberg-limited scaling. The key ingredient is a method for emulating the continuous quantum control required by iterative learning algorithms using only such lower-bounded evolution times. This reduces the learning task to sparse pure-state tomography. Notably, for logarithmically sparse Hamiltonians, our algorithm achieves the information-theoretically optimal $1/\varepsilon$ scaling in total evolution time for any arbitrary constant minimum evolution time $T$. For many-body (polynomially sparse) systems, we uncover a rigorous quantitative tradeoff, showing that the minimum required evolution time can be significantly relaxed from the standard limit at a polynomial cost in total evolution time. Our results affirmatively resolve a prominent open problem in the field and reveal that high-bandwidth, ultra-short pulses are not fundamentally necessary for optimal quantum learning.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27220"></a>
### [Multirate characterization of relaxation mechanisms for two nonequivalent nuclear spins 1/2 in a liquid using maximally entangled pseudo-pure quantum states](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27220v1)

**Authors:** Georgiy Baroncha, Alexander Perepukhov, Boris V. Fine  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27220v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `QC/QI experiment` **3/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5** · `quantum measurements` **1/5**

**Summary.** The paper presents a method to simultaneously measure eight different relaxation rates in a two-spin NMR system using entangled Bell states. It identifies a universal ratio for diagonal relaxation rates and reveals an unconventional relaxation mechanism caused by weak couplings to distant spins. This approach provides a powerful tool for fingerprinting complex molecular dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to open quantum systems as it demonstrates the ability to selectively initialize and characterize the eigenmodes of off-diagonal relaxation using entangled states, providing insight into complex decoherence mechanisms.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to characterize multiple relaxation mechanisms in a two-spin system by accessing both diagonal and off-diagonal elements of the density matrix.

**Main result.** The authors successfully accessed 8 relaxation rates using Bell pseudo-pure states and identified a universal dimensionless ratio of diagonal relaxation rates determined by intra-pair magnetic dipolar interaction.

**Method.** The researchers used a multirate NMR approach, employing a new method based on a detuned Hartmann-Hahn double resonance condition to create maximally entangled Bell pseudo-pure states (Bell PPSs).

**Model / system.** A system of two non-equivalent nuclear spins 1/2 (1H and 13C) belonging to a molecule in a liquid, stable against chemical exchange.

**Key observables.** 8 relaxation rates, including inversion recovery, nuclear Overhauser effect, and off-diagonal relaxation rates.

**Important parameters / regimes.** dimensionless ratio of diagonal relaxation rates, J-couplings with distant nuclear spins, and intra-pair magnetic dipolar interaction.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The nuclear spin pair is stable with respect to chemical exchange.

**Paper structure.** The paper presents an experimental and theoretical investigation of spin relaxation, introducing a method for Bell PPS creation, demonstrating the selective initialization of relaxation eigenmodes, and providing a microscopic theory validated by parameter-free tests.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Multirate characterization of spin responses in nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) is a promising approach to fingerprinting complex molecules in the presence of multiple relaxation mechanisms. Here we present experimental and theoretical investigations simultaneously accessing 8 relaxation rates describing the density matrix of two adjacent non-equivalent nuclear spins 1/2 ($^1$H and $^{\ 13}$C) belonging to a molecule in a liquid. The selected nuclear pair is stable with respect to chemical exchange. Some of the rates are obtained from conventional measurements of inversion recovery and nuclear Overhauser effect, while other, less conventional ones, are extracted from the relaxation initialized by the maximally entangled pseudo-pure Bell states (Bell PPSs) of the spin pair. The Bell PPSs are created using a hereby introduced method based on a detuned Hartmann-Hahn double resonance condition. Microscopic theory behind the measured relaxation rates is presented, and its consistency is demonstrated by several parameter-free tests. In particular, it is shown both theoretically and experimentally, that the eigenmodes of the off-diagonal relaxation of the two-spin density matrix can be selectively initialized using Bell PPSs. Our multirate analysis suggests that the measured off-diagonal relaxation is partly due to an unconventional mechanism arising from very weak $J$-couplings of the spin pair with fluctuating distant nuclear spins. Furthermore, we identify a dimensionless ratio of diagonal relaxation rates, which is determined exclusively by intra-pair magnetic dipolar interaction and hence possesses a universal value for a broad class of nuclear spin pairs. This value is consistent with both our experiments and other experiments reported in the literature.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

Comparison of ⟨X2⟩as a function of x after d = 10 steps of time evolution between the ideal simulation (blue line for the original circuit, orange line for the prediction of QCL circuit) and the noisy simulation (green line for the original circuit, red line for the prediction of QCL circuit) with depo- larizing noise (p = 0.01).</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27648_figures/2604.27648_fig8.jpg" alt="Figure 8">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 8.</b> FIG. 7. Total Z magnetization Ztot at x = −0.3 under ampli- tude and phase damping noise. The learned circuit preserves the constant theoretical value with high accuracy, whereas the unmitigated circuit exhibits a large systematic drift caused by noise.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27648_figures/2604.27648_fig9.jpg" alt="Figure 9">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 9.</b> FIG. 9. Comparison of ⟨Z2⟩as a function of x after d = 10 steps of time evolution between the ideal simulation (blue line for the original circuit, orange line for the prediction of QCL circuit) and the noisy simulation (green line for the original circuit, red line for the prediction of QCL circuit) with bit- flipping noise (p = 0.005).</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">

Comparison of ⟨X1⟩as a function of x after d = 10 steps of time evolution between the ideal simulation (blue line for the original circuit, orange line for the prediction of QCL circuit) and the noisy simulation (green line for the original circuit, red line for the prediction of QCL circuit) with phase damping noise (p = 0.01).</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** The paper proposes using Quantum Circuit Learning to replace deep, noisy quantum simulation circuits with shallower, learned versions. By training the circuit to respect the conserved charges of integrable spin chains, the method significantly reduces the impact of hardware noise. This approach allows for more robust and accurate simulation of long-time dynamics on near-term quantum hardware.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for many-body dynamics as it provides a physics-informed way to extend the reach of NISQ devices by using the inherent symmetries of integrable systems to combat decoherence.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Mitigating hardware noise (decoherence and gate infidelity) in the quantum simulation of integrable spin chains on NISQ devices.

**Main result.** Quantum Circuit Learning (QCL) can produce shallower, noise-resilient circuits that effectively preserve conserved charges and dynamical observables compared to the original deep, noisy circuits.

**Method.** A machine learning framework where a shallow variational circuit is trained to approximate a deeper time-evolution operator by leveraging conserved charges and a small amount of dynamical information as training signals.

**Model / system.** Integrable quantum spin chains, specifically the XXX Heisenberg model, studied for two-qubit and three-qubit systems.

**Key observables.** Conserved charges (total magnetization Z_tot, SU(2) generators, Hamiltonian H) and dynamical observables (expectation values like <Z1>, <X1>, <Y1>).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Circuit depth (D and d), noise probability (p), coupling constant (J), and number of qubits (L).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method relies on the existence of conserved charges (integrability) and assumes a sufficiently small time step for the Trotterized approximation.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare predictions of learned circuits vs. true data, demonstrate accuracy improvements across different circuit depths, and show the suppression of noise-induced decay in conserved quantities under various noise models (bit-flip, depolarizing, amplitude, and phase damping).

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the noise mitigation problem, proposes the QCL strategy using integrability, details the circuit architecture and training process, presents numerical results for L=2 and L=3 systems under various noise channels, and discusses scalability and future directions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We propose a noise-mitigation quantum simulation strategy for near-term quantum devices based on Quantum Circuit Learning (QCL), which is in particular effective for integrable quantum spin chains. The method trains a shallow variational circuit to approximate a deeper time-evolution circuit by learning the conserved charges and only a small amount of dynamical information in the system. Under realistic noise models, the learned circuit maintains both conserved quantities and dynamical observables significantly closer to their true values than the noisy simulation of the original circuit. This demonstrates QCL as an effective, physics-informed error mitigation strategy, producing shorter, more robust circuits without exponential sampling overhead.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.28009"></a>
### [Learning quantum disentanglement scheduling from reduced states via modular hybrid policies](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28009v1)

**Authors:** Y. -X. Xiao, J. -Z. Han, Z. Zheng, Z. -H. Zhang, M. Xue, J. Li, X. Lv  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.28009v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

**Summary.** The paper proposes a hybrid quantum-classical machine learning approach to control the disentanglement of multiple qubits using only partial information from reduced density matrices. It reveals that classical preprocessing is crucial for success and provides design principles for scaling the quantum component of the controller.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is relevant for researchers in open quantum systems and quantum control as it addresses the practical challenge of managing many-body entanglement using only local, noisy, or incomplete information, which is a hallmark of near-term quantum device operations.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing an effective control strategy for multiqubit disentanglement when only partial information (two-qubit reduced density matrices) is available.

**Main result.** The study identifies that classical preprocessing is the primary driver of performance, while the quantum module acts as a compact latent representation; it also finds that increasing circuit width is more effective than increasing depth for policy efficiency.

**Method.** A modular hybrid quantum-classical policy framework involving classical preprocessing, a parameterized quantum circuit (PQC) as a nonlinear latent block, and classical postprocessing for pair-selection probabilities.

**Model / system.** A multi-qubit system (benchmarked on 4, 5, and 6-qubit tasks) undergoing a disentanglement scheduling process controlled by a hybrid agent.

**Key observables.** Two-qubit reduced density matrices and pair-selection probabilities.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Circuit width and circuit depth of the quantum module, and the availability of model budget.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The controller has restricted access to the full wave-function and can only observe reduced density matrices.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of partial-observation control, describes the modular hybrid policy architecture, presents benchmarking results across different qubit numbers, and analyzes the performance-efficiency trade-offs and circuit scaling.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum control with restricted state access is central to near-term quantum devices, where full wave-function information is unavailable. We study this problem through multiqubit disentanglement scheduling from partial observations, where a controller receives only two-qubit reduced density matrices and selects which qubit pair to disentangle at each step. We introduce a modular hybrid quantum--classical policy framework consisting of classical preprocessing, a parameterized quantum circuit as a compact nonlinear latent block, and classical postprocessing for pair-selection probabilities. Benchmarking 4-, 5-, and 6-qubit tasks, we find that preprocessing is the dominant factor governing performance under reduced-state observations, while the quantum module provides a conditional compact representation whose utility depends on the input features and model budget. We further identify a performance--efficiency trade-off across policy families and find that increasing circuit width is generally more useful than increasing depth. These results provide practical design principles for hybrid policies in reduced-information quantum control.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27173"></a>
### [Quantum Coordination without Conditioning under Restricted Information](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27173v1)

**Authors:** Faisal Shah Khan  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27173v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `quantum measurements` **3/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27173_figures/2604.27173_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Fig. 1. Tree representation of the binary illustrative example. The dashed line indicates that the two second-step nodes are indistinguishable under the available information, so the local rule generating X2 cannot depend on the realized value of X1.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that quantum correlations can enable coordination in sequential tasks where classical agents fail due to an inability to condition on past history. It identifies two distinct quantum mechanisms—shared latent variables and quantum discord—that allow for the implementation of otherwise unattainable joint distributions. Ultimately, it positions quantum correlations as a partial substitute for the classical property of perfect recall.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides fundamental insights into how quantum resources like discord can compensate for missing information in sequential processes, which is relevant to understanding the limits of quantum control and communication in networked systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating whether quantum correlations can implement joint distributions that are classically unattainable when agents are subject to information constraints like imperfect recall.

**Main result.** Quantum systems can act as a partial substitute for perfect recall, using both classical latent-variable encoding in separable states and non-commuting local structures (quantum discord) to achieve coordination impossible for classical local rules.

**Method.** The study uses a probabilistic framework comparing classical local conditional rules against quantum measurements (POVMs) on shared states, applying extensions of Kuhn's theorem to imperfect recall settings.

**Model / system.** A multi-stage sequential decision process involving agents (Alice, Bob, etc.) sharing multipartite quantum states, including both separable/diagonal states and discordant states.

**Key observables.** Target joint distributions (specifically anti-correlated distributions), quantum discord, and local measurement outcomes.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Information constraints (restricted access to past history $Y_k$), presence of quantum discord, and the degree of non-commutativity in local measurement bases.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes no signaling between agents and that quantum correlations are fixed in advance and cannot reproduce fully adaptive dependence on indistinguishable past outcomes.

**Figures summary.** A tree representation of a binary sequential process where dashed lines indicate nodes that are indistinguishable due to information constraints.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of coordination under restricted information, defines the classical and quantum models, proves two theorems regarding diagonal and discordant mechanisms for coordination, and discusses the fundamental limitations of quantum models compared to perfect recall.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study coordination under restricted information, where classical local models fail to implement certain correlated distributions because agents cannot condition on past history. We show that quantum systems overcome this limitation even when using only separable states. Both classically diagonal encodings (shared latent variables) and separable states with noncommuting local structure (quantum discord) enable the implementation of joint distributions that are unattainable by any classical local rules under the same information constraints. The quantum advantage arises from enabling latent-variable coordination without requiring agents to condition on the latent variable itself -- a construction that succeeds where no classical local model can. Separable states with nonzero quantum discord provide an alternative mechanism for realizing such coordination. At the same time, quantum models remain strictly limited by the information structure: unlike perfect recall, they cannot reproduce fully adaptive dependence on realized past outcomes that are observationally indistinguishable. Thus, quantum correlations serve as a partial substitute for perfect recall.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27171"></a>
### [Structure-Aware Transformers for Learning Near-Optimal Trotter Orderings with System-Size Generalization in 1D Heisenberg Hamiltonians](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27171v1)

**Authors:** Shamminuj Aktar, Reuben Tate, Stephan Eidenbenz  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27171v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5**

This trend shows that structured orderings become increasingly...</figcaption>

Dataset generation (left): For a given 1D XXZ Hamiltonian (L, ∆, g), we construct the commutation graph C(H) from Pauli terms and apply four coloring methods (xyz groups, greedy, gurobi, handcrafted), producing C = 24 candidate orderings (4×3! = 24). For each candidate ordering, we compute the fidelity F(σ) = |⟨ψexact|ψTrotter(σ)⟩|2 between the exact and Trotter-evolved states. These fidelities are used during training only: the oracle fidelity, defined as the best fidelity among the 24 structured candidates, provides the supervision label. Model learning (right): Per-term local features and global...</figcaption>
</figure>

Points are colored by method and shaped by Trotter regime (p, r), and the dashed line marks oracle-level performance y = x. The learned selector remains more tightly concentrated near the oracle line overall, while MajRegime shows substantially larger deviations, especially in the lower-fidelity regime.</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27171_figures/2604.27171_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Figure 4: Error landscape: fidelity gap f∗−f model versus transverse field g for each Trotter regime, averaged over five seeds on test sizes L ∈{16, 17, 18, 19, 20}. Color indicates anisotropy ∆, and the dashed line marks the poor-prediction threshold (gap = 0.01). Under first-order Trotter (top row), the poor-prediction rate remains low at 0.7% for r = 3 and 1.8% for r = 5. Under second-order Trotter (bottom row), failures are more frequent, with poor-prediction rates of 6.0% for r = 3 and 6.0% for r = 5. The largest gaps concentrate at g ≳2.0, where oracle fidelity is already low and differences between candidate orderings become more pronounced.</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27171_figures/2604.27171_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> Figure 5: Generalization sweep. Mean fidelity gap versus system size L for each validation size Lmax ∈{5, . . . , 15}, where training uses L ∈{3, . . . , Lmax −1} and evaluation is performed on larger sizes up to L = 20 (one line per Lmax, mean over three seeds). The shaded region marks the final held-out test range L ∈{16, . . . , 20}, and the dashed horizontal line marks the MajRegime reference. Lines for Lmax ≤8 remain near 10−2 on larger systems, indicating limited generalization. Generalization emerges at Lmax = 9, and lines for Lmax ≥13 compress toward 10−3 with little growth across system size, confirming clean extrapolation to larger systems.</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27171_figures/2604.27171_fig6.jpg" alt="Figure 6">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 6.</b> Figure 6: Sample efficiency: mean test fidelity gap versus the number of Hamiltonians per system size used in training. Colored lines show held-out test sizes L ∈{16, 17, 18, 19, 20}, and the dashed line marks the average MajRegime reference. By nham = 30, all plotted test sizes fall below the MajRegime reference and stabilize thereafter, indicating that 30 Hamiltonians per system size are sufficient for near-oracle generalization in this setting.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** The paper presents a Transformer-based machine learning model designed to select the most accurate sequence of Hamiltonian terms for Trotterized quantum simulations. By using a size-invariant input representation, the model successfully generalizes from small training systems to much larger, unseen 1D Heisenberg chains. This approach significantly outperforms standard baseline policies and reduces the computational overhead of finding optimal simulation parameters.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant for many-body dynamics as it provides an AI-driven method to reduce approximation errors in quantum simulations, potentially enabling more accurate long-time evolution of complex spin systems on near-term quantum hardware.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Optimizing the ordering of non-commuting Hamiltonian terms in Trotterization to maximize simulation fidelity, specifically addressing the exponential complexity of the search space and the need for system-size generalization.

**Main result.** A structure-aware Transformer encoder can predict near-optimal Trotter orderings for unseen, larger systems (up to 20 qubits) with a mean fidelity gap of only 0.00115 relative to the best of 24 structured candidates.

**Method.** The authors use a supervised learning approach with a Transformer encoder that takes size-invariant Hamiltonian features and global context as input to classify the best ordering from a predefined set of 24 candidates derived from commutation graph colorings.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on 1D XXZ Heisenberg Hamiltonians with an external transverse field, decomposed into local Pauli terms, using the Néel state as the initial state for time evolution.

**Key observables.** State fidelity (squared overlap between exact and Trotter-evolved states), mean fidelity gap, normalized gain, and poor-prediction rate.

**Important parameters / regimes.** System size (L), Trotter orders (p=1, 2), step counts (r=3, 5), anisotropy (Delta), and transverse field strength (g).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The search is restricted to a predefined set of 24 structured candidate orderings rather than the full permutation space; the study is limited to 1D XXZ Hamiltonians and a fixed target time.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares random vs. structured orderings; Figure 2 shows the dataset generation and model learning pipeline; Figure 3 shows the model tracking the oracle fidelity; Figure 4 shows the error landscape across field strengths; Figure 5 shows the generalization sweep across system sizes; Figure 6 shows sample efficiency.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the Trotter ordering problem, describes the construction of the candidate set via commutation graph coloring, details the Transformer architecture and size-invariant feature encoding, presents results on system-size generalization and sample efficiency, and concludes with limitations and future directions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Trotterization is a standard approach for simulating quantum time evolution on quantum computers, where the Hamiltonian is split into local terms and each term is applied in sequence. The order of these terms affects the fidelity of the simulation when they do not commute, so the choice of ordering directly impacts the accuracy of the simulation. We study this problem for one-dimensional XXZ Heisenberg Hamiltonians using a structured set of 24 candidate orderings derived from colorings of the Hamiltonian's commutation graph and their group permutations. Finding the best candidate for large systems becomes prohibitive because fidelity evaluation is computationally expensive. In this work, we train a transformer encoder on smaller systems to predict the best candidate ordering for larger systems directly from Hamiltonian and Trotter-configuration features, without computing candidate fidelities at inference time. The model is trained on in-range systems of 3 to 14 qubits with 15-qubit systems held out for validation. Experimental results show that the model reaches a mean test fidelity gap of 0.00115 relative to the best of the 24 candidates on out-of-range systems of 16 to 20 qubits. A training-size sweep further shows that generalization emerges once training includes systems up to L=8 qubits, with validation at L=9, and the gap continues to decrease as the training range grows. To our knowledge, this is the first application of a learned model to Trotter ordering, and it motivates future work on AI-guided Trotter ordering with generalization across Hamiltonian families and system types.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27809"></a>
### [Bosonic Josephson junction dynamics: interplay between quantum and thermal fluctuations](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27809v1)

**Authors:** Andrea Bardin, Francesco Lorenzi, Luca Salasnich  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum gases · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27809v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5**

**Summary.** This paper extends the mean-field description of Bosonic Josephson junctions by incorporating both quantum and thermal fluctuations. It demonstrates that these two types of fluctuations act in opposition to influence the system's dynamics. The authors conclude that in current experimental setups, quantum fluctuations are the dominant correction.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a precise understanding of how fluctuations modify fundamental nonlinear phenomena like self-trapping and symmetry breaking, which is crucial for controlling many-body dynamics in ultracold gases.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how thermal and quantum fluctuations affect the superfluid dynamics of a Bosonic Josephson junction beyond the mean-field approximation.

**Main result.** The study finds that quantum and thermal fluctuations play opposite roles: thermal fluctuations decrease the Josephson frequency, while both increase the thresholds for macroscopic quantum self-trapping and spontaneous symmetry breaking.

**Method.** A semianalytical approach using a formalism that accounts for fluctuations in a homogeneous gas to derive a corrected equation of motion within a two-site approximation.

**Model / system.** A Bosonic Josephson junction modeled within a two-site approximation, considering the dynamics of a superfluid gas.

**Key observables.** Josephson frequency, strength of macroscopic quantum self-trapping, and the threshold for spontaneous symmetry breaking.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Regimes where quantum fluctuations dominate over thermal fluctuations, assessed via parameters from recent experimental realizations.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The transport of the non-condensed component is assumed to be negligible.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of fluctuations in Josephson junctions, presents a corrected equation of motion, analyzes the effects on dynamical quantities, and compares theoretical predictions with experimental regimes.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the superfluid dynamics of a Josephson junction beyond the mean-field description, incorporating the role of thermal fluctuations as well as quantum fluctuations. Using a formalism that accounts for the fluctuations in a homogeneous gas, and under the assumption that the transport of the non-condensed component is negligible, we derive a corrected equation of motion within the two-site approximation. The resulting corrections for the typical dynamical quantities, like the Josephson frequency, the strength of macroscopic quantum self-trapping, and the threshold for spontaneous symmetry breaking, allow us to predict the effects of both types of fluctuations and assess their relative importance in different regimes in a semianalytical fashion. For all the dynamical quantities, the quantum fluctuations are shown to play an opposite role with respect to the thermal fluctuations. Josephson frequency is decreased by thermal fluctuations and both the critical strenghts of macroscopic quantum self trapping and spontaneous symmetry breaking are increased. We assess the experimentally accessible regimes by calculating the relevant parameters of recent experimental realizations of Bosonic Josephson junction and show that the expected regime is dominated by quantum fluctuations.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Cylindrical Matter: A beyond-quantum many-body system for efficient classical simulation of quantum pure-Ising like systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27248v1)

**Authors:** Sahar Atallah, Peter Carrekmor, Michael Garn, Yukuan Tao, Shashank Virmani

**Summary.** This paper introduces 'cylindrical matter,' a beyond-quantum many-body system used to efficiently classically simulate certain quantum Ising-like systems. By using a state space that allows for non-positive semi-definite operators, the authors find a way to sample measurement outcomes of entangled quantum states using a classical model. This approach identifies new regimes of classical simulability for long-range interacting quantum systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a novel theoretical framework for classical simulation using non-quantum models, which could offer new insights into the computational complexity of many-body dynamics and the limits of quantum advantage.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Determining the boundaries of classical simulability for quantum many-body systems by exploring whether 'beyond-quantum' descriptions can provide efficient classical simulation of certain entangled states.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that a hypothetical 'cylindrical matter' model can efficiently classically simulate various quantum pure-Ising-like systems, including those with power-law decaying interactions with exponents alpha > 3D/2.

**Method.** The study uses a continuous-time dynamical model of 'cylindrical bits' and analyzes the 'radial growth' of state space required to maintain a separable decomposition under diagonal unitary interactions.

**Model / system.** A many-body system of 'cylindrical bits' where each particle's state space is a cylinder in R3, interacting via diagonal Hamiltonians (Ising-like) on a graph with power-law decaying strengths.

**Key observables.** Measurement outcomes in the Z eigenbasis or the XY plane (aX + bY), and the growth factor of the cylinder radius.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Cylindrical radius (r), interaction decay exponent (alpha), spatial dimension (D), and the phase parameter (phi) of the diagonal gates.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model is 'non-free' due to required spatial constraints to ensure valid probabilities; measurements are assumed to be destructive or quasi-destructive; and the simulation assumes diagonal quantum circuit evolution can be computed efficiently.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the cylindrical state space protruding from the Bloch sphere; Figure 2 plots the growth factor as a function of gate phase, peaking at the CZ gate; Figure 3 presents a phase diagram of interaction decay versus allowable initial angle.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the cylindrical bit model, defines the mathematical framework for cylindrical separability, analyzes the growth factor of the radius under diagonal unitaries, establishes simulation conditions for power-law interactions, and compares the optimality of the cylindrical state space against alternatives.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Even simplified models of quantum many-body systems can be difficult to analyse. However, taking inspiration from the foundations of physics, one may wonder whether there are practical advantages to constructing alternative beyond-quantum descriptions of many-body systems. We explore this question in the context of quantum interactions that are diagonal in the computational basis. We construct a hypothetical model of a continuous time dynamical many-body system that is based upon lattices of interacting particles called "cylindrical bits", a concept first introduced in [6]. In the language of [5] our toy model is {\it non-free}, as we need spatial constraints on how the particles interact to ensure valid probabilities. We investigate these constraints and explore the resulting `entangled' states that can exist. Certain pure {\it quantum} entangled systems can be faithfully mimicked by our cylindrical worlds. This allows us to simulate efficiently classically, in the sense of sampling measurement outcomes, a variety of previously unknown quantum systems. Examples include some states created by pure Ising interactions algebraically decaying faster than $\sim 1/r^{3D/2}$, with spatial dimension $D$, under measurements in the $Z$ eigenbasis or eigenbases of $aX+bY$ for $a,b \in \mathbb{R}$. We also explore whether another choice of non-quantum `particle' could expand the applicability of the classical simulation by defining and partially optimising a figure-of-merit that attempts to capture how useful various possibilities may be.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

**Summary.** This paper examines how the topology of a complete graph affects the universality classes of interface growth models. It finds that while some models retain continuum-like features, others like Ballistic Deposition deviate significantly, exhibiting new growth regimes due to the infinite-dimensional connectivity.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates whether discrete lattice growth models (RSOS and BD) faithfully reproduce the behavior of their corresponding continuum equations (EW and KPZ) when defined on a complete graph, representing the infinite-dimensional limit.

**Main result.** While the RSOS model shows similarities to continuum equations, the BD model exhibits an 'ultrafast' growth regime and saturation roughness that increases with system size, placing it outside the KPZ universality class on a complete graph.

**Method.** Numerical Monte Carlo-style simulations and analytical derivations using probabilistic modeling and scaling analysis.

**Model / system.** Discrete interface growth models, specifically the Restricted Solid-on-Solid (RSOS) and Ballistic Deposition (BD) models, implemented on a complete graph (all-to-all connectivity).

**Key observables.** Global interfacial roughness (w), height fluctuations, power spectra (S(omega)), and two-time height autocorrelation functions.

**Important parameters / regimes.** System size (N), height constraint (m), and growth regimes (Random Deposition, ultrafast growth, and saturation).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The complete graph is assumed to be a valid proxy for the infinite-dimensional limit of statistical mechanical systems.

**Figures summary.** Plots showing squared roughness evolution over time, scaling of saturation roughness with system size, histograms of rescaled height fluctuations showing left tails, and the frequency-dependent power spectrum.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of growth on complete graphs, analyzes specific models (RSOS, BD, etc.), compares discrete results to continuum benchmarks, and concludes with discussions on universality and dimensionality.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the behavior of discrete interface growth models belonging to the Edwards--Wilkinson (EW) and Kardar--Parisi--Zhang (KPZ) universality classes, when defined on a complete graph, a topology commonly used to probe the infinite-dimensional limit of statistical mechanical systems. Our aim is to assess to what extent discrete lattice models reproduce the behavior of their corresponding continuum equations in this highly connected setting. After assessing the trivial behavior shown by some well known cases (like random deposition with surface relaxation or the etching model), we focus on two paradigmatic models associated with the KPZ universality class, the Restricted Solid-on-Solid (RSOS) and Ballistic Deposition (BD) models, and assess non-trivial behavior through several observables including the roughness, height fluctuations, power spectra, and two-time autocorrelation functions. Still, despite similarities with continuum equations, important differences arise in the fluctuations and long-time dynamics. In both discrete models the rescaled height fluctuations display a pronounced left tail, indicating the presence of lagging nodes. While the RSOS model exhibits a saturation roughness that decreases with system size, similarly to the EW and KPZ equations, the BD model exhibits a saturation roughness that increases with system size and an additional ultrafast growth regime, placing it outside the KPZ universality class on a complete graph.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proposes a framework where multi-qubit entangled states are used to represent and study the structural properties of tripartite graphs. By linking quantum observables like entanglement distance to graph metrics like 4-cycles, it demonstrates that quantum programming can be an effective tool for investigating complex classical graph topologies.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to develop a method for constructing multi-qubit entangled states that represent weighted tripartite graphs and to establish a quantitative link between quantum properties (entanglement and correlators) and graph structural properties.

**Main result.** The study establishes that quantum correlators and entanglement distance are explicitly determined by graph features like vertex degrees, common neighbors, and 4-cycles, and validates these analytical predictions using noisy quantum simulations.

**Method.** The authors use analytical derivations for spin expectation values and entanglement distance, complemented by quantum simulations on the IBM AerSimulator with realistic noise models (readout, Pauli-X, and CNOT errors).

**Model / system.** The system consists of multi-qubit quantum graph states constructed from weighted and directed tripartite graphs G(U, V, W, E) using specific two-qubit rotation gates (R_XY, R_XZ, R_YZ) applied to an initial separable state.

**Key observables.** Entanglement distance (E_ED), quantum correlators (Pauli expectation values such as <sigma_x sigma_x>, <sigma_y sigma_y>, and <sigma_z sigma_z>), and mean spin values.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Edge weights (phi_kl), initial state parameters (theta_k, alpha_k), vertex degrees, and graph topological features like 4-cycles and common neighbors.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation assumes a tripartite structure where no edges exist within the same vertex set, and that the rotation gates commute due to this structure; simplified formulas assume unweighted graphs and uniform parameters.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a quantum circuit for a triangle-based state; Figures 2, 4, and 6 compare analytical entanglement distance surfaces with noisy simulation data; Figures 3, 5, and 7 plot the absolute error between analytical and simulated results.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the construction method for tripartite quantum graph states, provides analytical derivations for entanglement and correlators, establishes the mapping between quantum and graph structural properties, and concludes with a numerical validation using noisy quantum simulations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We propose a method for constructing multi-qubit entangled quantum states representing weighted tripartite graphs. An expression for the entanglement distance for multi-qubit states corresponding to arbitrary tripartite graph structures is obtained. The entanglement of a qubit with the rest of the system in a quantum graph state is determined by the weights of the edges in the closed neighborhood of the corresponding vertex and by its degree with respect to other sets. We also calculate quantum correlators in the general case of tripartite quantum graph states. We establish a relationship between these quantum properties and the structural properties of the corresponding tripartite graphs, including the number of non-overlapping neighbors, the number of common neighbors of the corresponding vertices, and the number of 4-cycles. As an illustrative example, we consider a tripartite graph forming a triangle and compute the entanglement distance using quantum simulations on the AerSimulator with noise models. The numerical results are consistent with the theoretical predictions. The obtained results demonstrate that quantum graph states provide an effective framework for studying structural properties of tripartite graphs. They open up the possibility of investigating such properties using quantum programming. It is worth highlighting that tripartite graphs have applications in solving practical problems such as resource allocation, scheduling, and database and hypergraph modeling.

</details>

### [Nodal algebraic curves and entropy diagnostics in degenerate two-dimensional harmonic-oscillator shells](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28127v1)
