**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that the topological changes in the nodal patterns of 2D harmonic oscillator states are governed by algebraic singularities. It shows that while global entropies remain smooth, nodal-domain entropy can sharply detect these topology-changing events. This framework is relevant for characterizing structured light and trapped particle systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a rigorous mathematical framework for understanding the fine-scale structure of wavefunctions in degenerate systems, which is directly applicable to the control of structured light and the manipulation of motional states in trapped ion/atom experiments.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how the topological restructuring of nodal geometry (the zero-set of wavefunctions) within degenerate quantum eigenspaces is organized and how it can be quantified using entropy diagnostics.

**Main result.** The study finds that nodal geometry is organized by algebraic stratification (singularities and degeneracies) rather than spectral ordering, and that nodal-domain entropy is a sensitive diagnostic for topology changes that global entropies like Shannon entropy fail to detect.

**Method.** The authors use the theory of real algebraic curves to identify topological transitions via finite singularities and projective degeneracies, combined with information-theoretic diagnostics like nodal-domain entropy and mutual information.

**Model / system.** A two-dimensional isotropic quantum harmonic oscillator with a fixed energy shell N, where wavefunctions are real-phase Hermite-Gaussian superpositions.

**Key observables.** Nodal-domain entropy (S_dom), Cartesian mutual information (I(x;y)), configuration-space Shannon entropy (S_r), momentum-space Shannon entropy (S_p), and the entropic uncertainty sum (S_r + S_p).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Principal quantum number N, mixing parameter t, and the oscillator scaling parameter alpha.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is restricted to real-phase wavefunctions (no phase singularities/vortices) and focuses on fixed energy shells.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows mutual information vs. mixing parameter for N=1; Figure 2 illustrates nodal geometry evolution for N=2; Figure 6 shows nodal plots for N=3 at various mixing stages; Figure 7 shows nodal-domain entropy curves.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of nodal restructuring, establishes a mathematical framework using algebraic geometry, provides a theorem for regularity, analyzes specific low-N shells (N=1, 2, 3, 4) to demonstrate topological transitions, and concludes with experimental implications for optics and trapped ions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Degenerate quantum eigenspaces can support substantial changes in nodal geometry at fixed energy. We show that, for the two-dimensional isotropic harmonic oscillator, this restructuring is organized by the Hermite-constrained algebraic curve $P_N(x,y)=0$ appearing in every real shell state, $ψ_N=e^{-αr^2/2}P_N(x,y)$. Finite singularities, $P_N=\nabla P_N=0$, and projective degeneracies of the leading homogeneous part identify the strata where topology-changing events can occur. We combine these criteria with entropy diagnostics: the nodal-domain entropy $S_{\mathrm{dom}}$, Cartesian mutual information $I(x;y)$, and the entropic uncertainty sum $S_r+S_p$. The first three shells reveal a hierarchy: $N=1$ only rotates a nodal line; $N=2$ has a conic transition at $b^2=2ac$, sharply detected by $S_{\mathrm{dom}}$ but not by global entropies; and $N=3$ supports cubic close-branch regimes organized by the projective discriminant, with enhanced responses in $S_{\mathrm{dom}}$ and $I(x;y)$. Thus algebraic stratification, rather than spectral ordering, organizes nodal geometry inside a degenerate eigenspace, while entropy diagnostics quantify probability redistribution and correlation. The framework suggests experimentally reconstructible signatures for real-phase Hermite--Gaussian structured light and approximately isotropic trapped motional systems.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27130"></a>
### [Optimised Inference of Quantum Phenomena in High-Energy Collider Experiments](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27130v1)

**Authors:** Hai-Chau Nguyen, Gilberto Tetlalmatzi-Xolocotzi, Carmen Diez Pardos, Otfried Gühne, Matthias Kleinmann  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27130v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

**Summary.** This paper introduces a new method for detecting quantum entanglement in particle collider experiments using shadow tomography. By addressing the challenges of spin-momentum coupling in relativistic regimes, the framework allows for more efficient characterization of spin correlations. The method is demonstrated on top quark production at the LHC.

**Why it may be interesting.** It demonstrates the application of quantum information protocols (shadow tomography) to extract entanglement signatures from complex, high-energy relativistic systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Characterizing spin-spin entanglement in high-energy collider experiments is difficult because relativistic motion couples spin and momentum, and particle momenta are not experimentally controlled.

**Main result.** The development of a general framework using shadow tomography to efficiently infer spin-spin correlations and entanglement in collider environments.

**Method.** The authors utilize shadow tomography techniques to reconstruct quantum properties from experimental data.

**Model / system.** The framework is applied to top quark pair production at the Large Hadron Collider (LHC) as a proof of concept, though it is designed for general high-energy particle systems.

**Key observables.** spin-spin correlations and spin-spin entanglement

**Assumptions / limitations.** The framework assumes the applicability of shadow tomography to relativistic regimes where spin and momentum are coupled.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the challenge of entanglement in high-energy physics, presents a new framework based on shadow tomography, and demonstrates its utility using top quark pair production at the LHC.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Entanglement, a fundamental phenomenon of quantum theory, has recently been observed in processes in high-energy physics. This opens new avenues for probing quantum effects in relativistic regimes, but also poses conceptual and technical challenges. We develop a general framework based on shadow tomography techniques for characterising spin-spin correlations in collider experiments. This improves the analysis of spin-spin entanglement, where relativistic motion couples spin and momentum and the momenta of the investigated particles are not under experimental control. As a proof of concept we illustrate the application of our formalism to top quark pair production at the Large Hadron Collider at CERN. The framework, however, is general and flexible and can be readily applied to more complex final states and systems with more particles.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27135"></a>
### [Parametrized Variational Quantum Tomography](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27135v1)

**Authors:** V. A. Penas, M. Losada, D. Tielas, F. Holik  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27135v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

**Summary.** The paper introduces Parametrized Variational Quantum Tomography (PVQT), a method that uses a tunable cost function to bridge the gap between efficient but biased VQT and computationally expensive Maximum Entropy methods. By interpolating between 1-norm and infinity-norm penalties, the authors demonstrate that one can achieve higher-fidelity state reconstruction in underdetermined measurement scenarios.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for quantum information researchers as it provides a more efficient, scalable alternative to Maximum Entropy tomography, offering a way to control the bias in state reconstruction for noisy, incomplete experimental data.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Addressing the ambiguity and intrinsic bias in quantum state reconstruction when measurement data are incomplete (underdetermined).

**Main result.** The proposed Parametrized Variational Quantum Tomography (PVQT) achieves higher fidelity to the Maximum Entropy solution and higher von Neumann entropy than standard VQT or VQT_infinity by tuning hyperparameters.

**Method.** A new cost function that interpolates between the 1-norm and the infinity norm of unmeasured probabilities, formulated as a linear semidefinite program (SDP).

**Model / system.** The study focuses on n-qubit quantum systems (tested up to 5 qubits) using subsets of a Symmetric Informationally Complete POVM (SIC-POVM).

**Key observables.** Fidelity, trace distance, von Neumann entropy, and Kullback-Leibler (KL) divergence of unmeasured probabilities.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Hyperparameters alpha and beta that control the interpolation between VQT and VQT_infinity; number of measured observables K.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method assumes the reconstruction problem is underdetermined due to incomplete POVM measurements; testing is performed on Haar-random pure and mixed states.

**Figures summary.** Figures show trace distance and fidelity comparisons between MaxEnt, VQT, and VQT_infinity; histograms of fidelity distributions; and performance scaling for 3, 4, and 5-qubit systems under noise.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of incomplete tomography, defines the mathematical framework for VQT and VQT_infinity, proposes the PVQT interpolation method, presents numerical results comparing fidelity and entropy across different qubit counts, and discusses limitations regarding hyperparameter selection.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum state tomography provides a fundamental framework for reconstructing quantum states. When the measurement data are not informationally complete, the observed statistics admit multiple compatible density matrices, making the reconstruction problem inherently underdetermined and calling for the selection of a meaningful estimator. Two well-established approaches to address this ambiguity are Maximum Entropy (MaxEnt) and Variational Quantum Tomography (VQT). A variant of VQT, named VQT$_\infty$, has been introduced to reproduce MaxEnt-like solutions. In this work, we generalize this approach by introducing a parametrized cost function that interpolates between the 1-norm and the infinity norm, thereby unifying VQT and VQT$_\infty$ within a single framework. By tuning the associated hyperparameters, the proposed method enables controlled exploration of the set of compatible density matrices. We show that this interplay yields reconstructed states with higher fidelity to the MaxEnt solution than those obtained via VQT$_\infty$ while preserving computational tractability.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27216"></a>
### [Tuning of quantum nanoscaled friction within the Prandtl-Tomlinson model](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27216v1)

**Authors:** Dai-Nam Le, Lilia M. Woods  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27216v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `Frenkel-Kontorova` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

**Summary.** This paper explores how to control nanoscale friction by adjusting system parameters like corrugation and length ratios. It identifies new motion regimes and highlights how quantum effects like Landau-Zener tunneling influence frictional dynamics. These insights are crucial for controlling friction in future nanotechnological applications.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the mechanisms for controlling frictional force at both quantum and classical levels in nanoscale systems.

**Main result.** The study identifies that frictional dynamics can be tuned via corrugation and characteristic length ratio parameters, and uncovers motion types beyond the standard stick-slip regime, including the role of Landau-Zener tunneling.

**Method.** Theoretical investigation using the Prandtl-Tomlinson framework to analyze dynamical force behavior.

**Model / system.** A nanoparticle-chain system modeled within the Prandtl-Tomlinson framework to study nanoscale friction.

**Key observables.** Frictional force, dynamical force behavior, and types of motion (e.g., stick-slip).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Corrugation, characteristic length ratio, and Landau-Zener tunneling probability.

**Paper structure.** The paper utilizes the Prandtl-Tomlinson framework to systematically investigate control means of friction, explores different motion regimes, and analyzes the impact of quantum tunneling.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Nanoscaled friction is a fundamental tribological phenomenon with complex behavior of its dynamical force. Here, we utilize the Prandtl-Tomlinson framework to investigate systematically the different means of control of the frictional force at the quantum and classical levels. It is found that the frictional dynamics can be controlled by the corrugation and characteristic length ratio parameters dependent upon properties of the nanoparticle-chain system. In addition to the stick-slip regime, other types of motion are uncovered, highlighting the richness of the frictional dynamics. The importance of Landau-Zener tunneling for the quantum motion is also analyzed. These findings provide valuable insights for interpreting experimental observations and controlling quantum frictional behavior in nanoscale systems.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27440"></a>
### [Evidence for interior-gap pair-density-wave state in Kondo-Heisenberg chains](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27440v1)

**Authors:** Yuto Hirose, Shunsuke C. Furuya, Yasuhiro Tada  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27440v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **3/5**

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that an interior-gap pair-density-wave state can emerge dynamically in 1D Kondo-Heisenberg chains due to strong correlations, rather than requiring pre-existing Fermi-surface mismatch. By comparing S=1/2 and S=3/2 chains using DMRG, the authors show how the momentum distribution function develops characteristic features of interior-gap physics. This provides a new mechanism for realizing exotic superconducting states in strongly correlated systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The realization of interior-gap superconductivity in canonical strongly correlated models has been elusive, as it is traditionally associated with pre-existing Fermi-surface mismatch.

**Main result.** The authors find that the superconducting phase of 1D Kondo-Heisenberg models realizes an interior-gap pair-density-wave (PDW) state where the low-energy single-particle structure emerges dynamically from strong correlations.

**Method.** The study utilizes infinite density-matrix-renormalization-group (iDMRG) and finite DMRG calculations.

**Model / system.** One-dimensional Kondo-Heisenberg chains with spin S=1/2 and S=3/2.

**Key observables.** Pair-density-wave (PDW) correlation, momentum distribution function n(k), and real-space correlations.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Spin magnitude (S=1/2 and S=3/2), Kondo coupling, and the spin-gapped regime.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes the validity of the 1D Kondo-Heisenberg model as a representative for studying the emergence of interior-gap physics through strong correlations.

**Paper structure.** The paper presents evidence for an interior-gap PDW state, compares S=1/2 and S=3/2 chains, discusses the emergence of single-particle structure via Kondo coupling, and addresses the necessity of thermodynamic-limit calculations due to boundary effects.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Interior-gap superconductivity has long been discussed as an exotic paired state in the presence of Fermi-surface mismatch, but its realization in canonical strongly correlated models has remained elusive. Here we present evidence that the superconducting phase of one-dimensional Kondo-Heisenberg models realizes an interior-gap pair-density-wave (PDW) state generated by strong correlations. Combining infinite density-matrix-renormalization-group (iDMRG) and finite DMRG calculations for $S=1/2$ and $S=3/2$ chains, we show that the PDW correlation is the dominant bulk superconducting correlation in the spin-gapped regime and that the momentum distribution function $n(k)$ exhibits a reconstructed structure characteristic of interior-gap physics. In particular, while the feature in $n(k)$ for the $S=1/2$ chain is only hump-like, the corresponding structure in the $S=3/2$ chain develops into a clear dip, strongly supporting the interpretation in terms of an interior-gap-like dip structure. Unlike conventional interior-gap scenarios based on a mismatch between preexisting Fermi surfaces, the present system starts from a single bare conduction-electron Fermi surface, and the additional low-energy single-particle structure emerges dynamically together with the dominant PDW correlation through the Kondo coupling. Finite DMRG data further demonstrate that boundary effects can substantially modify real-space correlations in this gapless one-dimensional system, making a direct thermodynamic-limit calculation essential for identifying the intrinsic bulk momentum structure and the dominant correlation channel.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Momentum-Space Entanglement Signatures and Spinon Breakdown in the $J_1$-$J_2$ Zig-Zag Heisenberg Chain](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27048v1)

**Authors:** Tom Oeffner, Ludwig Bordfeldt, Andreas Feuerpfeil, Lukas Elter, Tobias Helbig, Tobias Hofmann, Martin Greiter, Ronny Thomale  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27048v1>

The low-lying entanglement levels are described by two SU(2)1 WZW models. (b) Entanglement spectrum within the spiral phase. Both the CFT levels as well as the intra-chain Heisenberg levels do not renormalize significantly, whilst a continuum of inter-chain excitations moves down without closing the entanglement gap.</figcaption>

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> Figure 5: Highest spinon weight W and entanglement gap ∆as a function of J1/J2 for the low-energy Kc = 0 and π state of a system with Lc = 8 unit cells and NA = 4 particles. The ground state is marked in black. We observe that W and ∆change continuously and break down only deep inside the frustrated regime J1 &lt; 0 (a) and J1 &gt; 0 (b). The points α± c of spinon confinement within the ground-state, which we identify as the gap closing for both signs of J1, is given by the change of momentum character from 0 to π. This entanglement transition does not coincide with the phase transition boundaries of the spiral (SRI) and dimer phase, as shown by the linear extrapolation of this level crossing to...</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27048_figures/2604.27048_fig6.jpg" alt="Figure 6">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 6.</b> Figure 6: Measures of spinon deconfinement for L = 22, with a momentum cut containing NA = 6 particles. (a) Highest spinon weight W and direct entanglement gap ∆as a function of J2/J1. The vertical blue dashed line marks the maximum of ∆and W at 0.16. (b) Haldane–Shastry fidelity F and indirect entanglement gap ¯∆, with the maximum of ¯∆and F being shifted slightly to 0.15. Across both definitions, the observables are maximized at finite J2, marking the regime where the Heisenberg antiferromagnet most closely resembles the Haldane–Shastry chain.</figcaption>

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 7.</b> Figure 7: Entanglement spectra for Lc = 10 unit cells, with a momentum cut contain- ing NA = 5 particles. For representative values of the frustration coupling α = J1/J2, the evolution from the decoupled limit to the spiral (a) and dimer (b) phase is shown. The double-spinon framework is protected by a finite entanglement gap, which collapses as the ground state momentum character shifts from Kc = π to 0. For the considered system size, this sharp transition occurs at α− c = −2.1403(4) and α+ c = 0.9261(1), respectively.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper explores how fractionalized excitations (spinons) persist in a frustrated zig-zag spin chain. It demonstrates that the spinon description is much more robust than field theories suggest and identifies a clear asymmetry in how ferromagnetic versus antiferromagnetic coupling leads to spinon confinement.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the stability and breakdown of spinon quasiparticles in the J1-J2 zig-zag Heisenberg chain, specifically addressing the tension between field theory predictions of immediate instability and numerical evidence of a robust quasi-long-range order phase.

**Main result.** The authors find that a 'double-spinon' description remains robust over an extensive parameter regime and that spinon stability is asymmetric: ferromagnetic coupling (J1 < 0) sustains fractionalization deep into the spiral phase, while antiferromagnetic coupling (J1 > 0) drives confinement much earlier.

**Method.** The study uses Exact Diagonalization (ED) with the Lanczos algorithm, momentum-space entanglement spectroscopy, and Conformal Field Theory (CFT) analysis, treating the system as two coupled SU(2)1 Wess-Zumino-Witten models.

**Model / system.** A spin-1/2 J1-J2 zig-zag Heisenberg chain with nearest-neighbor (J1) and next-nearest-neighbor (J2) interactions, analyzed in regimes ranging from the decoupled limit (J1=0) to highly frustrated regimes.

**Key observables.** Entanglement Gap (EG), spinon weight (W), entanglement spectrum (ES) levels, and ground-state momentum character.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Frustration ratio (alpha = J1/J2), ferromagnetic vs. antiferromagnetic coupling (sign of J1), and the Majumdar-Ghosh point.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the validity of the double-spinon description in the highly frustrated regime and utilizes a bosonic mapping with infinite on-site repulsion to model the spins.

**Figures summary.** Figure 6 shows the peak of spinon weight and entanglement gap at J2/J1 approx 0.16, alongside Haldane-Shastry fidelity; Figure 7 displays the evolution of momentum-space entanglement spectra across different alpha values.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of spinon stability, defines the J1-J2 model and the double-spinon framework, presents numerical results using entanglement spectroscopy to identify the breakdown of fractionalization, and concludes with a Renormalization Group explanation for the observed asymmetry in stability.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the resilience of spinon quasiparticles in the $J_1$-$J_2$ zig-zag spin chain ($J_2>0$) from the viewpoint of momentum-space entanglement. For small $J_2$, we show that deconfined spinons survive well past the liquid-dimer transition before eventually collapsing towards the Majumdar-Ghosh point. In the highly frustrated zig-zag regime ($J_2 \gg |J_1|$), we model the system as two coupled Heisenberg chains and by Fourier transforming each subchain individually, a framework we dub the double-spinon description. While continuum field theories predict that this decoupled phase is strictly unstable to any finite inter-chain coupling, our analysis reveals that the double-spinon description remains robust over an extensive parameter regime. Notably, we find a stark asymmetry in spinon stability reflecting the underlying renormalization group flow: ferromagnetic coupling ($J_{1} < 0$) is marginally irrelevant and sustains fractionalization deep into the spiral phase, whereas antiferromagnetic coupling ($J_{1} > 0$) is marginally relevant and drives confinement much earlier. The ultimate breakdown of this fractionalized description is driven by a continuum of inter-chain excitations which manifests itself as a sharp ground-state momentum shift distinct from macroscopic thermodynamic phase boundaries. Our results establish momentum cut entanglement analysis as a tool to trace the quasiparticle resilience of spinons, as we show that treating the zig-zag Heisenberg chain as two coupled SU(2)$_1$ Wess-Zumino-Witten models provides a theoretical framework for strongly frustrated quantum magnets applicable beyond the decoupled limit.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27381"></a>
### [Nonadiabatic Renormalization Group for Strongly Coupled Multiscale Quantum Systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27381v1)

**Authors:** Bing Gu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** numerical methods · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27381v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27381_figures/2604.27381_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 2. Schematic of NARG as a nested fiber bundle whereby the fiber of each layer is itself a fiber bundle.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27381_figures/2604.27381_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 1. Schematic of the leg-tied tensor ansatz (LETTA) in comparison with the conventional matrix product states (MPS). Physical legs can be shared between (a) nearest neigh- bours and (b) next-nearest-neighbours. (d) A LETTA with peridic boundary condition.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27381_figures/2604.27381_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> FIG. 3. Low-lying eigenstates of an strongly interacting bo- son model with 20 modes. The lowerst 16 eigenvalues are shown, with a rapid convergence with respect to the number of retained adiabatic states D.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27381_figures/2604.27381_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> FIG. 4. NARG for ab initio quantum chemistry. Electronic correlation is gradually incorporated into the NARG by in- creasing the retained adiabatic states. More than 80% of the correlation energy in the hydrogen chain H8 is captured by D = 200.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** The paper proposes a new renormalization group method called NARG to treat quantum systems with hierarchical energy scales. It introduces a new tensor network, LETTA, which shares physical legs between sites to better encode nonadiabatic entanglement. The method proves highly effective for both interacting boson models and molecular electronic correlation problems.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a novel tensor network architecture (LETTA) specifically designed for multiscale dynamics, offering a way to handle nonadiabaticity and strong coupling that standard MPS-based methods struggle to capture.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Efficiently modeling complex, multiscale quantum systems where different energy or time scales are strongly coupled, particularly when standard 'tracing out' or product basis approaches fail in the strong-coupling regime.

**Main result.** The introduction of the Nonadiabatic Renormalization Group (NARG) and the Leg-Tied Tensor Ansatz (LETTA), a new tensor network state that captures entanglement beyond conventional MPS by sharing physical legs among sites.

**Method.** An iterative approach that suppresses high-energy degrees of freedom to construct a nested fiber bundle structure, resulting in a tensor network (LETTA) where physical legs are shared across scales.

**Model / system.** The method is demonstrated on an interacting boson model with strong anharmonicity and ab initio quantum chemistry problems, specifically the hydrogen chain (H8) for electronic correlation.

**Key observables.** Low-lying many-body eigenstates, correlation energy, global overlap matrix, and the quantum geometric tensor (Riemann metric and Berry curvature).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Energy scales (Lambda vs. mu), bond dimension (number of retained adiabatic states D), and coupling strengths (anharmonicity and mode-coupling).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method assumes a hierarchy of energy scales and relies on the truncation of adiabatic eigenstates (coarse-graining) to manage the Hilbert space.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares MPS to the new LETTA architecture; Figure 2 illustrates the NARG as a nested fiber bundle; Figure 3 shows eigenvalue convergence in a boson model; Figure 4 compares ground state energy accuracy against HF and FCI methods.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by identifying the failure of standard methods in strong coupling, introduces the NARG framework and its geometric fiber bundle structure, derives the LETTA tensor network, and concludes with numerical demonstrations on boson and electronic systems.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Complex quantum systems are often multiscale in nature with strong interactions between different scales. We present a novel idea: iteratively suppressing, rather than tracing out, the fast, high-energy degrees of freedom in strongly correlated quantum systems with multiple energy scales in a non-perturbative way, termed nonadiabatic renormalization group. This leads to a quantum geometric structure of a nested fiber bundle, in which each fiber of a layer is itself a fiber bundle of the next layer. The nonadiabatic renormalization group brings a new type of tensor network states that shares physical legs among ''sites'' and encodes quantum entanglement beyond conventional matrix product states. We demonstrate how to apply the nonadiabatic renormalization group to different types of problems, including an interacting boson model and ab initio quantum chemistry with interacting electrons.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27836"></a>
### [Towards High Performance Quantum Computing (HPQ): Parallelisation of the Hamiltonian Auto Decomposition Optimisation Framework (HADOF)](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27836v1)

**Authors:** Namasi G Sankar, Georgios Miliotis, Simon Caton  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27836v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `QC/QI experiment` **3/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1: Standard QAOA circuit with alternating cost and mixer Hamiltonians[34]. The output produces a probability distribution over the solution space which can be samples with the shots parameter of the quantum simulation or backend. Higher sampled solutions are more likely to be solutions with better objective value.</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27836_figures/2604.27836_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Figure 3: General overview of the HADOF framework. Here, we use QAOA as the optimiser, which is called iteratively. (1) We choose subsets of sizes 5 from the binary variables of the global problem. (2) These are used to form the sub-Hamiltonians using P(xi), approximated as the expected value of each qubit. (3) The QAOA circuit set up with t = 1 layers and in every iteration we add a layer. In this study, we use 5 layers. (4) Once the n/k sub-Hamiltonians are optimised, we sample them and use an aggregation policy to form the global solution probability distribution. This can be done sequentially or in parallel.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27836_figures/2604.27836_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Figure 4: Simulating quantum circuit dynamics is hard classically, however with HADOF, problems up to size 500 variables are simulated in a comparable time to classical Simulated Annealing. Time advantage can be observed in HADOF simulation when running in parallel.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27836_figures/2604.27836_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> Fig. 4 and Fig. 5 show the wall clock time to solution for the different algorithms. In the ideal simulation setting (Fig. 4), both sequential and parallel HADOF exhibit approximately linear scaling with respect to the number of binary variables. However, the parallel implementation consistently achieves lower wall clock times, with the performance gap widening as the problem size increases. For instance, at 500 variables, parallel HADOF achieves roughly a 3× speedup over the sequential variant. This indicates that parallelisation not only reduces constant overheads but also improves the effective scaling coefficient, suggesting strong suitability for large-scale QUBO instances. In...</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27836_figures/2604.27836_fig6.jpg" alt="Figure 6">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 6.</b> Figure 5: Note up to 5 times faster simulation speed when run with noise. On a real device, additional to run time, queueing time is significant. Submitting and running jobs in parallel saves both QPU usage and wall clock time.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27836_figures/2604.27836_fig7.jpg" alt="Figure 7">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 7.</b> Fig. 7 reveals a more distinct separation between methods when considering the average accuracy across all sampled solutions. While ideal simulations still maintain high performance, the average accuracy is consistently lower than the most probable solution accuracy, indicating that suboptimal solutions contribute non-negligibly to the sampled distribution. Under noisy simulation, the degradation in average accuracy is more significant than in the most probable case, highlighting the sensitivity of the overall solution distribution to noise. Sequential implementations tend to retain</figcaption>

</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27836_figures/2604.27836_fig9.jpg" alt="Figure 9">

</div>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a parallelized version of the HADOF framework to enable High Performance Quantum (HPQ) computing. By decomposing large optimization problems into smaller sub-problems that can be executed concurrently across multiple quantum processors, the method significantly reduces computation time without sacrificing solution quality.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The scalability of quantum optimization algorithms like QAOA is limited by the low qubit counts, hardware noise, and connectivity constraints of NISQ-era devices when tackling large-scale combinatorial problems.

**Main result.** The parallelized HADOF framework achieves up to a 3-4x reduction in wall-clock time using multiple IBM QPUs and a 3x speedup on a single QPU, while maintaining competitive solution accuracy for genome assembly tasks.

**Method.** The researchers extend the Hamiltonian Auto Decomposition Optimisation Framework (HADOF) by implementing inter-QPU and intra-QPU parallelization using joblib to solve decomposed sub-Hamiltonians independently.

**Model / system.** The study uses Quadratic Unconstrained Binary Optimization (QUBO) models applied to genome assembly (TSP-like problems) and benchmarks them on IBM Quantum processors (e.g., IBM_Kingston, IBM_Fez) and classical PennyLane simulations.

**Key observables.** Wall clock time (makespan), QPU usage time, solution accuracy (approximation ratio), and probability distribution of sampled solutions.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Sub-Hamiltonian size (k=5), circuit depth (5 layers), and problem size (n up to 500 for simulation, up to 50 for real hardware).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The framework assumes that large global Hamiltonians can be effectively approximated by replacing inactive variables with their expected values from previous iterations.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a standard QAOA circuit; Figure 2 illustrates Trotterized decomposition; Figure 3 provides an overview of the HADOF framework; Figure 8 and 9 show the DAG overlap graph and the assembled genome sequence.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the scalability bottleneck, presents the HADOF decomposition and its parallelized extension, details the experimental setup on IBM hardware, reports performance benchmarks across different execution modes, and validates the approach with a genome assembly case study.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Practical applicability of quantum optimisation on near term devices is constrained by limited qubit counts and hardware noise, which restricts the scalability of quantum optimisation algorithms for combinatorial problems. The simulation of large quantum circuits is also difficult and constrained by memory requirement. The Hamiltonian Auto Decomposition Optimisation Framework (HADOF) addresses this by decomposing large QUBOs into smaller subproblems that can be solved iteratively on quantum or classical backends. This allows the scalability of quantum QUBO algorithms beyond device limits, as well as their simulation on classical devices. In this research, we extend the evaluation of HADOF by benchmarking on real IBM QPUs across sequential, single-QPU parallel, and multi-QPU parallel execution modes, advancing toward High Performance Quantum (HPQ) computing for combinatorial optimisation problems.   Experimental results on IBM quantum hardware demonstrate up to 3-4x reduction in wall clock time when utilising four QPUs compared to sequential execution baseline, while maintaining comparable solution quality. Notably, even single QPU execution benefits from parallelised job orchestration and execution, yielding up to 3x speedup. Simulated results predict over 5x speed-up in parallel execution mode. We further validate the practical applicability of the approach on real world genome assembly instances, showing that both sequential and parallel HADOF variants achieve competitive accuracy while significantly improving time to solution. These results highlight the importance of parallelism at both the algorithmic and system levels, positioning HADOF as a viable pathway toward scalable quantum optimisation.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27489"></a>
### [Anomalous tunneling as a low-energy theorem for Nambu-Goldstone modes](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27489v1)

**Authors:** Keisuke Fujii, Daichi Kagamihara, Masaru Hongo  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum gases · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27489v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27489_figures/2604.27489_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Schematic illustration of our result. At low en- ergies, the problem reduces to scattering of the NG mode in an effective potential Veff(x). Under scaling, symmetry- preserving potentials shrink, leading to perfect transmission, whereas symmetry-breaking ones grow, leading to perfect re- flection.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27489_figures/2604.27489_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2. Transmission coefficient T in Eq. (24) as a function of kia for several values of γ. The momentum inside the barrier is related to the incident momentum by ki = (co/ci)ko.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper establishes that the phenomenon of anomalous tunneling in broken-symmetry systems is a universal consequence of symmetry and scaling. It demonstrates that while symmetry-preserving defects become invisible at low energies, symmetry-breaking defects effectively block transmission. This result provides a unified theoretical rule for the behavior of Nambu-Goldstone modes like phonons and magnons.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a universal, symmetry-based principle for transport phenomena in many-body systems, which is highly relevant for understanding how excitations move through defects in BECs and other quantum fluids.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper seeks to establish the general conditions under which 'anomalous tunneling'—where the transmission coefficient approaches unity as energy tends to zero—occurs in systems with spontaneous symmetry breaking.

**Main result.** The authors prove that anomalous tunneling is a universal low-energy theorem where symmetry-preserving potentials are irrelevant and lead to perfect transmission, whereas symmetry-breaking perturbations are relevant and suppress transmission.

**Method.** The study uses a low-energy effective field theory (EFT) framework combined with scaling analysis and dimensional power counting to evaluate the impact of localized potentials.

**Model / system.** The framework applies to Nambu-Goldstone modes (Type-A and Type-B) in systems with spontaneous symmetry breaking, specifically illustrating the effect with superfluid phonons and magnons in ferromagnets.

**Key observables.** Transmission coefficient (T), reflection coefficient (R), and energy flux (Q).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Low-energy/low-momentum limit (k -> 0), symmetry-preserving vs. symmetry-breaking perturbations, and the scaling dimension of localized potentials.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis for Type-B modes neglects subleading omega^2 g_t(x) terms to focus on the low-energy limit.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 provides a schematic of the scaling analysis showing how potentials shrink or grow under scaling; Figure 2 shows the transmission coefficient approaching 1 as the momentum-width product (kia) approaches 0.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the concept of anomalous tunneling, establishes a general EFT framework for Nambu-Goldstone modes, performs scaling and power-counting analysis to distinguish perturbation types, and provides explicit examples using phonons and magnons.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Anomalous tunneling refers to the phenomenon in which the transmission coefficient through a potential barrier approaches unity as the energy of an incident particle or quasiparticle tends to zero. This counterintuitive effect has been reported in systems exhibiting spontaneous symmetry breaking (SSB), such as superfluids, yet the general conditions for its occurrence remain unclear. In this Letter, we establish that anomalous tunneling of Nambu-Goldstone (NG) modes is a universal low-energy theorem dictated solely by symmetry and scaling, using a low-energy effective field theory (EFT) framework. We formulate the scattering of NG modes by external potentials in terms of spatially dependent EFT coefficients and demonstrate that symmetry-preserving localized potentials are irrelevant in the long-wavelength limit, leading to perfect transmission. In contrast, symmetry-breaking perturbations are relevant and suppress transmission, resulting in the absence of anomalous tunneling. We illustrate this universal behavior with explicit examples of superfluid phonons and magnons.

</details>

### [Hyperfine-resolved laser excitation and detection of nuclear isomer in trapped $^{229}$Th$^{3+}$ ions](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27614v1)

**Summary.** This paper presents a theoretical framework for the laser excitation and detection of the 229Th nuclear isomer in trapped ions. It proposes specific electronic fluorescence channels to improve detection efficiency and demonstrates that the nuclear transition can be found relatively quickly using current VUV technology. This work is a significant step toward the realization of practical nuclear clocks.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides critical theoretical guidance for the development of nuclear clocks and explores the control of hyperfine-resolved dynamics in a system coupling nuclear and electronic degrees of freedom.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Achieving efficient laser excitation and detection of the low-energy nuclear isomeric state of 229Th in trapped-ion systems, specifically overcoming low fluorescence signals and large frequency uncertainties.

**Main result.** The study identifies that matching laser linewidth, detuning, and irradiation time is essential for excitation and proposes that a 1088-nm detection scheme provides the highest photon rate, enabling the nuclear transition to be located within one month.

**Method.** A quantum master equation approach in the Lindblad form is used to analyze hyperfine-resolved excitation dynamics and population transfer efficiency.

**Model / system.** The system consists of 229Th3+ ions in a trap, modeling the nuclear transition between ground and isomeric states via an M1 transition and electronic transitions via E1 transitions using a hyperfine-coupled basis.

**Key observables.** Isomeric population, electronic fluorescence photon rates (690, 984, and 1088 nm), and total search time for the nuclear transition frequency.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Nuclear Rabi frequency (~3.4 Hz), laser linewidths (100 Hz to 10 MHz), nuclear isomeric lifetime (~2500 s), and transition energy (~8.4 eV).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The nuclear isomer decay is neglected for short time scales (up to 500 s) to treat the system as a two-level model, and the rotating-wave approximation is applied.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows nuclear and electronic energy levels and proposed fluorescence wavelengths; Figure 2 displays hyperfine structures; Figure 10 shows electronic population as a function of laser detuning.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the scientific problem, describes the physical system and hyperfine model, details the quantum master equation methodology, presents results for excitation and detection schemes, and concludes with an optimization of the nuclear transition search time.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present a comprehensive theoretical investigation of hyperfine-resolved excitation and detection of the low-energy isomeric state of $^{229}$Th in trapped $^{229}\mathrm{Th}^{3+}$ ions. Using a quantum master equation approach, we analyze the dependence of the isomeric population on laser linewidth, detuning, and irradiation time, showing that their proper matching is essential for efficient excitation. We further propose two nuclear-state detection schemes based on three hyperfine-resolved electronic fluorescence channels at 690, 984, and 1088 nm. Our analysis shows that the 690-nm and 984-nm scheme yields detectable photon rates on the order of $10^4~\mathrm{s}^{-1}$ per ion for each wavelength, whereas the 1088-nm scheme achieves a higher rate on the order of $10^5~\mathrm{s}^{-1}$ per ion. By quantifying the trade-off between irradiation time and scan-step size, we show that the nuclear transition can be located within one month for a 100-MHz uncertainty using currently available vacuum-ultraviolet laser technology. These results provide practical guidance for trapped-ion $^{229}\mathrm{Th}$ spectroscopy and the development of nuclear clocks.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27834"></a>
### [Hypergeometric Functions of Nilpotent Operators: Functional Collapse and Structural Depth at Exceptional Points](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27834v1)

**Authors:** Ramon Moya  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27834v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 8.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 9</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27834_figures/2604.27834_page10.jpg" alt="Figure 9">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 9.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 10</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27834_figures/2604.27834_page11.jpg" alt="Figure 10">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 10.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 11</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper proves that applying analytic functions to nilpotent operators causes a 'functional collapse' that reduces the complexity of their Jordan structure. It provides a specific criterion to calculate how much the Jordan depth of an exceptional point decreases based on the function's contact order. This has direct implications for understanding the stability and spectral properties of non-Hermitian systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for researchers in non-Hermitian quantum mechanics and open quantum systems, as it provides a precise mathematical tool to predict how physical transformations (like time evolution or spectral filtering) can simplify or destroy the complex algebraic structure of exceptional points.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates how analytic functional transformations (like hypergeometric functions) affect the Jordan structure and nilpotency index of operators at exceptional points.

**Main result.** The author establishes a 'nilpotent depth criterion' showing that a function with contact order r reduces the Jordan depth of an exceptional point from m+1 to at most ceil((m+1)/r).

**Method.** The study uses functional calculus of nilpotent operators, Taylor expansions, and algebraic analysis within C-associative algebras.

**Model / system.** The mathematical model focuses on non-Hermitian Hamiltonians at exceptional points, represented by H = lambda I + N, where N is a nilpotent operator.

**Key observables.** Jordan depth (nilpotency index), trace of hypergeometric functions, and the order of the pole in the modified resolvent.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Nilpotency index (m+1), contact order (r) of the function at the eigenvalue, and the parameter-termination vs. nilpotent-argument termination mechanisms.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The operators are assumed to be finite-dimensional and nilpotent; functions are assumed to be analytic at the eigenvalue.

**Figures summary.** The paper includes explicit 3x3 Jordan block computations for various functions (exponential, Kummer, Gauss hypergeometric) to validate the theoretical bounds.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining the functional collapse of hypergeometric functions, introduces the nilpotent depth criterion (Theorem 2), applies this to Hamiltonians at exceptional points (Theorem 3), and concludes with explicit 3x3 matrix examples and consequences for the resolvent.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study hypergeometric functions of nilpotent operators in finite-dimensional settings, motivated by the algebraic structure of exceptional points in non-Hermitian quantum mechanics. Our starting point is the following exact result: if N is a nilpotent operator of index m+1 in an associative algebra over C, then every generalized hypergeometric function pFq evaluated at N reduces to a finite polynomial in N of degree at most m, without any analytic convergence requirement. This "functional collapse" is distinct from the classical parameter-termination mechanism and arises purely from the nilpotent structure of the argument. The main result is a "nilpotent depth criterion" (Theorem 2): if the first non-constant coefficient of a formal series F appears in degree r >= 1, then the nilpotent part F(N) - F(0)I has nilpotency index bounded above by ceil((m+1)/r). We apply this criterion to Hamiltonians at exceptional points, where H = lambda I + N with N^{m+1} = 0. Theorem 3 establishes that a function F analytic at lambda reduces the Jordan depth of the exceptional point from m+1 to at most ceil((m+1)/r), where r is the contact order of F at lambda. As consequences: the time evolution operator e^{tH} preserves the full Jordan depth for all t != 0; a function with a zero of order m+1 at lambda annihilates the entire Jordan structure; and the order of the pole of the modified resolvent is reduced from m+1 to at most m+1-r. Results are illustrated with explicit 3x3 Jordan block computations for 1F1, 2F1, and the time evolution operator, confirming sharpness of the bounds.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27885"></a>
### [Quantum integrable matrix models of spinor Bose gases in one spatial dimension](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27885v1)

**Authors:** Hannes Köper, Thomas Gasenzer  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum gases · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27885v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **2/5**

**Summary.** This paper develops a mathematical framework using matrix-valued bosonic fields to describe 1D spinor Bose gases with arbitrary spin. It successfully maps the spin-1 case to a set of solvable integral equations, allowing for the precise calculation of the ground state phase diagram. The work highlights how interactions can lead to bound states that change the effective exclusion principle for the particles.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a powerful integrable framework for studying complex spin multiplets in 1D, offering new insights into how bound states can alter the fundamental statistics (Pauli exclusion principle) of interacting bosons in cold atom systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to generalize the description of one-dimensional spinor Bose gases to arbitrary spin multiplets using a matrix-based integrable model.

**Main result.** The authors derive a quantum integrable matrix extension of the nonlinear Schrödinger model and compute the ground state phase diagram for a spin-1 Bose gas, revealing that paired bound states modify the effective Pauli exclusion principle for bosons.

**Method.** The study employs algebraic Bethe-ansatz techniques to construct eigenstates and derive Bethe equations, alongside the derivation of integral equations for thermodynamic properties.

**Model / system.** A quantum integrable matrix extension of the nonlinear Schrödinger model where fundamental fields are represented by an m x n matrix of bosonic field operators, specifically applied to spin-1 Bose gases with repulsive density-density and anti-ferromagnetic spin-spin interactions.

**Key observables.** Ground state phase diagram, spectra of conserved quantities, and equilibrium thermodynamic properties.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Chemical potential, external magnetic field, and the Lieb parameter (gamma).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is restricted to one spatial dimension with repulsive density-density interaction and anti-ferromagnetic spin-spin coupling.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces a matrix extension of the nonlinear Schrödinger model, applies the algebraic Bethe-ansatz to find eigenstates and Bethe equations, focuses on the spin-1 case to derive thermodynamic integral equations, and concludes with a phase diagram analysis and discussion of bound state effects.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Degenerate spinor Bose gases with repulsive density-density interaction and anti-ferromagnetic spin-spin coupling in one spatial dimension are shown to be described by a quantum integrable matrix extension of the nonlinear Schrödinger model, whose fundamental fields are described by an $m\,\times\,n$ matrix of bosonic field operators. The eigenstates of this model are constructed for arbitrarily sized matrix field operators by means of algebraic Bethe-ansatz techniques, and the corresponding Bethe equations governing the spectra of conserved quantities are derived. The approach thus generalizes previously chosen techniques to account for arbitrary spin multiplets and their spin-spin interaction. Focusing on the specific case of the $2\times2$ model, which is shown to correspond to a spin-$1$ Bose gas, a set of integral equations is derived, which describe its equilibrium thermodynamic properties. From these, the ground state phase diagram is computed both, numerically and analytically in the parameter plane spanned by the chemical potential and an external magnetic field. Furthermore, the existence of paired bound states is shown to modify the Pauli exclusion principle for interacting bosons in one dimension. In particular, it is found that no two quasiparticle rapidities can coincide, provided that the Lieb parameter satisfies $γ>4/3$.

</details>

### [Reorganizing Quantum Measurement Records Improves Time-Series Prediction](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28160v1)

**Authors:** Markus Baumann, Maximilian Zorn, Thomas Gabor, Claudia Linnhoff-Popien, Jonas Stein

**Summary.** The paper introduces a simple algorithmic technique called split-ensemble training to reorganize quantum measurement shots. By grouping shots into intermediate averages, it balances the trade-off between measurement noise and training sample size, significantly improving the accuracy of quantum-classical hybrid time-series prediction on both simulators and real hardware.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** How to optimize the use of finite measurement records in Quantum Reservoir Computing (QRC) to improve time-series prediction without increasing the quantum hardware execution budget.

**Main result.** The proposed 'split-ensemble' training method, which reorganizes shots into partially denoised groups, consistently outperforms standard expectation-value and raw-stacking approaches, especially on noisy real hardware.

**Method.** A split-ensemble training protocol that partitions $N$ shots into $G$ disjoint groups, each averaged to create a distinct, partially denoised feature vector for a classical linear readout.

**Model / system.** A Quantum Reservoir Computing framework using a fixed $Q$-qubit circuit (e.g., ring-CNOT entangler) with repeated executions, combined with a classical leaky integrator and a linear ridge regression readout.

**Key observables.** Single-qubit $Z_i$ and $X_i$ observables derived from measurement bitstrings.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Total execution budget ($S_{total}$), group size ($k$), training-support ratio ($ho_{EV}$), reservoir size ($Q$), and leakage rate ($\eta$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The total number of quantum circuit executions is a fixed resource; the classical readout is a linear model.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the three data organization strategies; Figure 2 shows NRMSE results for various time-series tasks; Figure 3-5 show performance across reservoir sizes and architectures; Figure 6 compares split-ensemble to EV-dup; Figure 7-8 show hardware validation on IBM hardware.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of shot-noise vs. data scarcity, defines the three data organization protocols, provides a theoretical framework for the split-ensemble advantage, presents numerical benchmarks on various time-series tasks, performs ablation studies to rule out simple data augmentation, and validates the method on real superconducting quantum hardware.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Near-term quantum computers are accessed through repeated circuit executions, which produce finite measurement records rather than exact deterministic outputs. In quantum reservoir computing, these records are converted to feature vectors for a classical readout. The standard expectation-value approach averages all shots from one labeled time step into a single feature vector. This reduces finite-shot noise, but it also gives the readout only one training example from many circuit executions. We introduce split-ensemble training: the same shots are split into groups, and each group average is used as a separate, partially denoised feature vector for the same target. The quantum circuit, task, and measurement budget remain unchanged. Across simulated forecasting benchmarks and real hardware experiments, this simple reorganization improves prediction when full averaging leaves the readout with too few training examples, with the strongest gains observed on hardware. Our results establish shot-record organization as a simple, broadly applicable algorithmic lever for improving near-term quantum learning without additional quantum hardware cost.

</details>

### [Quantum Anonymous Secret Sharing with Permutation Invariant Codes](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27284v1)

**Authors:** Varin Sikand, Andrew Nemec  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27284v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27284_figures/2604.27284_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Fig. 1: Overview of ANONQ(|ϕ⟩) (Protocol II.4) with Alice encircled in green and Bob in blue. Each participant holds one qubit of the n-qubit GHZ state, denoted as σi, and all participants besides Alice and Bob apply an H gate to their qubit, leaving only Alice and Bob with an entangled pair. The green arrow represents the teleportation step after the AE protocol.</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27284_figures/2604.27284_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Fig. 2: Overview of the QASS protocol. We initially assume the secret has been encoded into n shares. In Step 1, each green arrow denotes a share sent from each k participating shareholders via the ANONQ protocol. Once received by Bob, the identities of each share is entirely lost due to ANONQ. Finally, Bob decodes the anonymous set of retrieved shares using the PI code’s decoding circuit to obtain the secret.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27284_figures/2604.27284_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">

PI code [46] and the ((4, 2, 2)) Hagiwara and Nakayama PI code [13], the ramp QSS scheme using the [[4, 1, 2]] Leung et al. stabilizer code [44], and the HQASS scheme using Aydin et al. PI code [46] for both quantum and classical secrets. All three 4-qubit QSS schemes have identical Hmin values, whereas the HQASS scheme has a threshold of k = 3 with no intermediate share sets.</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27284_figures/2604.27284_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Fig. 4: Comparison of Hmin across various PI codes of distance 3. (a) The ((7, 2, 3)) codes of Pollatsek and Ruskai [47], [49] and Aydin et al. (c) Three ((11, 2, 3)) codes including the one given by Kubischta and Teixeira [49], the shifted gnu (3, 3, 1)-PI code of Ouyang [45], and the Q4,1,2,−1 code of Aydin et al. [46]. (d) Two ((13, 2, 3)) codes including one given by Kubischta and Teixeira [49] and the shifted gnu (3, 3, 1)-PI code of Ouyang [45].</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a new quantum secret sharing protocol that ensures the identities of the senders remain anonymous during the decoding process. By using permutation-invariant codes, the authors also provide a new mathematical way to precisely measure how much information an adversary can steal from partial shares.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Existing Quantum Secret Sharing (QSS) schemes fail to protect the identities of senders during decoding and lack a robust single-shot metric to quantify information leakage in ramp-type schemes.

**Main result.** The authors construct a Quantum Anonymous Secret Sharing (QASS) protocol using permutation-invariant codes that achieves sender-anonymity and can tolerate missing shareholders. They also establish quantum conditional min-entropy as a valid single-shot measure for quantifying information leakage.

**Method.** The study utilizes permutation-invariant (PI) quantum error-correcting codes combined with anonymous quantum transmission algorithms (ANONQ). They use semidefinite programming (SDP) and the Knill-Laflamme conditions to evaluate information leakage and fidelity.

**Model / system.** The protocol operates on n-qubit systems using permutation-invariant codes and GHZ states, modeled under quantum erasure and depolarizing channels.

**Key observables.** Sender-anonymity, quantum conditional min-entropy (Hmin), maximum fidelity (F), and quantum correlation (q_corr).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Code distance (d), threshold (k), number of physical qubits (n), and number of participating shareholders (np).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The protocol assumes all shareholders and the receiver are honest; it also assumes the use of permutation-invariant codes and relies on GHZ-based anonymous transmission.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the ANONQ protocol steps; Figure 2 shows the QASS protocol flow; Figure 3 compares Hmin decay across different PI codes.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of anonymity and leakage, describes the construction of the QASS protocol using PI codes, provides a theoretical justification for the min-entropy metric via Knill-Laflamme conditions, and concludes with numerical evaluations of various stabilizer codes.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum secret sharing schemes are a family of quantum cryptographic protocols which provide secure quantum encodings, mapping one secret to multiple shares of information such that the original secret cannot be accessed without an authorized set of shares present for decoding. In this work, we describe a protocol that enables sender-anonymity during the secret decoding process. By using permutation-invariant QEC codes along with a set of anonymous quantum transmission algorithms, we construct a quantum anonymous secret sharing scheme that achieves sender-anonymity. We quantify information leakage in ramp quantum secret sharing schemes via the quantum conditional min-entropy, justifying it as a valid measure of leaked information by relating it to the Knill-Laflamme quantum error correction conditions. Finally, we evaluate several permutation-invariant codes using this measure to make observations on the information leakage of intermediate shares for each quantum anonymous secret sharing scheme.

</details>

### [Wavelet-based multiresolution analysis of quantum fractals in confined dynamics](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27851v1)

**Summary.** The paper introduces a wavelet-based multiresolution framework to quantify the fractal dimensions of quantum carpets in confined dynamics. This method avoids the biases of traditional geometric or Fourier methods and provides a robust way to characterize space, time, and space-time fractality. It successfully recovers predicted universal fractal dimensions even in numerically limited, pre-fractal regimes.

**Why it may be interesting.** This provides a new, computationally efficient diagnostic tool for analyzing multiscale features and interference-driven dynamics in any system exhibiting complex, self-similar patterns, such as wave propagation in confined geometries or anomalous transport.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The difficulty in quantitatively and robustly characterizing quantum fractals (self-similar structures in quantum states) due to the limitations of traditional Fourier-based or geometric scaling methods which are sensitive to finite-size effects and numerical truncation.

**Main result.** The development of a wavelet-based multiresolultion analysis (MRA) framework that provides a robust, scale-adaptive, and hypothesis-free method to extract fractal dimensions for space, time, and space-time quantum fractals.

**Method.** Wavelet-based Multiresolution Analysis (MRA) using discrete wavelet transforms (Haar and Daubechies families) to analyze the scale-dependent distribution of wavelet energies and Bohmian trajectories to probe space-time correlations.

**Model / system.** A quantum particle confined within a one-dimensional infinite potential well, starting from initial states with spatial discontinuities (e.g., square wave functions).

**Key observables.** Probability density, probability flux, Bohmian trajectories, and fractal dimensions (spatial, temporal, and space-time).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Dyadic scales (j), spectral truncation (N), recurrence time (T), and wavelet family type.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is subject to a 'pre-fractal' regime due to finite spectral resolution/truncation, and the scaling exponent is extracted via linear regression within an intermediate scaling window.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows space-time quantum carpets with superimposed Bohmian trajectories; Figure 2 shows spatial profiles and fractal dimension estimates; Figure 3 shows temporal profiles and fractal dimension estimates; Figure 4 shows the convergence of fractal dimensions as a function of spectral truncation.

**Paper structure.** The paper identifies limitations in existing fractal characterization methods, introduces the wavelet MRA framework, applies it to confined 1D dynamics, validates the results against Berry's predicted universal dimensions, and demonstrates the method's robustness and efficiency.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Fractal structures naturally emerge in quantum systems whose initial states exhibit spatial discontinuities, a phenomenon first identified by Berry in the paradigmatic case of a particle confined in an infinite potential well. While previous analyses of quantum fractals have mainly relied on spectral decompositions and geometric scaling arguments, their quantitative characterization often depends on scale choices and truncation effects. Here we present a wavelet-based multiresolution framework that enables a direct and assumption-free quantification of quantum fractality. Fractal dimensions are extracted from the scale-dependent distribution of wavelet energies, without invoking prior power-law hypotheses. The method is applied to space and time quantum fractals arising in confined dynamics, as well as to dynamical curves generated by the associated quantum probability flux. These flux-driven trajectories provide a natural space--time parametrization of the underlying fractal structure and yield scaling properties fully consistent with Berry's predictions for space--time fractals. The resulting fractal dimensions are shown to be robust with respect to the choice of wavelet family, numerical cutoffs, and system parameters. Beyond validating earlier conjectures, the present framework offers a unified and computationally efficient tool for the multiscale analysis of quantum fractality in confined and interference-driven quantum dynamics. That is, it provides an operational, scale-adaptive criterion that unifies the characterization of space, time, and space--time quantum fractals within a single, hypothesis-free approach.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.28050"></a>
### [A No-Cloning Trade-off Between Black Hole No-Hair and Horizon Smoothness](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28050v1)

**Authors:** Sudhanva Joshi, Sunil Kumar Mishra  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.28050v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.28050_figures/2604.28050_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1: Penrose diagram of a black hole formed by gravitational collapse. The spacetime is divided into an interior region I (above the dashed horizon line H +) and an exterior region E (below). An infalling quantum system F in state |ψ⟩crosses the horizon, with its state embedded in I via the isometric embedding V . The reduced states ρI(ψ) and ρE(ψ) encode the information accessible to interior and exterior observers, respectively. The trade-off inequality Eq. (16) constrains the relationship between horizon smoothness (ε) and exterior distinguishability (Dmax).</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper establishes a fundamental mathematical trade-off between the ability to distinguish black hole microstates from the outside and the smoothness of the event horizon. It proves that observable quantum hair is quantitatively incompatible with a smooth horizon, meaning any 'hair' requires a violation of the equivalence principle. This provides a rigorous information-theoretic constraint on various black hole microstate models like fuzzballs and soft hair.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the fundamental compatibility between the existence of observable quantum hair (exterior distinguishability) and the smoothness of the black hole event horizon (equivalence principle validity) under unitary evolution.

**Main result.** The authors derive a quantitative trade-off inequality, epsilon >= D_max^2/8, proving that any model predicting non-zero exterior quantum hair must necessarily involve a violation of horizon smoothness.

**Method.** The study employs quantum information-theoretic tools, specifically the Stinespring dilation of quantum channels, the diamond norm, and the Kretschmann-Schlingemann-Werner (KSW) continuity theorem.

**Model / system.** A semiclassical black hole model where the spacetime is partitioned into interior and exterior sectors, treating the horizon crossing as a quantum channel acting on an infalling system.

**Key observables.** epsilon (horizon smoothness violation/deviation from isometry), D_max (maximum exterior distinguishability/trace distance), and F_I (interior fidelity).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The Bekenstein-Hawking entropy (S_BH) scale, the regime of perfect isometry (epsilon = 0), and the firewall/fuzzball regimes (epsilon ~ O(1)).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Global unitarity, horizon causality (no signaling from interior to exterior), and diamond-norm interior accessibility.

**Figures summary.** A Penrose diagram illustrating the black hole interior and exterior regions, showing an infalling system crossing the horizon and the encoding of states.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by establishing an exact no-hair lemma for perfect isometries, then uses the KSW theorem to extend this to an approximate theorem, ultimately deriving the quadratic trade-off inequality and discussing implications for fuzzballs and soft hair.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The black hole no-hair theorem is traditionally derived from the uniqueness theorems of general relativity. We show that a quantitative form follows from unitarity together with the standard semiclassical assumptions of horizon causality and interior accessibility. For a semiclassical black hole, we prove that the trace distance between exterior states corresponding to two same-charge infalling states is bounded by $2\sqrt{2\varepsilon}$, where $\varepsilon$ quantifies the diamond norm departure of the interior channel from a perfect isometry which is a quantitative measure of horizon-smoothness violation that upper-bounds $1 - F_I$, where $F_I$ is the interior fidelity capturing how faithfully the infalling state is retained. Inverting this relation yields a trade-off inequality, $\varepsilon \geq D_{\max}^2/8$, between the maximum exterior distinguishability $D_{\max}$ and the degree of horizon smoothness. This establishes that observable exterior quantum hair is quantitatively incompatible with exact horizon smoothness under unitary evolution: any model predicting nonzero exterior hair must violate the equivalence principle at the horizon by a quantifiable amount. Pre-existing entanglement with the infalling system is the only channel for quantum hair compatible with both unitarity and horizon smoothness.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27363"></a>
### [Constructing Bulk Topological Orders via Layered Gauging](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27363v1)

**Authors:** Shang Liu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27363v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. The layered gauging procedure. (a) Sequentially gauge a bilayer symmetry acting on every neighboring pair of layers (n, n+1). From n = 1 to n = 2, n = 3, and so on. This is the main approach taken in this paper. (b) Alternatively, we may take an even-odd gauging pattern: First gauge the bilayer symmetries acting on layer pairs (2l −1, 2l), and then gauge those acting on layer pairs (2l, 2l + 1).</figcaption>
