Panels (b) and (c) are equivalent if the anyon line N satisfies N × ¯ N = 1.</figcaption>

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> FIG. 5. 2d bulk stabilizers constructed from the 1d anomalous Z2 symmetry. Each blue bond represents a controlled-Z operator. Stabilizers in (a) are denoted by SE n+1/2,j where (n+1/2, j) locates the center of the four Pauli-X. Stabilizers in (b) are denoted by SM n,j+1/2 where (n, j + 1/2) locates the center of the four Pauli-Z. When stabilizers in (b) are satisfied, (a) can be reduced to the form of (c) which we denote by SER n+1/2,j. Either (a) and (b), or (c) and (b), lead to the double semion topological order.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">

</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** The paper introduces 'layered gauging,' a new method to construct higher-dimensional topological orders by stacking and gauging lower-dimensional layers. It successfully derives well-known models like the X-cube and Toric Code and extends the construction to complex non-invertible symmetries. This provides a powerful tool for exploring the landscape of fracton and liquid topological phases.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a systematic way to build complex many-body phases and explores the deep connection between bulk topological order and boundary symmetry, which is fundamental to understanding many-body dynamics and topological phases.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing a versatile and physically intuitive method to construct (k+1)-dimensional bulk topological orders, including both liquid and fracton types, from k-dimensional generalized symmetries.

**Main result.** The proposed 'layered gauging' construction successfully generates various higher-dimensional topological orders, such as the X-cube model, anisotropic fracton orders, and the 2D double semion model, from lower-dimensional symmetries.

**Method.** The method involves stacking k-dimensional quantum systems into a (k+1)-dimensional pile and sequentially gauging a diagonal or bilayer symmetry acting on adjacent layers.

**Model / system.** The construction uses various building blocks including 1D Z2 Ising chains, 2D Z2 ferromagnets, and the 2D plaquette Ising model, which are stacked and gauged to create 2D and 3D models like the Toric Code and X-cube model.

**Key observables.** Ground state degeneracy, anyon mobility, and boundary symmetry enforcement.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Coupling constants J, h, and lambda; regimes where symmetries are spontaneously broken (e.g., h=0).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method's universality is conjectured but lacks a rigorous mathematical proof; the construction is most effective when the k-dimensional layers spontaneously break their symmetries completely.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the sequential and even-odd layered gauging procedures; Figure 2 shows anyon loops on disk and cylinder geometries; Figure 4 illustrates the construction of the X-cube model; Figure 7/8 show the implementation of non-invertible gauging.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the layered gauging concept, demonstrates its application to conventional and higher-form symmetries (Toric Code), extends it to subsystem symmetries (fracton models), and finally generalizes the procedure to non-abelian and non-invertible symmetries.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Understanding quantum phases and phase transitions in the presence of symmetries is a central objective of quantum many-body physics. A powerful modern paradigm for investigating this problem is topological holography, which relates symmetries in $k$ dimensions to "bulk" topological orders in $(k+1)$ dimensions. While conceptually profound, most existing bulk construction methods rely on sophisticated mathematical formalisms and can be difficult to apply to certain symmetry types. In this work, we propose a physically intuitive and versatile method, termed the layered gauging construction, to systematically generate $(k+1)$-dimensional (liquid or fracton) topological orders from $k$-dimensional generalized symmetries. Roughly speaking, the prescription is to stack many layers of $k$-dimensional quantum systems with certain symmetries into a $(k+1)$-dimensional pile, and then sequentially gauge a diagonal symmetry acting on each nearest-neighbor pair of layers. The detailed procedure depends on the specific symmetry types. We have successfully implemented the method in a number of examples in different spatial dimensions, with symmetries that are conventional, higher-form, subsystem, anomalous, nonabelian, or noninvertible. We hence conjecture the method to be very general. For example, from the subsystem symmetry of the $2d$ plaquette Ising model, we derive the X-cube model and also an anisotropic fracton topological order. Additionally, starting from an anomalous $\mathbb Z_2$ symmetry in $1d$, we construct a new square lattice model realizing the double semion topological order.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27857"></a>
### [Magnetic excitation spectrum and hierarchy of magnetic interactions in ErFeO3](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27857v1)

**Authors:** Dnyaneshwar R. Bhosale, Piotr Fabrykiewicz, Devashibhai Adroja, Martin Meven, Astrid Schneidewind, Michal Stekiel  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27857v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **2/5**

**Summary.** This paper provides a detailed map of the magnetic excitations in ErFeO3 using neutron spectroscopy. It reveals how different magnetic sublattices interact to create a complex hierarchy of energy scales. The findings clarify the roles of exchange coupling and crystal field effects in orthoferrites.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to characterize the magnetic excitation spectrum and the hierarchy of magnetic interactions in the orthoferrite ErFeO3.

**Main result.** The researchers identified two distinct excitation components: dispersive spin waves from the Fe3+ sublattice and crystal electric field (CEF) excitations from Er3+ ions, revealing how exchange coupling and sublattice interactions drive dispersion and level splitting.

**Method.** Time-of-flight neutron spectroscopy was used to measure the excitation spectrum, with data analyzed using linear spin wave theory.

**Model / system.** The system is the ErFeO3 orthoferrite, consisting of interacting Fe3+ and Er3+ sublattices characterized by Fe-Fe exchange, Er-Er exchange, and Er-Fe interactions.

**Key observables.** Spin wave dispersions, spectral weight, and crystal electric field (CEF) excitation energies.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Fe-Fe exchange parameters, energy scales ranging from below 36 meV to 65 meV, and the influence of dipolar and antisymmetric exchange interactions.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The spin wave dispersions and spectral weight are assumed to be well-captured within the framework of linear spin wave theory.

**Paper structure.** The paper presents an investigation of the excitation spectrum, details the identification of spin wave and CEF components, discusses the extraction of exchange parameters via linear spin wave theory, and explores the role of dipolar and antisymmetric exchange in the magnetic ground state.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We report a comprehensive investigation of the excitation spectrum of ErFeO$_3$ orthoferrite by means of time-of-flight neutron spectroscopy. The spectrum consists of two distinct components: strongly dispersive spin wave excitations of the Fe$^{3+}$ sublattice spanning $\approx$~9 - 65 meV, and crystal electric field (CEF) excitations of Er$^{3+}$ ions below 36 meV. The observed spin wave dispersions and spectral weight are well captured within linear spin wave theory, enabling extraction of the key Fe-Fe exchange parameters. Low-energy incident neutrons with their enhanced energy resolution, further revealed the dispersive character and splitting of Kramers-degenerate CEF levels. We show that the dispersion is caused by the exchange coupling between Er$^{3+}$ ions, while the degeneracy is lifted by interactions between the Er$^{3+}$ and Fe$^{3+}$ sublattices. We further explore the influence of dipolar and antisymmetric exchange interactions with the focus on the magnetic ground state of ErFeO$_3$, with particular attention to the low-temperature spin arrangement. Taken together, our results provide a detailed account of the spin dynamics in ErFeO$_3$ and reveal a hierarchy of interactions scales characteristic for orthoferrites.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27965"></a>
### [Propelling catalytic structures using active phase separation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27965v1)

**Authors:** Benjamin Sorkin, Ned S. Wingreen  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27965v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

**Summary.** The paper proposes a mechanism where active phase separation, driven by enzymatic cycles, can propel particles through a medium. This provides a way for biological transport to occur via biomolecular condensates without relying on traditional molecular motors.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how active phase separation can be used to achieve self-propulsion of a colloid without the need for complex molecular motors.

**Main result.** Demonstrated that a passivation-activation cycle of phase-separating proteins can induce symmetry breaking and create a repulsive force that propels colloids at speeds up to 100 microns per second.

**Method.** Numerical simulations of an active droplet system involving phase-separating proteins and enzymatic reactions.

**Model / system.** A spherical colloid in a homogeneous environment containing phase-separating proteins, enzymes that passivate the proteins, and complementary enzymes anchored to the colloid surface that reactivate them.

**Key observables.** Colloid propulsion speed, stability of the condensate, and resistance to Brownian fluctuations.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Micron-sized scale, reaction rates of the passivation-activation cycle, and presence of external forces/Brownian noise.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is autonomous, homogeneous, and isotropic prior to the symmetry-breaking event.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the biological motivation, proposes a minimal mechanism based on enzymatic cycles, presents numerical evidence for propulsion and speed, and discusses the robustness of this motor-free transport mode.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Living systems routinely consume energy to achieve motility, often using intricate biomolecular machinery. In this work, we show that active droplets can sustain indefinite self-propulsion of a spherical colloid in an otherwise homogeneous, isotropic, and autonomous environment. Our proposed minimal mechanism consists of phase-separating proteins, enzymes passivating them, and complementary enzymes anchored to the colloid surface that reactivate the proteins. This passivation-activation cycle gives rise to a symmetry breaking - nucleation and stabilization of a condensate near the colloid surface, which in turn exerts a repulsive force on the colloid. We numerically demonstrate that this mechanism can propel micron-sized colloids at speeds of up to a hundred microns per second. This propulsion mode is strongly resistant to Brownian fluctuations and external forces, suggesting that propulsion mechanisms based on biomolecular condensates may offer a complementary, motor-free route to biological transport.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27873"></a>
### [Theory for the mixed alkali effect in glasses](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27873v1)

**Authors:** Justus Leiber, Quinn Emilia Fischer, Sven Lohmann, Philipp Maass  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27873v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

**Summary.** This paper proposes a theoretical framework to explain why mixing different types of mobile ions in glasses causes strong nonlinear changes in conductivity. The theory demonstrates that the effect is driven by the energy differences between ion types occupying specific sites and is supported by both simulations and experimental data from phosphate glasses.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Explaining the mixed alkali effect in glasses, where the transport properties of mobile ions change nonlinearly upon mixing different ion types.

**Main result.** A theory is developed showing that the mixed alkali effect arises from distinct site energies for different ion types and is enhanced by mismatch energies, even with identical energy distributions.

**Method.** Development of a theory based on thermally activated hopping transport in disordered site energy landscapes, incorporating joint probability density, generalized Fermi distributions, and non-diagonal Onsager coefficients, validated by kinetic Monte Carlo simulations.

**Model / system.** A system of mixed mobile ions undergoing thermally activated hopping transport within disordered site energy landscapes, specifically applied to mixed alkali phosphate glasses.

**Key observables.** Ionic transport properties, conductivity activation energies, and mobility of majority/minority ions.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Site energy distributions, mismatch energy, and spatial correlations between site energies.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The theory assumes thermally activated hopping transport and considers the statistical-mechanical and kinetic aspects of the ion mixture.

**Paper structure.** The paper presents a theoretical framework for the mixed alkali effect, details the statistical-mechanical and kinetic components (probability densities, Fermi distributions, Onsager coefficients), compares the theory to kinetic Monte Carlo simulations, and validates the model against experimental data from phosphate glasses.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The mixed alkali or mixed mobile ion effect in glasses manifests itself by strong nonlinear variations of ionic transport properties upon mixing of different types of mobile ions. We develop a theory for this effect based on thermally activated hopping transport in disordered site energy landscapes that consistently incorporates the statistical-mechanical and kinetic aspects of a mobile ion mixture. This includes a consideration of the joint probability density of site energy states, generalized Fermi distributions for mean site occupations, and cross-terms in the current response described by nondiagonal Onsager coefficients. The theory shows that a mixed alkali effect can arise even when the two ion species share identical site energy distributions. It suffices that sites have distinct energies when occupied by ions of different type. Taking into account that a mismatch energy is needed for ions of one type to occupy sites adapted to the other type, the mixed alkali effect becomes stronger. Spatial correlations between site energies are needed for the mobility of the majority ion to decrease stronger than exponential upon replacement by the minority ion. The theory agrees well with kinetic Monte Carlo simulations. Application to mixed alkali phosphate glasses yields good agreement with measured conductivity activation energies.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27886"></a>
### [Unentangled stoquastic Merlin-Arthur proof systems: the power of unentanglement without destructive interference](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27886v1)

**Authors:** Yupan Liu, Pei Wu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27886v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27886_figures/2604.27886_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1: Relationships among StoqMA(2), its variants, and other complexity classes.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27886_figures/2604.27886_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 3: Conjunction of stoquastic verifiers V (1) x1 , V (2) x2 , and V (3) x3 .</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper explores the complexity of quantum proof systems that are both unentangled and stoquastic. It demonstrates that even without the power of destructive interference, unentangled stoquastic systems can capture significant complexity classes like NP and PSPACE.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the computational power and structural properties of the complexity class StoqMA(2), which involves unentangled (separable) quantum proofs within sign-problem-free (stoquastic) systems.

**Main result.** The authors establish that StoqMA(2) is surprisingly powerful, containing NP and having upper bounds in EXP and PSPACE, while showing that unentanglement provides significant power even without destructive interference.

**Method.** The study employs techniques such as stoquastization of QMA(2) protocols, distribution testing, the Sum-of-Squares (SoS) algorithm, and a new rectangular closure testing framework.

**Model / system.** The model consists of stoquastic Merlin-Arthur proof systems where the witness is a separable state across two registers and the underlying Hamiltonian is sign-problem-free (non-negative off-diagonal elements).

**Key observables.** Completeness and soundness errors, promise gaps, and acceptance probabilities.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of provers (k), proof length (qubit count), and the presence of perfect completeness or exponentially small promise gaps.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The optimality results are stated under the Exponential-Time Hypothesis (ETH).

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 provides a summary diagram of the relationships between various complexity classes like StoqMA(2), QMA(2), NP, and PSPACE; Figure 3 illustrates the architecture of a verifier for the intersection of promise problems.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the StoqMA(2) class, establishes lower bounds via distribution testing, derives upper bounds using the SoS algorithm and rectangular closure testing, and concludes with an analysis of precise and symmetric variants.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Stoquasticity, originating in sign-problem-free physical systems, gives rise to $\sf StoqMA$, introduced by Bravyi, Bessen, and Terhal (2006), a quantum-inspired intermediate class between $\sf MA$ and $\sf AM$. Unentanglement similarly gives rise to ${\sf QMA}(2)$, introduced by Kobayashi, Matsumoto, and Yamakami (CJTCS 2009), which generalizes $\sf QMA$ to two unentangled proofs and still has only the trivial $\sf NEXP$ upper bound. In this work, we initiate a systematic study of the power of unentanglement without destructive interference via ${\sf StoqMA}(2)$, the class of unentangled stoquastic Merlin-Arthur proof systems. Although $\sf StoqMA$ is semi-quantum and may collapse to $\sf MA$, ${\sf StoqMA}(2)$ turns out to be surprisingly powerful. We establish the following results:   - ${\sf NP} \subseteq {\sf StoqMA}(2)$ with $\widetilde{O}(\sqrt{n})$-qubit proofs and completeness error $2^{-{\rm polylog}(n)}$. Conversely, ${\sf StoqMA}(2) \subseteq {\sf EXP}$ via the Sum-of-Squares algorithm of Barak, Kelner, and Steurer (STOC 2014); with our lower bound, our refined analysis yields the optimality of this algorithm under ETH.   - ${\sf StoqMA}(2)_1 \subseteq {\sf PSPACE}$, and the containment holds with completeness error $2^{-2^{{\rm poly}(n)}}$.   - ${\sf PreciseStoqMA}(2)$, a variant of ${\sf StoqMA}(2)$ with exponentially small promise gap, cannot achieve perfect completeness unless ${\sf EXP}={\sf NEXP}$. In contrast, ${\sf PreciseStoqMA}$ achieves perfect completeness, since ${\sf PSPACE} \subseteq {\sf PreciseStoqMA}_1$.   - When the completeness error is negligible, ${\sf StoqMA}(k) = {\sf StoqMA}(2)$ for $k\geq 2$.   Our lower bounds are obtained by stoquastizing the short-proof ${\sf QMA}(2)$ protocols via distribution testing techniques. Our upper bounds for the nearly perfect completeness case are proved via our new rectangular closure testing framework.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27110"></a>
### [Beyond Project-Based Learning: Conference-Style Writing as Authentic Assessment in Interdisciplinary Quantum Engineering Education](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27110v1)

**Authors:** Nischal Binod Gautam, Enrique P. Blair  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27110v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable

**Summary.** This paper explores an advanced pedagogical approach for quantum engineering education that uses conference-style paper writing as a form of authentic assessment. The results suggest that while more challenging than standard projects, this method effectively prepares students for professional scientific communication and research.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates how to move beyond simple project-based learning in quantum engineering education to provide more authentic disciplinary preparation through conference-style writing.

**Main result.** Students found the conference-style paper requirement demanding but meaningful, noting benefits to engagement, confidence, and technical skill development.

**Method.** The study uses post-course survey responses from a pilot run of an introductory quantum mechanics course for engineers.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on an educational curriculum involving project-based learning and a conference-style paper requirement in a quantum engineering course.

**Key observables.** Perceived effects on conceptual understanding, scientific communication, research readiness, and attitudes toward writing.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes that student survey responses accurately reflect the educational impact of the pedagogical intervention.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the context of project-based learning, describes the implementation of the conference-style writing requirement, presents results from student surveys, and concludes with recommendations for scaffolding.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Project-based learning is recognized as an effective approach for improving engagement and applied understanding in STEM education. In quantum engineering courses, however, the question is no longer only whether students benefit from projects but how those projects should culminate if the goal is authentic disciplinary preparation. This paper examines the educational role of a conference-style paper requirement embedded within a project-based learning implementation for an introductory quantum mechanics course for engineers. We use post-course survey responses from students in a pilot run of the course. We evaluate perceived effects on conceptual understanding, scientific communication, research readiness, and attitudes toward the writing requirement itself. The results suggest that students viewed the project as beneficial for engagement, confidence, and technical skill development, while the conference-style paper emerged as a demanding but meaningful component of the experience. We argue that once PBL has been established in quantum mechanics education, conference-style writing can serve as an extension of that model, especially for graduate students. The findings support retaining the conference-paper requirement with improved scaffolding.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27788"></a>
### [Chern number reversal and emergent superconductivity in rhombohedral graphene induced by in-plane magnetic fields](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27788v1)

**Authors:** Xiaozhou Zan, Hangzhe Li, Jiawei Guo, Gengdong Zhou, Kangyao Chen, Cihan Gao, Zijun Xu, Kenji Watanabe, Takashi Taniguchi, Anqi Wang, Jie Shen, Jinsong Zhang, Zhida Song, Yayu Wang  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27788v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates how in-plane magnetic fields can control the Chern number in rhombohedral graphene and induce new superconducting phases. The discovery of a field-induced superconducting phase provides strong evidence for spin-triplet pairing in these moire systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the interplay between topological states and unconventional superconductivity in eight-layer rhombohedral graphene under in-plane magnetic fields.

**Main result.** The study observes Chern number reversal in the QAH state and identifies three distinct superconducting phases with varying responses to in-plane magnetic fields, including a field-induced phase suggesting spin-triplet pairing.

**Method.** Transport measurements on hBN-aligned eight-layer rhombohedral graphene moire superlattices using displacement fields and in-plane magnetic fields as control parameters.

**Model / system.** hBN aligned eight-layer rhombohedral graphene moire superlattices hosting topological flat bands.

**Key observables.** Chern number, superconducting phase transitions, and response to in-plane magnetic fields.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Electron-doped vs. hole-doped carriers, displacement fields, and in-plane magnetic field strength.

**Paper structure.** The paper investigates the electronic properties of 8-layer rhombohedral graphene, specifically focusing on the QAH state and three superconducting phases under magnetic and electric field tuning.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Rhombohedral graphene with topological flat bands offers an ideal platform for realizing correlated and topological quantum phases. Here we investigate hBN aligned eight-layer rhombohedral graphene moire superlattices, which host a robust quantum anomalous Hall (QAH) state alongside three unconventional superconducting phases. For electron-doped carriers away from the moire potential, we observe QAH Chern number reversal driven by the displacement fields and in plane magnetic fields. For hole-doped carriers near the moire superlattice, the three superconducting phases exhibit distinctively different in plane magnetic field responses: one is weakly enhanced, the second is strongly suppressed, and the third exclusively induced by in plane magnetic field. The isotropic in plane magnetic field response in the QAH regime points to interplay between orbital magnetism and spin-orbit coupling, and the field-emergent superconductivity provides compelling evidence for spin-triplet pairing. Our work demonstrates a highly versatile platform for coexisting topological and superconducting states, and highlights in plane magnetic field as a powerful in-situ control knob for engineering novel quantum devices.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27058"></a>
### [Clifft: Fast Exact Simulation of Near-Clifford Quantum Circuits](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27058v1)

**Authors:** Bradley A. Chase, Farrokh Labib  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27058v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable

**Summary.** The authors present Clifft, an open-source simulator that optimizes the classical simulation of near-Clifford circuits by focusing computational effort on a dynamic active subspace. This approach allows for the first exact, large-scale simulation of complex fault-tolerant processes like magic state cultivation. It provides a significant speedup over existing tools, making it possible to study error-correction protocols over hundreds of billions of shots.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Classical simulation of fault-tolerant quantum circuits is limited by exponential scaling in qubit count, T-count, or per-shot tracking overhead.

**Main result.** The introduction of Clifft, a simulator that achieves orders-of-magnitude throughput gains by shifting exponential costs to a dynamic active subspace, enabling the first exact end-to-end simulation of magic state cultivation at scale.

**Method.** Clifft uses an architecture that factors the quantum state into an offline Clifford frame, an online Pauli frame, and a dynamically sized active state vector, resolving deterministic transformations ahead of time.

**Model / system.** Near-Clifford quantum circuits, specifically focusing on fault-tolerant protocols including magic state cultivation and the escape stage.

**Key observables.** Discrepancy between true T-gate circuits and S-proxy circuits; error rates/failures in the escape stage.

**Important parameters / regimes.** T-count, qubit count, peak active virtual dimension, decoder-gap thresholds, and magic state cultivation stages.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The simulation assumes the circuit can be decomposed into Clifford and non-Clifford components where the non-Clifford dimension remains manageable.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the Clifft architecture, compares its performance against existing GPU-accelerated and stabilizer-rank simulators, and demonstrates its utility through large-scale simulations of magic state cultivation and error analysis.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Exact classical simulation of fault-tolerant quantum circuits remains limited by a tradeoff between exponential state vector scaling, exponential $T$-count scaling in stabilizer-rank approaches, and per-shot tracking overhead in sparse generalized stabilizer simulators. In this work, we introduce Clifft, an open-source simulator that shifts the dominant exponential cost from the total qubit count to a dynamic active subspace by factoring the quantum state into an offline Clifford frame, an online Pauli frame, and a dynamically sized active state vector. This architecture resolves deterministic Clifford coordinate transformations ahead of time, generalizing Stim's compile-once, sample-many execution model to circuits with non-Clifford operations. Consequently, exponential simulation costs are determined by the peak active virtual dimension, which expands during non-Clifford operations and contracts during measurements. Clifft remains within a constant factor of standard tools in the pure-Clifford and non-Clifford limits, while delivering up to orders-of-magnitude throughput gains over GPU-accelerated near-Clifford simulators on low-magic fault-tolerant benchmarks. Executing on commodity CPUs and exposing a Stim-like API, Clifft enables, to our knowledge, the first exact end-to-end simulation of magic state cultivation including the escape stage, over hundreds of billions of shots. These simulations show that escape-stage failures suppress the discrepancy between the true $T$-gate circuit and its $S$-proxy at low decoder-gap thresholds, while at high thresholds the full-protocol behavior approaches the larger discrepancy observed in the cultivation stages alone.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.28176"></a>
### [Defending Quantum Classifiers against Adversarial Perturbations through Quantum Autoencoders](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28176v1)

**Authors:** Emma Andrews, Sahan Sanjaya, Prabhat Mishra

This is compared against the confidence threshold T. If the calculated confidence metric C meets the confidence threshold T, the predicted class label from the VQC is used. Otherwise, the sample is rejected as a...</figcaption>

**Summary.** This paper introduces QAE++, a defense framework designed to protect quantum machine learning models from adversarial noise. By using a quantum autoencoder to reconstruct input data and a diagnostic metric to reject suspicious samples, the method achieves much higher classification accuracy under attack than previous methods.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Variational Quantum Classifiers (VQCs) are highly vulnerable to adversarial attacks like FGSM and PGD, where small input perturbations cause incorrect predictions, and existing defenses like adversarial training are often impractical or prone to overfitting.

**Main result.** The proposed QAE++ framework, which uses a quantum autoencoder for purification and a confidence metric for sample rejection, significantly outperforms classical autoencoder defenses, achieving up to a 68% improvement in prediction accuracy under attack.

**Method.** The authors propose an adversarial-training-free defense that uses a Quantum Autoencoder (QAE) to reconstruct and 'purify' input states, combined with a confidence metric based on encoding fidelity and logit difference to identify and reject adversarial samples.

**Model / system.** The study utilizes Variational Quantum Classifiers (VQCs) with strongly entangling layers and Quantum Autoencoders (QAE) for data compression and reconstruction, using amplitude embedding to map MNIST and FashionMNIST data onto qubits.

**Key observables.** Prediction accuracy, encoding fidelity (measured via SWAP test), and logit difference between the highest and second-highest class predictions.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Perturbation magnitude (epsilon), latent space dimension (k), and confidence thresholds (T) derived from clean validation sets.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The decoder is assumed to be the Hermitian conjugate of the encoder (reversibility), and the defense threshold is determined using a clean validation set.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate adversarial attacks on MNIST, the architectures of VQCs, CAEs, and QAEs, the SWAP test for fidelity, and various performance plots showing accuracy improvements and reconstruction quality.

**Paper structure.** The paper identifies the vulnerability of VQCs, introduces the QAE-based purification method, details the QAE++ confidence metric, presents comparative evaluations against classical and undefended models, and discusses limitations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Machine learning models can learn from data samples to carry out various tasks efficiently. When data samples are adversarially manipulated, such as by insertion of carefully crafted noise, it can cause the model to make mistakes. Quantum machine learning models are also vulnerable to such adversarial attacks, especially in image classification using variational quantum classifiers. While there are promising defenses against these adversarial perturbations, such as training with adversarial samples, they face practical limitations. For example, they are not applicable in scenarios where training with adversarial samples is either not possible or can overfit the models on one type of attack. In this paper, we propose an adversarial training-free defense framework that utilizes a quantum autoencoder to purify the adversarial samples through reconstruction. Moreover, our defense framework provides a confidence metric to identify potentially adversarial samples that cannot be purified the quantum autoencoder. Extensive evaluation demonstrates that our defense framework can significantly outperform state-of-the-art in prediction accuracy (up to 68%) under adversarial attacks.

</details>

### [Dimensionality-Driven Electronic and Orbital Transitions Mediating Interfacial Magnetism in LaNiO3/CaMnO3 Observed In Situ](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28054v1)

**Authors:** B-A. Courchene, A. Hampel, S. Beck, J. R. Paudel, J. D. Grassi, L. A. Lapinski, A. M. Derrico, M. Terilli, M. Kareev, C. Klewe, A. Gloskovskii, C. Schlueter, S. K. Chaluvadi, F. Mazzola, I. Vobornik, P. Orgiani, J. Chakhalian, A. J. Millis, A. X. Gray  
**Type:** both · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.28054v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

<img src="2604.28054_figures/2604.28054_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Fig. 2 | Dimensionality-driven evolution of the LNO spectral function from DFT+DMFT. a, Unfolded</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.28054_figures/2604.28054_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Fig. 3 | Polarization-dependent ARPES linear dichroism in LNO/CMO superlattices. a-d, Momentum-</figcaption>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that the emergent ferromagnetism at the LaNiO3/CaMnO3 interface is controlled by the electronic state of the LaNiO3 layer. As the layer is thinned, a metal-insulator transition and orbital reconstruction occur, which shuts down the charge transfer necessary for interfacial magnetism.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how dimensionality-driven electronic and orbital transitions in ultrathin LaNiO3 layers mediate emergent interfacial magnetism in LaNiO3/CaMnO3 superlattices.

**Main result.** Reducing LaNiO3 thickness drives a metal-insulator transition and an orbital-polarization crossover, which suppresses charge transfer across the interface and subsequently suppresses the interfacial Mn magnetic moment.

**Method.** The study combines in situ pulsed-laser deposition, polarization-dependent ARPES, XMCD, and HAXPES with first-principles DFT+DMFT calculations.

**Model / system.** Epitaxial LaNiO3/CaMnO3 superlattices with varying LaNiO3 thicknesses (1, 3, 4, and 6 unit cells) grown on LaAlO3 (001) substrates.

**Key observables.** Fermi-surface intensity maps, quasiparticle coherence, orbital polarization (dz2 vs dx2-y2), interfacial Mn magnetic moment, and near-Ef spectral weight.

**Important parameters / regimes.** LaNiO3 thickness (1 to 6 unit cells), ultrathin limit, metal-insulator transition (MIT) onset at 3 u.c., and biaxial compressive strain (-1.1%).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Calculations were performed in the paramagnetic state and used Padé approximants for analytical continuation of self-energy.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the superlattice schematic and experimental geometry; Supplemental figures show structural characterization (XRD, XRR, LEED, STM), chemical composition (HAXPES), and theoretical spectral functions (DFT+DMFT).

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of interfacial magnetism, describes the experimental growth and characterization of superlattices, presents ARPES and XMCD results showing the thickness-dependent electronic and magnetic transitions, and concludes with DFT+DMFT validation of the orbital-driven mechanism.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Emergent magnetic states at oxide interfaces arise from the interplay of charge transfer, orbital reconstruction, and dimensional confinement, offering a route to engineered correlated-electron behavior in nanoscale spintronic materials. Here, we combine in situ synthesis, polarization-dependent angle-resolved photoelectron spectroscopy, X-ray magnetic circular dichroism, and first-principles electronic-structure calculations to investigate LaNiO3/CaMnO3 superlattices. We show that reducing the LaNiO3 thickness drives a metal-insulator transition accompanied by loss of electronic coherence and an orbital-polarization crossover in the ultrathin limit. These changes weaken charge transfer across the interface and suppress the interfacial Mn magnetic moment in CaMnO3, revealing that the emergent ferromagnetic state is directly governed by electronic confinement in LaNiO3. The insulating state and orbital reconstruction are reproduced by density functional theory combined with dynamical mean-field theory. Together, these results establish a direct and tunable coupling among electronic, orbital, and magnetic degrees of freedom in oxide heterostructures.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27992"></a>
### [Discontinuous BBP transitions](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27992v1)

**Authors:** Dario Bocchi, Giulio Biroli, Chiara Cammarota, Federico Ricci-Tersenghi  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27992v1>

Each row shows (left to right) the inverse resolvent map z(g), the spectral density near the right edge, and the eigenvector overlap as a function of the spike strength. **Summary.** This paper identifies a new class of discontinuous BBP transitions in random matrices where signal detection occurs abruptly. It reveals that in finite systems, an extended pre-critical region allows for signal recovery even below the theoretical threshold, which has significant implications for signal processing and neural network training.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the conditions under which the Baik-Ben Arous-Péché (BBP) transition, which governs the emergence of outlier eigenvalues in spiked random matrices, is discontinuous rather than continuous.

**Main result.** The authors show that BBP transitions are discontinuous when the spectral density vanishes super-linearly (exponent a > 2) at the spectral edge, leading to a finite jump in eigenvector overlap and a pre-critical region where signal recovery occurs below the asymptotic threshold.

**Method.** The study employs Random Matrix Theory, specifically using the resolvent formalism, Stieltjes transform, and Free Probability Theory (R-transform) to analyze the spectral properties and finite-size scaling.

**Model / system.** The paper analyzes spiked models consisting of rank-one perturbations of large random matrices, specifically deformed Gaussian Orthogonal Ensembles (GOE) and reweighted Wishart ensembles.

**Key observables.** Outlier eigenvalue, eigenvector overlap (correlation between the signal and the leading eigenvector), and spectral edge density.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Signal-to-noise ratio (alpha), power-law exponent of the spectral density (a), matrix size (N), and the aspect ratio (q) for Wishart ensembles.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes the large-N limit for the fundamental transition and asymptotic freeness between the unperturbed matrix and the Wigner component.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares continuous vs. discontinuous BBP transitions via the inverse resolvent and overlap; Figure 2 illustrates the transition type in specific ensembles; Figure 4 demonstrates finite-size scaling and data collapse for the maximum eigenvalue.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the BBP transition, establishes the mathematical condition for discontinuity based on spectral edge decay, analyzes specific models (deformed GOE and Wishart), develops a finite-size scaling theory, and discusses implications for machine learning and biological systems.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The Baik-Ben Arous-Peche (BBP) transition sets fundamental limits for detecting low-rank structure in noisy high-dimensional data and underlies a wide range of spectral methods in many fields from physics to statistics and data sciences. In standard settings, this transition is continuous, implying that signal recovery emerges gradually above a sharp threshold. We show that BBP transitions can instead be discontinuous in very general settings and provide a full theory of this phenomenon. When the eigenvalue density vanishes faster than linearly at the spectral edge, the overlap between the leading eigenvector and the signal jumps discontinuously at the critical point. We study this mechanism in deformed Gaussian and reweighted Wishart ensembles. We analyze in detail the finite-size effects, which play a central and qualitatively new role in the discontinuous BBP transition. Unlike the continuous BBP transition, we establish the existence of an extended pre-critical region where informative eigenvectors emerge well before the asymptotic threshold. The main consequence-and difference from the continuous BBP transition-is that signal recovery can occur at significantly lower signal-to-noise ratio and it is accompanied by strong sample-to-sample variability. Our results show the relevance and the novelty of the discontinuous BBP transition, and highlight the practical implications for signal detection.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27310"></a>
### [Exotic Spin Excitation Continuum in a Weakly Coupled Quantum Chainsaw Antiferromagnet](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27310v1)

**Authors:** Asiri Thennakoon, Prena Chaudhary, Sankha Subhra Bakshi, Tommy Park, Tristen Lowrey, Daniel Pajerowski, Christina Hoffmann, Junghong H. He, Hiroaki Ueda, Collin Broholm, Gia-Wei Chern, Seung-Hun Lee

**Summary.** This paper identifies a unique state of 'anisotropic fractionalization' in a Ti-based kagome-related antiferromagnet. By using neutron scattering, the authors show that a 2D lattice can behave as a network of weakly coupled 1D chains, demonstrating that 1D-like quantum criticality can survive within a 2D framework.

**Why it may be interesting.** The paper explores how many-body dynamics and fractionalization can persist across a dimensional crossover, which is highly relevant to understanding the stability of exotic excitations in many-body systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the dimensional crossover of quantum disorder, specifically how fractionalized excitations evolve when transitioning from 1D criticality to 2D topological order in a frustrated lattice.

**Main result.** The researchers discovered a regime of 'anisotropic fractionalization' in a Ti-based kagome-related magnet, where 2D lattice dynamics reorganize into a network of weakly coupled spin-1/2 chains, maintaining 1D-like signatures.

**Method.** The study combines single-crystal neutron diffraction for structural refinement with inelastic neutron scattering (INS) using the CNCS and TOPAZ spectrometers at the Spallation Neutron Source.

**Model / system.** The physical system is the Ti-based quantum antiferromagnet Cs8LiNa3Ti12F48, which features a distorted kagome lattice of Ti3+ (S=1/2) ions and an anisotropic exchange Hamiltonian with four distinct nearest-neighbor couplings and Dzyaloshinskii-Moriya interactions.

**Key observables.** Dynamic spin correlation function S(Q, h-omega), equal-time response function S(Q), magnetic susceptibility, and crystal structure refinement statistics.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Exchange couplings (JA=11.26, JB=3.20, JC=8.15, JD=1.22 meV), Curie-Weiss temperature (theta_CW = -36.9 K), and measurement temperatures (1.7 K and 30 K).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The structural refinement assumed a fixed position for the Lithium atom due to high correlation with the local background; the magnetic model assumes a 2D nearest-neighbor kagome framework.

**Figures summary.** The figures likely include crystal structure refinements, magnetic susceptibility plots, and momentum-resolved inelastic neutron scattering maps showing the excitation continuum and rod-like scattering.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of dimensional crossover, describes the crystal structure and synthesis of the Ti-based magnet, presents neutron diffraction and susceptibility data, details the inelastic neutron scattering results showing the excitation continuum, and concludes with a discussion on anisotropic fractionalization.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Collective motions in strongly interacting magnets involve many spins and are often described in terms of integer-spin excitations. However, in certain cases, the collective motion can behave as if these integer excitations break apart into smaller, particle-like entities with unusual properties. Such fractionalized excitations in quantum magnets are commonly associated either with topological order in two dimensions or with criticality in one dimension. It remains unclear how these distinct mechanisms are connected across a dimensional crossover. Here we investigate the Ti-based quantum antiferromagnet, $Cs_{8}LiNa_{3}Ti_{12}F_{48}$, in which $Ti^{3+}$ ($3d^{1}$, $S=1/2$) ions interact antiferromagnetically within distorted kagome planes. Our inelastic neutron scattering study on a single crystal reveals a frustrated network of weakly coupled spin-$1/2$ chainsaws, realizing a regime of dimensional frustration in which interchain couplings fail to establish coherent two-dimensional order. The magnetic excitation spectrum exhibits a strong continuum spanning the full measured momentum and energy phase space. In addition, the dynamic spin correlation function displays rod-like scattering in momentum space, indicating a quasi-one-dimensional nature of the magnetic correlations. These results point to fractionalized excitations with intrinsically directional character, demonstrating that signatures of one-dimensional criticality can persist within a two-dimensional lattice. Our findings establish anisotropic fractionalization as a distinct organizing principle for quantum-disordered states.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

**Summary.** This paper presents a new quantum algorithm for the NP-hard densest k-subgraph problem using Grover's search and Dicke states. It demonstrates a quadratic speedup over classical brute-force search and provides an explicit gate-level implementation for the edge-counting oracle. The method avoids the complexities of QUBO mapping and is applicable to other graph optimization tasks.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Finding the densest k-vertex subgraph in an undirected graph, which is an NP-hard combinatorial optimization problem.

**Main result.** The authors propose a quantum search algorithm that provides a provable quadratic speedup over classical brute-force search, with an oracle cost scaling as O(k^2 sqrt(N)).

**Method.** The approach uses a Grover-based search framework with Dicke state preparation, a QFT-based edge-counting oracle, and an iterative thresholding strategy.

**Model / system.** The computational model is a circuit-based quantum computer utilizing a vertex register of n qubits and an edge counter register of L+1 qubits, operating on an undirected graph G=(V, E) represented by an adjacency matrix.

**Key observables.** Edge count within the subgraph and the oracle call cost (number of queries required for a 95% success probability).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of vertices (n), subgraph size (k), search space size (N = n choose k), and the penalty coefficient (lambda) for the QUBO formulation.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The algorithm assumes the availability of efficient Dicke state preparation and that the high T-count/gate depth of the QFT-based oracle can be managed.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the Grover iteration sequence; Figure 2 details the gate-level oracle scheme; Figures 3-5 illustrate the diffusion operator, convergence of edge counts, and numerical simulations; Figure 6-7 show the scaling of oracle costs relative to the search space size.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the DkS problem and its applications, reviews existing QUBO and variational methods, proposes a new Grover-based algorithm with explicit oracle construction, presents numerical simulations comparing the algorithm to classical baselines, and concludes with scaling analysis and limitations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

This paper addresses the problem of finding the densest $k$-vertex subgraph in an arbitrary graph. This problem is NP-hard and has important applications in social network analysis, fraud detection, recommendation systems, and bioinformatics. We propose two quantum approaches to solve this problem: reduction to Quadratic Unconstrained Binary Optimization (QUBO) and using Grover's quantum search algorithm. For the latter approach, we present an explicit gate-based oracle circuit utilizing Dicke states and Quantum Fourier Transform for edge counting. Numerical simulations demonstrate a quadratic speedup over classical Brute-force search.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper presents a new method called FinITE for performing finite imaginary-time evolution on quantum computers to solve optimization problems. It provides an analytical way to choose the optimal evolution time to guarantee a specific ground-state fidelity while controlling the computational cost of amplitude amplification. The method is particularly efficient for higher-order optimization problems as it avoids the need for complex quadratization.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Implementing non-unitary imaginary-time evolution (ITE) on quantum hardware for solving optimization problems, specifically managing the trade-off between ground-state fidelity and the success probability of the LCU implementation.

**Main result.** The authors derive an exact identity relating LCU success probability and ground-subspace fidelity, providing a closed-form rule for choosing an optimal finite imaginary time beta-star to achieve target fidelity.

**Method.** The FinITE algorithm uses the Linear Combination of Unitaries (LCU) framework for termwise block-encoding of commuting Pauli-Z strings, combined with Fixed-Point Amplitude Amplification (FPAA) to boost success probability.

**Model / system.** Polynomial Unconstrained Binary Optimization (PUBO) problems, including QUBO and HUBO, represented by diagonal Pauli-Z cost Hamiltonians consisting of pairwise-commuting terms.

**Key observables.** Ground-subspace fidelity (Fg), LCU success probability (PLCU), and the spectral gap (Delta).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Imaginary time (beta), initial ground-subspace overlap (gamma_0), L1-norm of Pauli coefficients (W), and the spectral gap (Delta).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The Hamiltonian must consist of pairwise-commuting Pauli terms (diagonal PUBO case), and the initial state must have a non-zero overlap with the ground subspace.

**Figures summary.** Simulations verifying the exponential decay identity of the success-fidelity product, comparisons of different initial state warm-starts, and the performance of FPAA in amplifying success probability.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the FinITE algorithm, establishes the mathematical framework for LCU-based ITE, proves the exact spectral identity and the beta-selection rule, provides a complexity analysis for gate counts and queries, and validates the theory via statevector and shot-based simulations on QUBO and HUBO instances.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Imaginary-time evolution is a standard primitive for ground-state preparation but is nonunitary, precluding direct quantum implementation. We develop Finite Imaginary-Time Evolution (FinITE), a finite-beta construction for diagonal Pauli-Z cost Hamiltonians arising from polynomial unconstrained binary optimization (PUBO) instances, including QUBO and HUBO cases. FinITE uses the linear-combination-of-unitaries (LCU) framework to implement a scaled imaginary-time propagator. The commuting Pauli-Z structure makes termwise block-encodings compose without product-formula error, and higher-order Pauli-Z terms are handled directly without quadratization. The structure yields an exact finite-beta identity between the LCU success probability and the ground-subspace fidelity. Combined with a gap-based fidelity lower bound, the identity yields a closed-form sufficient imaginary-time threshold beta-star for a chosen target fidelity. The threshold depends on estimates of the spectral gap and the initial ground-subspace overlap. Because the LCU success event is flagged by a known ancilla outcome, we integrate fixed-point amplitude amplification with an explicit query-complexity bound. Statevector simulations verify the identity on a five-vertex MaxCut (QUBO) and an eight-qubit cubic HUBO instance, and shot-based simulations on the MaxCut instance illustrate the predicted finite-beta threshold and amplification procedure.

</details>

### [g-tensor Optimization in Ge/SiGe Quantum Dots](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28081v1)

**Authors:** Aram Shojaei, Edmondo Valvo, Maximilian Rimbach-Russ, Eliska Greplova, Ana Silva  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.28081v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable

**Summary.** This paper presents a numerical framework for optimizing the g-tensor in germanium-based quantum dots to mitigate the effects of device variability. By engineering the heterostructure potential, the authors show it is possible to suppress specific g-tensor components, which is a key step toward reliable, large-scale semiconductor quantum computing.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Device performance in planar germanium heterostructures is limited by quantum dot variability, which causes uncertainty in qubit energy levels and random spin quantization axis orientations.

**Main result.** The authors developed a flexible optimization framework to engineer g-tensor properties, demonstrating that reshaping the out-of-plane potential in SiGe-Ge-SiGe quantum wells can suppress in-plane g-tensor components to enable gapless single-spin qubit encoding.

**Method.** A numerical optimization framework is used to design heterostructure engineering parameters, specifically adjusting silicon concentration within the quantum well to reshape the potential.

**Model / system.** The system consists of planar germanium heterostructures (SiGe-Ge-SiGe quantum wells) hosting hole-spin qubits.

**Key observables.** g-tensor components (in-plane and out-of-plane), spin quantization axis orientation, and qubit energy levels.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Silicon concentration within the quantum well, out-of-plane potential profile, and g-tensor anisotropy.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The framework assumes that heterostructure engineering via silicon concentration adjustment is a viable way to reshape the potential.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of variability in Ge-based qubits, presents a new optimization framework for g-tensor engineering, uses the reshaping of SiGe-Ge-SiGe quantum wells as a benchmark case, and concludes with implications for scalable quantum computing.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Planar germanium heterostructures hosting hole-spin qubits are among the leading platforms for scalable semiconductor-based quantum computing. Yet, device performance is hindered by significant quantum dot variability, which leads to uncertainty in qubit energy levels and random orientations of the spin quantization axis. Tailored control of the g-tensor offers a strategy to overcome these limitations and achieve more reliable qubit operations. Here, we introduce a flexible optimization framework for engineering g-tensor properties. As a benchmark, we numerically obtain the optimal reshaping of the out-of-plane potential in a SiGe-Ge-SiGe quantum well to suppress the in-plane g-tensor components and realize the recently proposed gapless single-spin qubit encoding. This reshaping is achieved through heterostructure engineering, specifically by adjusting the silicon concentration within the quantum well, though the framework remains readily adaptable to alternative design objectives. Our results provide practical design principles for improving the tunability of the spin response, paving the way towards large-scale germanium-based quantum computers.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27779"></a>
### [Geometric memory in incomplete phase transitions across dimensions](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27779v1)

**Authors:** F. Tolea, M. Tolea  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27779v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that the memory of phase distributions during incomplete solid-state transitions is driven by a purely geometric mechanism. It shows that the strength of this memory is highly dependent on the dimensionality of the growing phases, being most pronounced in 2D.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how dimensionality and geometric blocking affect the persistence of memory in the size distribution of phases during incomplete solid-state phase transitions.

**Main result.** The researchers find that while geometric memory is robust across different geometries, it is significantly stronger in 2D than in 3D or 3D lamellar configurations.

**Method.** The authors use a nucleation-and-growth model involving self-similar expansion and impingement, supplemented by differential scanning calorimetry simulations and Shannon size-entropy calculations.

**Model / system.** A model of first-order solid-solid phase transitions where plates (squares, cubes, or lamellae) grow homothetically until they reach a maximum size or are stopped by impingement.

**Key observables.** Size mass ratio, plate-size distribution, Shannon size-entropy, and differential scanning calorimetry (DSC) signals.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Dimensionality (2D, 3D, and 3DL), plate geometry, and the degree of incomplete reversion.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The phase transition is modeled as a purely geometric process of nucleation and growth with regular plate shapes and self-similar expansion.

**Figures summary.** The paper includes growth snapshots, arrest-regrowth cycles, size distributions, and simulated DSC curves.

**Paper structure.** The paper extends a 2D model to 3D and 3DL geometries, introduces a quantitative memory descriptor, analyzes the impact of dimensionality, and provides simulation-based validation.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We model a direct solid-state phase transition through a nucleation-and-growth process in which plates have simple, regular shapes - squares, cubes, or square-faced lamellae - and grow homothetically (self-similarly) until they either reach a randomly assigned maximum size or are stopped by impingement with previously formed plates. The reverse transformation is represented by the preferential disappearance of smaller plates, while larger plates are retained during an incomplete reversion. A subsequent direct transformation therefore produces a modified plate-size distribution, a memory effect that forms the main focus of this study. Building upon an earlier two-dimensional (2D) formulation, we extend the model to cubes (3D) and to lamellar plates (3DL) in order to examine how dimensionality affects transformation memory. We introduce a quantitative descriptor of memory, the size mass ratio, and find that memory is robust in all geometries but overall stronger in 2D than in 3D or 3DL. We provide growth snapshots, arrest-regrowth cycles, size distributions, and differential scanning calorimetry simulations, and we compute the Shannon size-entropy to quantify configurational diversity. Although motivated by the thermal memory effect in shape-memory alloys, the model more generally identifies a purely geometric mechanism for memory in first-order solid-solid transformations, highlighting the role of dimensionality and geometric blocking in controlling the strength of transformation memory.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27817"></a>
### [High-Girth Regular Quantum LDPC Codes from Square-Base Hypergraph Products via CPM Lifts](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27817v1)

**Authors:** Koki Okada, Kenta Kasai  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27817v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27817_figures/2604.27817_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1: p–FER plot for the randomized P = 64 lift derived from the generalized-quadrangle base B15. Each point aggregates independent decoding trials at the same p, using the degeneracy- aware BP decoder followed by OSD-lite post-processing; shaded bands indicate Wilson 95% confidence intervals. Zero-failure points are plotted at the upper endpoint of the Wilson interval. The dashed vertical line is the population-dynamics estimate pDE ≈0.1529 of the BP density- evolution threshold for the (3, 6)-regular CSS-LDPC ensemble on the depolarizing channel, and the dash-dotted vertical line is the depolarizing-channel hashing limit phash ≈0.18929.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27817_figures/2604.27817_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 2: Matrix plots of the smaller square base matrices.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27817_figures/2604.27817_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Figure 3: Matrix plots of the larger square base matrices.</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27817_figures/2604.27817_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Figure 4: Matrix plots of the HGP check matrices obtained from the Fano-plane base matrix B7.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper presents a new method for constructing high-girth, regular quantum LDPC codes by lifting square-base hypergraph product matrices. It demonstrates that these codes can achieve high-performance decoding near theoretical limits, providing a scalable approach to designing robust quantum error-correcting codes.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Designing regular, high-girth quantum low-density parity-check (QLDPC) codes using square-base hypergraph product constructions while managing short cycles and logical qubit counts.

**Main result.** The authors developed a construction using Circulant Permutation Matrix (CPM) lifts that produces a [[28800, 62]] girth-8 (3,6)-regular CSS-LDPC code with exceptional decoding performance at $p=0.1402$.

**Method.** The method uses square binary base matrices derived from finite geometries (like Fano planes and generalized quadrangles) combined with CPM lifting and a randomized local search to satisfy CSS orthogonality and cycle-suppression constraints.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on CSS-type quantum LDPC codes under the depolarizing noise channel, utilizing the Hypergraph Product (HGP) framework.

**Key observables.** Tanner girth, code rate, logical qubit count (k), decoding failure rate (Frame Error Rate), and the depolarizing error probability (p).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Depolarizing probability $p=0.1402$, code parameters $[[28800, 62]]$, and the theoretical hashing limit $p_{hash} \approx 0.18929$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The construction is fundamentally limited to a Tanner girth of 8 due to orthogonality-forced cycles; the design rate of the square-base construction is zero.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a performance plot of FER vs. p; Figures 2 and 3 display visual matrix representations of various base matrices; Figure 4 illustrates the HGP check matrices for the Fano plane construction.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the square-base HGP construction, establishes conditions for regularity and girth control, analyzes the constraints of CPM lifting, presents specific high-girth base matrix examples, and concludes with numerical decoding performance simulations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study square-base Calderbank--Shor--Steane (CSS) hypergraph-product codes as a finite-length class for regular high-girth quantum low-density parity-check (LDPC) design. For base matrices of small column weight, we give checkable conditions for regularity, rank deficiency, and short-cycle exclusion, and we present explicit column-weight-three and column-weight-four examples with Tanner girth 6 and 8. We also analyze circulant permutation matrix (CPM) lifts of this class. Using the standard voltage-sum criterion, we identify orthogonality-forced Tanner 8-cycles and show that CPM lifting cannot raise the Tanner girth beyond 8 when these cycles are present. As a representative finite-length instance, a randomized CPM lift of the girth-8 base construction gives a $[[28800,62]]$ girth-8 $(3,6)$-regular CSS-LDPC code. Under degeneracy-aware belief-propagation decoding with optional ordered-statistics-decoding-lite post-processing, this code produced zero decoding failures in $2.993\times 10^8$ independent trials at depolarizing probability $p=0.1402$; the Wilson 95% upper confidence bound is $1.28\times 10^{-8}$.

</details>

### [Local Current Algebra for the HK Universality Class](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27051v1)

**Authors:** Yuting Bai, Philip W. Phillips  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27051v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper resolves the non-locality criticism of the Hatsugai-Kohmoto model by showing it can be reformulated as a local theory using real-space currents. By establishing an su(2) affine Lie algebra structure, the authors prove that the physical response of the model remains unchanged, reinforcing the utility of current algebras for studying strong interactions.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the criticism that the Hatsugai-Kohmoto (HK) model is non-local in real space by seeking a local, effective Hamiltonian in terms of real-space currents.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that a Hamiltonian based on an su(2) affine Lie algebra eliminates the non-locality of the HK model and yields a charge susceptibility identical to the original fermionic field model.

**Method.** The study employs the Bjorken-Johnson-Low (BJL) prescription to evaluate anomalous commutators and uses a current algebra construction (Sugawara-like) to derive the effective theory.

**Model / system.** The system is a 1D doped Mott insulator described by the Hatsugai-Kohmoto (HK) model, which captures Mott physics via the breaking of Z2 symmetry and is considered a solvable alternative to the Hubbard model.

**Key observables.** Charge susceptibility (direct and mixed components), charge density, and current-current correlators.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Interaction strength U, Fermi velocity vF, Fermi momentum kF, and the Dirac limit (large kF and mass m).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the Dirac limit, a quadratic dispersion, and focuses on the low-energy, long-wavelength (IR) dynamics of the hole-doped ground state.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining the HK model and its non-locality problem, introduces the current algebra construction using the BJL prescription, derives the commutation relations and effective Hamiltonian, and concludes by verifying the consistency of the charge susceptibility.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We show that a Hamiltonian in terms of the local real-space currents obeying an $\mathfrak{su}_1(2)$ affine Lie algebra eliminates the non-locality in the Hatsugai-Kohmoto model for a doped Mott insulator. We establish this local correspondence through the Bjorken-Johnson-Low prescription for anomalous commutators. With this result, we show that the charge susceptibility computed from the current Hamiltonian is identical to that with the elemental Fermionic fields. Consequently, the HK model is local in real space, though not in terms of the Fermionic fields, thereby eliminating the key criticism of this model and reinforcing the utility of current algebras for strong interactions.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27507"></a>
### [Magnetic Quantum Criticality inside the Superconducting State Revealed by Penetration Depth Scaling with Local $T_{\mathrm c}$](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27507v1)

**Authors:** Yusuke Iguchi, Kaede Inoh, Ryosuke Koizumi, Makoto Yokoyama  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27507v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable

**Summary.** Researchers used scanning SQUID microscopy to reveal a magnetic quantum critical point hidden inside the superconducting state of Zn-doped CeCoIn5. By using local transition temperatures to account for doping disorder, they identified a disorder-modified quantum critical regime. This method provides a new way to study intrinsic quantum criticality in inhomogeneous unconventional superconductors.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Identifying a magnetic quantum critical point hidden within the superconducting state of Zn-doped CeCoIn5, which is typically obscured by doping-induced inhomogeneity.

**Main result.** The discovery of a magnetic quantum critical point embedded in the superconducting state, characterized by a disorder-modified critical exponent that exceeds the clean spin-density-wave value.

**Method.** Scanning SQUID microscopy was used to measure local superconducting transition temperature (Tc) and magnetic penetration depth (lambda(0)), allowing for scaling analysis based on local Tc to bypass doping inhomogeneity.

**Model / system.** Zn-doped CeCoIn5, a heavy-fermion superconductor.

**Key observables.** Local superconducting transition temperature (Tc) and magnetic penetration depth at zero temperature (lambda(0)).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Zn substitution level, local Tc, and the critical exponent of the magnetic penetration depth.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The scaling of lambda(0) can be accurately parameterized by local Tc rather than nominal Zn concentration to account for disorder.

**Paper structure.** The paper presents the experimental observation of a peak in lambda(0), describes the scanning SQUID methodology for local measurements, performs scaling analysis using local Tc, and compares the extracted critical exponent to the clean spin-density-wave limit.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We demonstrate a magnetic quantum critical point embedded within the superconducting state of Zn-doped CeCoIn$_5$, revealed by a pronounced peak in the magnetic penetration depth at zero temperature $λ(0)$. Using scanning SQUID microscopy, we determine the local superconducting transition temperature $T_{\mathrm c}$ and $λ(0)$. By parameterizing $λ(0)$ in terms of the local $T_{\mathrm c}$ rather than nominal Zn substitution, we circumvent the ambiguity caused by doping inhomogeneity and enable a more precise extraction of the critical exponent. The extracted exponent exceeds the clean spin-density-wave value, indicating a disorder-modified quantum critical regime. The enhancement of $λ(0)$ reflects the suppression of the superfluid stiffness and is consistent with critical scaling. Our approach provides a route to uncover intrinsic quantum critical behavior hidden by inhomogeneity in unconventional superconductors.

</details>
