</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27294_figures/2604.27294_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> Figure 1(d) shows the theoretical curve 𝛺𝑛𝑜𝑟𝑚(VG) alongside the normalised</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** The researchers demonstrated how to electrically tune the polarization of terahertz light by using a gate voltage to reshape the Fermi surface in a Dirac semimetal. By selectively modulating the Berry curvature-driven component of the emission, they achieved programmable control of THz chirality. This provides a new route for creating on-chip, electrically controllable chiral light sources.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Achieving all-electrical, deterministic control over the chirality (polarization state) of Terahertz (THz) emission by manipulating quantum geometry.

**Main result.** Demonstrated that electrostatic gating of Cd3As2 can reshape Fermi pockets to selectively modulate the Berry curvature-driven THz component, enabling programmable tuning of THz polarization across the Poincaré sphere.

**Method.** THz emission spectroscopy using circularly polarized femtosecond laser pulses on a gated Cd3As2 thin-film device, combined with Stokes parameter analysis.

**Model / system.** A 40 nm thin film of the 3D Dirac semimetal Cd3As2 in a field-effect transistor geometry, subject to Floquet Weyl physics via optical excitation.

**Key observables.** THz electric field components (Ex and Ey), ellipticity angle (chi), azimuth angle (psi), and Stokes parameters (S1, S2, S3).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Gate voltage (Vg) from -90 V to +90 V, Fermi energy (Ef) shifts, and Berry curvature integral.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes a monopole-like divergence for Berry curvature near Weyl nodes and uses an asymmetric screening model for carrier accumulation/depletion.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1: Schematic of Floquet band engineering and device architecture; Fig 2: Evidence of selective gate-dependent tuning of THz components; Fig 3: Evolution of polarization ellipses across voltages; Fig 4: Quantitative mapping of Stokes parameters and ellipticity vs. voltage.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of controlling optical observables via quantum geometry, describes the Floquet-Weyl mechanism in Cd3As2, presents experimental evidence of gate-tunable THz chirality, provides quantitative verification via Berry curvature integration, and concludes with potential applications for programmable THz sources.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum geometry encoded in the momentum space structure of electronic wavefunctions, governs charge dynamics through Berry curvature, enabling unconventional transport and optical responses. In topological semimetals, this geometry is sampled over Fermi pockets, suggesting electrical control by Fermi surface tuning, yet such control has remained largely limited to DC transport. Here we show that electrostatic gating of the 3D Dirac semimetal Cd3As2 reshapes Fermi pockets surrounding photoinduced Floquet Weyl nodes, enabling electrical control of terahertz (THz) emission chirality. Gate tuning selectively modulates the Berry curvature driven linearly polarized THz component by up to 60% and 49% at positive and negative bias, respectively, while the orthogonal linearly polarized photon-drag component remains unchanged. With the two orthogonal fields intrinsically phase-locked at \sfracπ{2} by the excitation geometry, the selective gate-tuned amplitude control enables the polarization tuning across the Poincaré sphere, achieving near-circular polarization (χ\approx-42°) at +10 V. These results establish Fermi surface tuning as a general route to programmable quantum geometric control of chiral terahertz emission.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27719"></a>
### [Emergent electric fields driven by phonon-coupled skyrmion resonances](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27719v1)

**Authors:** Seno Aji  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27719v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **2/5**

**Summary.** This paper provides a theoretical framework to understand how lattice vibrations (phonons) can drive electric fields in skyrmion lattices. It shows that internal skyrmion dynamics like breathing and rotation can generate measurable electrical signals even without moving the entire lattice. This is significant for developing new ways to control and detect topological magnetic textures using light or microwaves.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to describe the generation of macroscopic emergent electric fields resulting from the coupling between phonons and the breathing/rotational dynamics of a skyrmion lattice under microwave excitation.

**Main result.** The study identifies the specific symmetry and dynamical conditions required to produce both rectified (dc) and oscillating (ac) electric fields, even without net translational motion of the lattice.

**Method.** The authors develop a coarse-grained theoretical description and a unified framework for analyzing spin-charge-lattice coupling in the long-wavelength phonon limit.

**Model / system.** The system consists of a skyrmion lattice in topological magnets subject to phonon-coupled lattice deformations and microwave excitation.

**Key observables.** Macroscopic emergent electric fields (dc and ac components), skyrmion profile parameters, and harmonic components of the electrodynamic response.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Equilibrium radius, domain-wall width, dynamical resonance frequency, long-wavelength phonon limit (q -> 0), and finite phonon frequency.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis focuses on the dilute-lattice limit and the long-wavelength phonon limit.

**Paper structure.** The paper presents a theoretical development of a coarse-grained model, followed by an analysis of symmetry and dynamical conditions for electric field generation, and concludes with the identification of harmonic components in the electrodynamic response.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We develop a coarse-grained theoretical description of the macroscopic emergent electric field generated by phonon-coupled lattice deformations in the breathing and rotational dynamics of a skyrmion lattice under microwave excitation. The analysis identifies the symmetry and dynamical conditions that yield rectified (dc) and oscillating (ac) electric fields, even in the absence of net translational motion of the skyrmion lattice, particularly in the dilute-lattice limit. Using experimentally measurable skyrmion profile parameters such as the equilibrium radius, domain-wall width, and dynamical resonance frequency of skyrmion lattice, the model further enables identification of harmonic components contributing to the observed macroscopic electrodynamic response in the long-wavelength phonon limit ($q \to 0$) and at finite phonon frequency, providing a unified framework for phonon-driven spin-charge-lattice coupling in topological magnets.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27076"></a>
### [Superconductivity-Enabled Conversion of Ferromagnetic Resonance into Standing Spin Waves](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27076v1)

**Authors:** Ya. V. Turkin, N. G. Pugach, F. M. Maksimov, A. S. Pakhomov, A. I. Chernov, V. I. Belotelov, S. N. Polulyakh, V. S. Stolyarov  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27076v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **2/5**

**Summary.** The researchers show that a superconductor can act as a control knob to convert uniform magnetic resonance into standing spin waves in an adjacent insulator. This process is driven by spin-transfer torque from triplet Cooper pairs and electromagnetic effects from superconducting vortices. This discovery opens new ways to manipulate magnonic modes using superconducting states.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Exploring the coherent coupling between a diffusive superconductor and short-wavelength magnons in an adjacent insulating magnet to enable mode conversion.

**Main result.** Demonstrated that superconductivity can convert uniform ferromagnetic resonance (FMR) into perpendicular standing spin waves (PSSWs) via spin-polarized triplet Cooper pairs and electromagnetic proximity from Abrikceptov vortices.

**Method.** Experimental microwave transmission measurements on bilayers combined with a microscopic theory coupling the Keldysh-Usadel description of superconductivity to Landau-Lifshitz-Gilbert dynamics.

**Model / system.** Bi-substituted iron-garnet/Nb bilayers consisting of a ferrimagnetic insulator adjacent to a conventional diffusive superconductor.

**Key observables.** Microwave transmission resonance features and magnetic susceptibility lineshapes.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Nb transition temperature, spin-polarized triplet Cooper pair-mediated torque, and Abrikosov vortex-induced effective field.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The superconductor is treated as a conventional diffusive system described by the Keldysh-Usadel formalism.

**Paper structure.** The paper presents an experimental demonstration of FMR to PSSW conversion in bilayers, followed by a microscopic theoretical derivation using Keldysh-Usadel and LLG dynamics to explain the mechanism.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Superconductors can transport spin without Joule dissipation, yet their coherent coupling to short-wavelength magnons in insulating magnets remains largely unexplored. Here we demonstrate experimentally and theoretically that a conventional diffusive superconductor can enable the conversion of the uniform ferromagnetic-resonance (FMR) mode into perpendicular standing spin waves (PSSWs) in an adjacent ferrimagnetic insulator. In Bi-substituted iron-garnet/Nb bilayers, the microwave transmission develops an additional resonance feature that appears only below the Nb transition temperature and lies close to the uniform FMR peak. A microscopic theory that self-consistently couples the quasiclassical Keldysh--Usadel description of the superconducting condensate to the Landau--Lifshitz--Gilbert dynamics shows that the conversion requires two ingredients: (i) an interfacial spin-transfer torque mediated by spin-polarized triplet Cooper pairs and (ii) a depth-dependent effective field produced by Abrikosov vortices (electromagnetic proximity). The resulting susceptibility reproduces the measured lineshapes and establishes superconductivity as an active control knob for exchange standing-wave modes in magnetic insulators.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27946"></a>
### [Theory of quantum decoherence in macroscopic topological insulators](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27946v1)

**Authors:** Xian-Peng Zhang, Yan-Qing Feng, Wanxiang Feng, Yugui Yao  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27946v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

**Summary.** This paper develops a theory to explain how quantum decoherence affects the spin Hall effect in large-scale topological insulators. It reveals a new, stronger mechanism for the extrinsic spin Hall effect and predicts a specific scaling law that can be used to experimentally detect decoherence effects. This work highlights the importance of decoherence in the development of future spintronic technologies.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a new perspective on how environmental coupling (decoherence) fundamentally alters transport properties and introduces a new mechanism for the spin Hall effect through second-order scattering.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Understanding the microscopic mechanisms of quantum decoherence and its impact on the quantum spin Hall effect in macroscopic, infinite-size topological insulators.

**Main result.** The authors identify a new second-order skew-scattering mechanism for the extrinsic spin Hall effect tied to decoherence and predict a new scaling law where spin Hall conductivity scales quadratically with longitudinal conductivity.

**Method.** Development of a comprehensive theoretical framework to quantitatively investigate decoherence-induced corrections in topological transport.

**Model / system.** Macroscopic topological insulators subject to interactions with an environment and impurity-induced scattering.

**Key observables.** Quantum spin Hall conductivity, longitudinal conductivity, and impurity density scaling.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Impurity density and the relationship between spin Hall and longitudinal conductivity.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The theory focuses on the regime of macroscopic, infinite-size topological insulators.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of decoherence in topological insulators, develops a theory for decoherence-induced corrections, identifies a new skew-scattering mechanism, and concludes with a predicted experimental scaling law.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum decoherence-the loss of quantum coherence due to interactions with an environment-plays a central role in quantum transport, and controlling this ubiquitous yet inevitable phenomenon is essential for practical quantum technologies. Despite its importance, the microscopic mechanisms of decoherence in infinite-size topological insulators remain poorly understood. Here, we develop a comprehensive theory that quantitatively investigates how quantum decoherence shapes the quantum spin Hall effect in macroscopic topological insulators, and reveal that decoherence-induced corrections scale quadratically with impurity density. Besides, we uncover a previously unidentified mechanism of the extrinsic spin Hall effect: a second-order skew-scattering process intrinsically tied to quantum decoherence-fundamentally distinct from, yet substantially stronger than, the conventional third-order skew-scattering mechanism. Furthermore, we predict a new scaling law in which the decoherence-induced spin Hall conductivity scales quadratically with the longitudinal conductivity, providing a clear experimental signature of decoherence effects. Our results establish the essential role of decoherence in quantum transport of topological insulators and reveal that macroscopic topological insulators offer a promising platform for next-generation spintronic applications.

</details>

### [Transport Detection of Whirlpools in GaAs Electron Liquid](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27860v1)

**Summary.** This paper reports the experimental detection of steady-state electron whirlpools in a GaAs-based 2D electron liquid using transport measurements. By showing that the appearance of negative resistance scales with the Gurzhi length, the authors provide strong evidence for hydrodynamic flow and probe the limits of the hydrodynamic analogy.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The difficulty of directly observing and characterizing hydrodynamic vortices (electron whirlpools) in electron liquids and distinguishing them from ballistic transport.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate the formation of large-scale steady-state whirlpools in a GaAs electron liquid, showing that the transition to negative resistance scales universally with the Gurzhi length (D/D_nu approx 8.0).

**Method.** The study combines four-terminal transport measurements in GaAs/AlGaAs heterostructures with numerical hydrodynamic simulations and scaling analysis.

**Model / system.** A high-mobility two-dimensional electron gas (2DEG) in a GaAs/AlGaAs heterostructure, featuring a device geometry with circular cavities adjoining a wide conducting channel.

**Key observables.** Four-terminal resistance (R), potential difference (V), and current streamlines.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Gurzhi length (D_nu), cavity diameter (D), temperature (T), electron density (n), and mobility (mu).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The hydrodynamic model assumes a slip boundary condition consistent with previously reported perfect slip flow in GaAs.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows device schematics and simulated current/potential maps; Fig 2 shows potential difference; Fig 3 shows the scaling of Gurzhi length with cavity diameter; Fig 4 shows resistance in a reference geometry without vortices.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of vortex detection, describes the GaAs experimental platform and device geometry, presents transport measurements and scaling analysis, compares results to numerical simulations, and concludes with a discussion on the breakdown of standard viscosity relations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We report the formation of large-scale steady-state whirlpools in a GaAs-based two-dimensional electron liquid and demonstrate them by straightforward transport measurements. A whirlpool forming inside a circular cavity adjoining a wide conducting channel appears as a negative four-terminal resistance over a broad range of temperatures and cavity sizes. The effect scales with the Gurzhi length, in quantitative accord with the hydrodynamic analogy. Obtained results firmly establish this analogy and probe the limits of its applicability.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27554"></a>
### [Topological antiqued mechanical toy](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27554v1)

**Authors:** Hirofumi Wada, Hayato Mizobata, Shuto Ueno, Taiju Yoneda  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27554v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `Frenkel-Kontorova` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

**Summary.** This paper investigates the mechanics of the 'Jacob's ladder' toy, identifying its flipping waves as topological solitons. It reveals that unlike standard topological chains, the toy's flexibility allows for the coexistence of kink and antikink waves. The study uses a combination of experiment and theory to show how symmetry and gravity drive these unique motions.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to uncover the physical origin and design principles behind the unidirectional flipping waves in the 'Jacob's ladder' mechanical toy.

**Main result.** The researchers demonstrate that the toy's waves are a class of topological solitons arising from bistability under gravity, and that its symmetric connection creates a topologically singular state allowing for kink-antikink coexistence.

**Method.** The study combines water-tank experiments, numerical simulations, and analytical theory including the use of the index theorem.

**Model / system.** A mechanical frame known as 'Jacob's ladder' consisting of rigid blocks connected by strings, analyzed under the influence of gravity and pretension.

**Key observables.** Unidirectional flipping waves, kink and antikink waves, and the pair annihilation of kink-antikink waves.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Gravity, pretension, and the symmetry/asymmetry of the connections.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes the toy's behavior can be modeled by comparing it to the Kane-Lubensky topological chain and analyzing its bistability.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with experimental verification in a water tank to rule out domino effects, followed by analytical demonstration of bistability and topological soliton properties, a comparison with the Kane-Lubensky chain, an application of the index theorem to identify differences, and finally an analysis of asymmetric toy configurations and wave annihilation.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

{\it Jacob's ladder} -- a classic children's toy -- is a simple mechanical frame comprising rigid blocks connected by strings that shows curious unidirectional flipping waves. Nonetheless, its physical origin remains elusive. By combining experiment, numeral simulation, and theory, we show that understanding the underlying design principle of this toy requires diverse physical ideas. First, we conduct a water-tank experiment that excludes the domino-like mechanism, thus defying widespread expectations. Subsequently, we analytically demonstrate that the toy is bistable under gravity, thus implying its kink wave as a class of topological solitons. The waves are surprisingly reminiscent -- both experimentally and theoretically -- to those in the Kane--Lubensky topological chain, owing to the stiffening of zero modes by the pretension under gravity. However, a close examination based on the index theorem reveals that the similarity remains superficial and that the floppiness of the toy underlies the kink and antikink coexistence -- a forbidden mode in the topological chain. By analyzing a generalized asymmetric toy, we reveal that its symmetric connection renders it topologically singular, thus resulting in amusing motions. We demonstrate these ideas by experimentally observing a dramatic pair annihilation of kink and antikink waves.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.28008"></a>
### [Compressibility of micromagnetic solutions in tensor train format](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28008v1)

**Authors:** Thierry Valet, Nicolas Vukadinovic  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** numerical methods · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.28008v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

Closure domains and a sur- face vortex are clearly visible, highlighting the non-trivial and fully three-dimensional micromagnetic configuration.</figcaption>
</figure>

(a) CR as a function the MPVE for the TT-TCI compressed magnetization data of the 200 × 100 × 100nm3 prism as the discretization is refined. </div>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that using Tensor Train compression for micromagnetic simulations can bypass the cubic scaling bottleneck of traditional 3D methods. By exploiting the spatial sparsity of domain walls and edges, the computational complexity scales much more favorably with both system size and grid resolution. This provides a foundation for developing highly efficient, tensor-network-based micromagnetic solvers.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Standard 3D micromagnetic simulations suffer from severe computational bottlenecks due to cubic scaling with object size and grid refinement, failing to exploit the inherent spatial sparsity of magnetic structures.

**Main result.** Tensor Train (TT) representations successfully exploit informational sparsity, achieving sub-cubic scaling of approximately L^1.8 and (1/a)^1.2, which significantly outperforms traditional dense discretization.

**Method.** The authors use Tensor Cross-Interpolation (TCI) via the tntorch package to compress magnetization data obtained from GPU-accelerated MuMax3 simulations.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on 3D equilibrium flux-closure states in soft-magnetic rectangular prisms with a 2:1:1 aspect ratio, characterized by localized domain walls and vortices.

**Key observables.** Compressed Parameter Count (CPC), Compression Ratio (CR), and Maximal Pointwise Vector Error (MPVE).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Exchange length (approx. 5.69 nm), saturation magnetization (8.0 x 10^5 A/m), and exchange stiffness (1.3 x 10^-11 J/m).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the magnetic state can be effectively captured by the Tensor Train format due to the presence of low-dimensional high-gradient regions.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a 3D flux-closure state; Figure 2 plots compression ratio and parameter count scaling against prism size; Figure 3 shows compression ratio and parameter count scaling against grid refinement.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the scaling bottleneck of micromagnetics, presents the Tensor Train compression approach, demonstrates scaling advantages through variable-size and variable-refinement simulation series, and concludes with implications for future TT-based solvers.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

For three-dimensional (3D) magnetic objects with linear size $L$ exceeding a few exchange lengths, the micromagnetic state exhibits pronounced informational sparsity: low-dimensional, high-gradient regions (e.g., domain walls) coexist with near-uniformly magnetized volumetric domains. Because standard micromagnetic simulation methods discretize the magnetization on near-uniform 3D grids with linear cell size $a$, they cannot take advantage of this sparsity. The computational problem scales as $\sim L^3$ and $\sim (1/a)^3$. In this Letter, we establish that direct tensor-train (TT) representations overcome these poor scalings by exploiting the spatial sparsity optimally, while preserving accuracy in a controlled way. Focusing on representative flux-closure configurations in soft-magnetic rectangular prisms, in the near-micrometer regime, we demonstrate that the parameter count of TT-compressed micromagnetic data scales approximately as $L^{1.8}$ and $(1/a)^{1.2}$. Hence the relative advantage over dense discretizations rapidly grows with the problem size and refinement level. These first results provide a strong motivation for future developments of micromagnetic solvers in TT format which could transcend the limitations of traditional simulators, with far reaching potential impacts on fundamental research and technology development.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27236"></a>
### [Dual role of core electrons in electronic friction](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27236v1)

**Authors:** Runfeng Zhou, Emilio Artacho  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27236v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

**Summary.** This paper investigates how core electrons influence the energy loss of ions passing through matter. It discovers that core electrons both add a new way for energy to dissipate and reduce the excitation of valence electrons through electron capture. This finding challenges standard semi-empirical models and provides a new perspective on ion-matter interactions.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Understanding the role of core electrons in non-equilibrium energy dissipation and electronic stopping power during swift-ion irradiation.

**Main result.** The study identifies a dual role for core electrons: they provide an additional dissipation channel through excitation while simultaneously suppressing valence electron excitations via an electron capture process.

**Method.** First-principles calculations using real-time time-dependent density functional theory (rt-TDDFT) with explicit treatment of all electrons.

**Model / system.** Beryllium self-irradiation, treated as a multi-shell swift-ion/matter system.

**Key observables.** Electronic stopping power (Se) and the structure of the Bragg peak.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes the validity of real-time TDDFT for describing the electron dynamics during the ion-matter interaction.

**Paper structure.** The paper presents a problem statement regarding energy dissipation, describes the TDDFT methodology, presents results regarding the Bragg peak structure, discusses the dual mechanism of core electrons, and compares findings to existing models like the shake-up effect in water.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Non-equilibrium energy dissipation in multi-shell swift-ion/matter systems remains a fundamental yet incompletely understood problem, with electronic stopping power $\mathcal{S}_\text{e}$ as a relevant observable for electronic friction. Using real-time time-dependent density functional theory, we perform first-principles calculations of $\mathcal{S}_\text{e}$ for beryllium self-irradiation with explicit treatment of all electrons. Our results reveal a Bragg peak exhibiting a distinct structure which lies beyond the reach of standard semi-empirical models. We attribute its appearance to a dual effect of the presence of core electrons, by which their excitation provides an additional dissipation channel while simultaneously suppressing valence electron excitations. An electron capture process by the projectile's core from the host cores is behind such suppression, rather than Pauli blocking. This dual mechanism contrasts with the shake-up effect reported for water, whereby the inclusion of core electrons enhances valence excitation. Our work provides a new perspective on the effect and importance of core electrons in projectile energy dissipation in matter.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27331"></a>
### [Low-Energy Purification of Crystal Defects by Rydberg Excitons](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27331v1)

**Authors:** Shiva Kant Tiwari, Tijs Karman, Valentin Walther  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27331v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **2/5**

**Summary.** This paper develops a multichannel theory to explain how Rydberg excitons can be used to purify crystals by neutralizing charged impurities. It identifies that while high-energy collisions can hinder purification through elastic and inelastic scattering, an ultralow-energy quantum regime exists that optimizes the purification process. This regime can be experimentally accessed using degenerate two-photon excitation.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a theoretical framework for controlling impurity-exciton interactions using tunable recoil, offering a platform to study exciton-impurity scattering physics that extends beyond the capabilities of traditional atomic gas experiments.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to understand the efficiency of crystal purification via Rydberg excitons by resolving the competing roles of capture, elastic scattering, and inelastic transitions during exciton-impurity collisions.

**Main result.** The authors identify a quantum regime at ultralow collision energies where s-wave scattering enhances capture while suppressing inelastic and elastic channels, thereby maximizing purification efficiency.

**Method.** The authors develop a multichannel theory of Rydberg exciton-impurity scattering to analyze different collision energy regimes.

**Model / system.** The physical system consists of Rydberg excitons in cuprous oxide (Cu2O) interacting with charged impurities in a crystal lattice.

**Key observables.** Capture rate, elastic scattering cross-section, inelastic transition rates, and purification efficiency.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Collision energy, s-wave contribution, and the distinction between single-photon and two-photon excitation regimes.

**Paper structure.** The paper presents a multichannel theory, compares high-energy vs. low-energy collision regimes, and proposes a method for accessing the optimal purification regime via two-photon excitation.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Recent experiments show that optically generated Rydberg excitons in cuprous oxide can neutralize charged impurities, strongly reducing stray electric fields and effectively purifying the crystal. Here, we develop a multichannel theory of Rydberg exciton-impurity scattering that resolves the competing roles of capture, elastic scattering, and inelastic transitions between excitonic states. We find that at high collision energies, as effective under conventional single-photon excitation, purification is reduced relative to Langevin capture. These collisions are accompanied by inelastic redistribution and dominant elastic scattering, including pronounced glory scattering, which suppress purification efficiency. We identify a quantum regime at ultralow collision energies favorable for purification, where only the s-wave contributes: capture is enhanced while elastic and inelastic channels are strongly suppressed. This regime can be accessed via degenerate two-photon excitation of even-parity Rydberg excitons with tunable recoil, additionally enabling the systematic exploration of exciton-impurity scattering over a wide range of collision energies beyond what is readily achievable in atomic counterparts in atomic gas experiments.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27729"></a>
### [On the proposed concept of mechanical phasons in Ni-Mn-Ga modulated martensite](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27729v1)

**Authors:** Petr Sedlák, Tomáš Grabec, Hanuš Seiner  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27729v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Frenkel-Kontorova` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

**Summary.** This paper proposes that the extreme elastic softness observed in Ni-Mn-Ga shape memory alloys is caused by mechanical phasons—dynamic shifts in the modulation phase. The model explains how these phasons relax macroscopic shear loads in commensurate structures but fail to do so in incommensurate ones, reconciling theory with experiment.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Resolving the discrepancy between experimental observations of extreme elastic softening in 10M Ni-Mn-Ga martensite and the much stiffer lattice predicted by first-principles calculations.

**Main result.** The authors propose that 'mechanical phasons' (collective phase shifts in the modulation wave) can relax external shear loads in commensurate and weakly incommensurate regimes, explaining the observed macroscopic compliance.

**Method.** A mechanical model that merges adaptive martensite theory with electronic-structure considerations, using energy minimization of a perturbed modulation function.

**Model / system.** The system is the 10M modulated martensite in Ni-Mn-Ga ferromagnetic shape memory alloys. The model treats the modulation as a periodic atomic displacement that can undergo phase shifts (phasons) within a Landau-type energy landscape.

**Key observables.** Shear modulus (c55), macroscopic monoclinic distortion (gamma), modulation vector (q), and twinning stress.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Modulation wave vector (q), weighting factor (epsilon) controlling the transition from sine-wave to zig-zag modulation, and phason amplitude (delta phi).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The lattice consists of rigid planes that can shift along the modulation amplitude; the modulation is initially assumed to be a simple sine wave.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows phonon branches and the modulation notation; Figure 2 illustrates the energy-minimizing lattice and energy landscape for different epsilon values; Figure 3 shows the cumulative sum of monoclinic distortion and the effect of phase shifts.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the experimental discrepancy, presents a mechanical model of phasons, develops the energy minimization framework for the modulated lattice, analyzes the transition from commensurate to incommensurate regimes, and concludes by linking the model to observed elastic properties.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We discuss modulation phasons as a possible source of unusual elastic behavior of five-layer modulated (10\,M) martensite of the Ni-Mn-Ga shape memory alloy. This material exhibits anomalous macroscopic shear compliance along specific planes perpendicular to the modulation vector, and this compliance disappears when the modulations become incommensurate. Using a simple mechanical model, we show that modulation phasons in Ni-Mn-Ga can have macroscopic mechanical manifestations, and that the resulting 'mechanical phasons' can relax external shear loadings for commensurate and weakly incommensurate modulations, but not for strongly incommensurate modulations. The model merges ideas from the adaptive martensite theory and electronic-structure considerations, and enables straightforward explanations of several properties of the 10\,M lattice, such as spontaneous monoclinic distortion or easy formation and propagation of $a/b$ twins.

</details>

### [Quantum Metric and Nonlinear Hall Effect of Photons](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27751v1)

**Authors:** Keidai Akiba, Naoki Yamamoto  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27751v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27751_figures/2604.27751_page4.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 4</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.27751_figures/2604.27751_page5.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 5</figcaption>

</div>

**Summary.** The paper shows that photons possess a nontrivial quantum metric in momentum space. This metric leads to a nonlinear Hall effect in media with varying refractive indices and introduces $O(\hbar^2)$ corrections to gravitational lensing in curved spacetime. These effects represent a higher-order geometric correction to light propagation that does not depend on photon helicity.

**Why it may be interesting.** This paper is interesting for quantum optics researchers as it identifies a new class of nonlinear geometric effects (the nonlinear Hall effect of light) that arises from the momentum-space geometry of photons, extending beyond the well-known spin Hall effect.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates whether photons possess a nontrivial quantum metric in momentum space and explores the resulting nonlinear transport phenomena, such as a nonlinear Hall effect in inhomogeneous media and gravitational lensing corrections in curved spacetime.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that photons have a nontrivial quantum metric that induces a trajectory shift at second order in the derivatives of the refractive index (nonlinear Hall effect of light) and provides $O(\hbar^2)$ corrections to gravitational lensing.

**Method.** The study employs the path-integral formalism and perturbative diagonalization methods (Luttinger-Kohn and Schrieffer-Wolff) to derive the semiclassical action and equations of motion.

**Model / system.** The system consists of photons in either inhomogeneous media with a varying refractive index $n(x)$ or in a static, weak background gravitational field, modeled using a spin-1 Hamiltonian $H = \pm c \mathbf{S} \cdot \mathbf{p}$.

**Key observables.** Quantum metric ($G_{ij}$), Berry curvature ($\Omega$), photon trajectory shifts, and gravitational lensing corrections.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Semiclassical limit, geometric-optics limit, weak gravitational field limit ($|\phi|/c^2 \ll 1$), and the scaling of effects by powers of $\hbar$ (specifically $O(\hbar)$ spin Hall effect vs $O(\hbar^2)$ nonlinear Hall effect).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Static and weak gravitational fields, linear wave equation requirements, and the geometric-optics limit where the wavelength is much smaller than the spacetime curvature radius.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with a path-integral derivation of the photon's semiclassical action, moves to the derivation of the quantum metric and Berry curvature, then applies these to light in inhomogeneous media, and concludes with the application to gravitational lensing in curved spacetime.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Using the path-integral formalism, we show that photons possess a nontrivial quantum metric in momentum space. We derive the semiclassical action and equations of motion by taking into account the quantum metric. In media with a spatially varying refractive index $n(\mathbf{x})$, the quantum metric induces a shift in the trajectory of light at second order in derivatives of $n$, which may be regarded as a nonlinear Hall effect of light. The quantum metric also gives rise to corrections to gravitational lensing in curved spacetime at the nonlinear order in wavelength. This gravitational nonlinear Hall effect results from the interplay between the geometry of position space and that of momentum space.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27342"></a>
### [Ultrafast Sliding Ferroelectric Switching in Bilayer Hexagonal Boron Nitride Revealed by Deep Learning Molecular Dynamics](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27342v1)

**Authors:** Yinan Wang, Poyen Chen, Teruyasu Mizoguchi  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27342v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

**Summary.** This paper uses machine learning-enhanced molecular dynamics to simulate the switching of ferroelectricity in bilayer h-BN. It demonstrates that polarization switching occurs via an extremely fast, 5-picosecond rigid sliding process. This work provides a computational bridge between atomistic dynamics and experimental observations of ferroelectric hysteresis.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Understanding the atomistic dynamics of electric-field-driven polarization switching in sliding ferroelectric bilayer h-BN.

**Main result.** The study reveals an ultrafast switching mechanism consisting of coherent single-domain rigid sliding that completes within 5 ps, reproducing experimental ferroelectric hysteresis loops.

**Method.** A data-driven framework integrating a MACE machine learning potential with an equivariant graph convolutional neural network (EGCNN) for real-time Born effective charge prediction, combined with real-space path-integral polarization formalism.

**Model / system.** AB-stacked bilayer hexagonal boron nitride (h-BN) under applied electric fields.

**Key observables.** Spontaneous polarization, Born effective charge (BEC), and ferroelectric hysteresis loops.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Switching timescale (5 ps), electric field strength, and AB-stacking configuration.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The use of a fine-tuned MACE machine learning potential and a specific Gaussian convolution background extraction procedure to isolate intrinsic polarization.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of sliding ferroelectricity, presents a coupled atomistic framework using ML potentials and EGCNN, describes the polarization extraction methodology, and concludes with the simulation of ultrafast switching and hysteresis loops.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Sliding ferroelectricity in bilayer hexagonal boron nitride (h-BN) offers compelling prospects for next-generation non-volatile memory, yet the atomistic dynamics of electric-field-driven polarization switching remain poorly understood. Here, we present a fully data-driven, coupled atomistic framework that integrates a fine-tuned MACE machine learning potential (MLP) with an equivariant graph convolutional neural network (EGCNN) for real-time Born effective charge (BEC) prediction, enabling large-scale non-equilibrium molecular dynamics simulations of AB-stacked bilayer h-BN under applied electric fields. By implementing a rigorous real-space path-integral polarization formalism combined with a state-constrained Gaussian convolution background extraction procedure, we successfully isolate the intrinsic spontaneous polarization from the dominant dielectric background. Our simulations reveal that coherent single-domain rigid sliding, completing within 5 ps, constitutes a physically viable ultrafast switching mechanism, and reproduces clean ferroelectric hysteresis loops whose shape is qualitatively consistent with experimental observations.

</details>

### [A nanoionic diode: Equilibrium rectifying junction enabling large and stable resistance variations](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28029v1)

**Authors:** Chuanlian Xiao, Joachim Maier  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.28029v1>

**Summary.** The paper introduces a new class of nanoionic diodes that operate in thermodynamic equilibrium, avoiding the drift issues of traditional semiconductors. By using mixed conductors like Li-intercalated TiO2, the device achieves stable, highly tunable rectification even at the nanoscale and high temperatures.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Conventional semiconductor diodes (p-n and Schottky) rely on non-equilibrium frozen dopant profiles that are unstable and prone to drift when scaled to the nanoscale or exposed to high temperatures.

**Main result.** The development of a nanoionic diode based on an equilibrium Gouy-Chapman space charge layer that is stable against drift, highly tunable, and capable of high current-on/off ratios exceeding 6-7 orders of magnitude.

**Method.** The researchers used electrochemical tuning to control lithium content in TiO2 films on Ru and performed temperature-dependent I-V measurements, impedance spectroscopy, and dielectric modulus analysis.

**Model / system.** A nanoionic rectifier consisting of a mixed ionic/electronic conductor interface, specifically Li-intercalated TiO2 (anatase) films on a Ruthenium (Ru) substrate, operating under a Gouy-Chapman model.

**Key observables.** Current-on/off ratio, rectification function, resistance (R_perpendicular), capacitance (C_perpendicular), point of zero charge (PZC), and chemical diffusion coefficient.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Mobility hierarchy (u_electron >> u_ion >> 0), temperature range (-196 C to 230 C), lithium content (x), and nanometer-scale screening lengths.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumed activation energy of 0.5 eV for diffusion calculations and that the Debye length is smaller than the lattice parameter for space charge control.

**Figures summary.** Schematic of the device; I-V curves showing rectification and lithium-content dependence; impedance and dielectric modulus plots for identifying interfacial vs. bulk contributions and the point of zero charge; energy level diagrams under different bias conditions.

**Paper structure.** Introduction of the instability problem in conventional diodes, description of the nanoionic equilibrium mechanism, experimental setup and material characterization, demonstration of rectification and stability, and discussion of scalability and diffusion dynamics.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We report on a new type of rectifier which is in full contact equilibrium and thus, if down-sized to the nanoscale, shows no drift even if exposed to elevated temperatures and/or extreme waiting times. This is in contrast to existing diodes which rely on frozen doping profiles and are hence non-equilibrium devices. Our rectifiers are related to Schottky diodes but employ "dopants" whose mobilities are high enough to follow the electrical field quickly but low enough to not compete with the electrons in terms of conductivities. In order to realize such a device based on mixed conductors, we use nanosized TiO2 films on Ru as a substrate which can store Li at the interface according to a job-sharing mechanism (Li-ions on the TiO2 side, electrons on the Ru side). The excellent functionality of this nanoionic device is demonstrated (e.g., current on-off ratio can exceed 6-7 orders of magnitude) and the additional advantages stressed (such as ease of preparation and tuning the characteristics electrochemically).

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27876"></a>
### [Acoustic modulation of shear thickening transition in dense adhesive suspensions](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27876v1)

**Authors:** Aoxuan Wang, Fabrice Toussaint, Thomas Gibaud  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27876v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable

**Summary.** This study demonstrates that ultrasound can control the flow of dense suspensions by shifting the onset of shear thickening to higher shear rates. It reveals that acoustic waves destabilize the internal force networks through localized hydrodynamic fluctuations. This provides a mechanism for using sound to prevent flow instabilities in industrial processing.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Understanding the physical origin of how high-power ultrasound reduces the apparent viscosity and modifies the discontinuous shear thickening (DST) transition in dense adhesive suspensions.

**Main result.** Ultrasound does not directly reduce viscosity but shifts the DST transition toward higher shear rates by destabilizing force networks through fluctuating hydrodynamic forces.

**Method.** The researchers used a rheo-ultrasound setup to study dense adhesive cornstarch suspensions under acoustic modulation and analyzed stress probability distributions.

**Model / system.** Dense adhesive cornstarch suspensions characterized by fragile, load-bearing force networks embedded in heterogeneous density-wave structures.

**Key observables.** Apparent viscosity, shear rate, stress probability distributions, and the shear-thickening transition threshold.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Shear rate, ultrasound power/frequency, and the separation of time scales between acoustic oscillations and suspension dynamics.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The suspension can be modeled as an effectively immobile porous medium subjected to high-frequency interstitial flows during the acoustic period.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of DST-induced instabilities, describes the rheo-ultrasound experimental setup, presents the observation of the shifted transition via stress distribution collapse, proposes a mechanism involving hydrodynamic fluctuations and time-scale separation, and concludes with the implications for controlling flows.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Discontinuous shear thickening (DST) in dense suspensions leads to flow instabilities that limit processing in many systems. While high-power ultrasound has been reported to reduce the apparent viscosity of such materials, the origin of this effect remains unclear. Here, we investigate dense adhesive cornstarch suspensions, where shear thickening arises from fragile, load-bearing force networks embedded in heterogeneous density-wave structures. Using a rheo-ultrasound setup, we show that ultrasound does not directly reduce viscosity but instead shifts the shear-thickening transition toward higher shear rates. This is evidenced by the collapse of stress probability distributions onto master curves, revealing a continuous evolution toward more fluid-like states without a sharp threshold. We interpret these results through a separation of time scales, in which the suspension behaves as an effectively immobile porous medium subjected to high-frequency interstitial flows. Fluidization then arises from a combination of boundary slip, bulk destabilization of force networks by drag-force fluctuations, and localized acoustic streaming. Beyond these mechanisms, we propose that ultrasound modifies the stability of force networks by introducing fluctuating hydrodynamic forces at the pore scale. As a result, larger stresses or shear rates are required to sustain jammed states, leading to a continuous renormalization of the DST transition. These findings provide a consistent physical picture of acoustic fluidization in adhesive suspensions and establish ultrasound as a powerful tool to control discontinuous shear thickening in confined flows.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.28066"></a>
### [Anharmonic phonon coupling enabled by local inversion symmetry breaking at domain walls in ferroelastic](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.28066v1)

**Authors:** Seyyed Jabbar Mousavi, Vivek Unikandanunni, Niccolo Sellati, Paolo Barone, Martina Basini, Steven L. Johnson, Andrey Shalit, Peter Hamm, Mattia Udina, Thomas Feurer  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.28066v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable

**Summary.** The researchers used 2D Raman-terahertz spectroscopy to demonstrate that ferroelastic domain walls in LaAlO3 break inversion symmetry, enabling previously hidden anharmonic phonon coupling. This discovery provides a way to probe subtle symmetry-breaking effects in complex materials using nonlinear spectroscopy.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating whether local inversion symmetry breaking at ferroelastic domain walls enables anharmonic phonon coupling that is otherwise absent in the centrosymmetric bulk.

**Main result.** Direct evidence of mechanical and electrical anharmonic coupling between A1g and Eg phonons was observed in LaAlO3, driven by local symmetry breaking at domain walls.

**Method.** Two-dimensional Raman-terahertz spectroscopy was used to detect cross-peaks in the spectra.

**Model / system.** The physical system is ferroelastic LaAlO3, specifically focusing on the lattice dynamics at twin domain walls where inversion symmetry is locally broken.

**Key observables.** Cross-peaks in two-dimensional Raman-terahertz spectra, A1g Raman-active phonon, and Eg infrared-active phonon.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Local inversion symmetry breaking at domain walls; mechanical and electrical anharmonicity.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The bulk crystal is assumed to be centrosymmetric, with symmetry breaking restricted to the domain walls.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the concept of local symmetry breaking in ferroelastic domain walls, presents experimental evidence using 2D Raman-THz spectroscopy, and attributes the observed signals to specific phonon coupling mechanisms.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

In ferroelastic materials, spontaneous symmetry breaking leads to the formation of twin domains. Although the bulk crystal typically remains centrosymmetric, inversion symmetry can be locally broken at the domain walls, potentially changing phonon selection rules and enabling local anharmonic phonon coupling. Here we report direct evidence of such anharmonic coupling in ferroelastic LaAlO$_3$ using two-dimensional Raman-terahertz spectroscopy. We attribute the cross-peaks observed in the two-dimensional spectra to both mechanical and electrical anharmonicity between the $A_{1g}$ Raman-active phonon and the $E_g$ phonon, which acquires finite infrared activity through local inversion symmetry breaking at ferroelastic domain walls. These findings provide new insight into the complex lattice dynamics of ferroelastic materials and highlight the potential of two-dimensional Raman-terahertz spectroscopy to uncover subtle symmetry breaking through the detection of intrinsically weak anharmonic signals.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.27266"></a>
### [AutoREC: A software platform for developing reinforcement learning agents for equivalent circuit model generation from electrochemical impedance spectroscopy data](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27266v1)

**Authors:** Ali Jaberi, Yonatan Kurniawan, Robert Black, Shayan Mousavi M., Kabir Verma, Zoya Sadighi, Santiago Miret, Jason Hattrick-Simpers  
**Type:** both · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27266v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable

**Summary.** This paper introduces AutoREC, a Python package that uses reinforcement learning to automatically create circuit models for interpreting electrochemical data. By treating model generation as a decision-making task, it eliminates the need for manual expert intervention. This automation is a key step toward enabling self-driving laboratories for materials science.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Automating the generation of equivalent circuit models (ECMs) from electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) data to replace manual, trial-and-error identification processes.

**Main result.** The development of AutoREC, an open-source platform that uses reinforcement learning to achieve a 99.6% success rate on synthetic data and demonstrates strong generalization to experimental battery and corrosion data.

**Method.** The authors formulate ECM construction as a sequential decision-making problem using a Markov Decision Process and implement a Double Deep Q-Network with prioritized experience replay and a dead-loop mitigation strategy.

**Model / system.** The system involves electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) data from various electrochemical systems, including batteries, corrosion, oxygen evolution reaction, and CO2 reduction.

**Key observables.** Equivalent circuit models (ECMs) and impedance spectra.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The paper discusses the existence of limitations in current agent designs and potential strategies for future mitigation.

**Paper structure.** Introduction of the AutoREC package, formulation of the ECM construction as a Markov Decision Process, implementation details of the RL agent (Double DQN), evaluation on synthetic and experimental datasets, and discussion of limitations and future work.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

This paper introduces AutoREC, an open-source Python package for developing reinforcement learning (RL) agents to automatically generate equivalent circuit models (ECMs) from electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) data. While ECMs are a standard framework for interpreting EIS data, traditional identification is typically based on manual trial-and-error, which requires domain experts and limits scalability, particularly in autonomous experimental pipelines such as self-driving laboratories. AutoREC addresses this challenge by formulating ECM construction as a sequential decision-making problem within a Markov Decision Process framework. It implements a Double Deep Q-Network with prioritized experience replay, along with a dedicated dead-loop mitigation strategy, to efficiently explore a complex action space for circuit generation. To demonstrate the capabilities of the platform, we trained an RL agent using AutoREC and evaluated its strengths and limitations across diverse datasets, while also discussing possible strategies to mitigate these limitations in future agent designs. The trained agent achieved a success rate exceeding $99.6\%$ on synthetic datasets and demonstrated strong generalization to unseen experimental EIS data from batteries, corrosion, oxygen evolution reaction, and CO$_2$ reduction systems. These results position AutoREC as a promising platform for adaptive and data-driven ECM generation, with potential for integration into automated electrochemical workflows.

</details>

### [Complex Effects of Salt on Small-Angle X-ray Scattering of BSA Originate From the Interplay of Ions and Hydration Water](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27913v1)

**Authors:** Anshika Dhiman, Sanbo Qin, Huan-Xiang Zhou  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27913v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable

**Summary.** This paper investigates how salt concentrations affect the structural properties of the protein BSA as measured by SAXS. By combining advanced computational modeling with molecular dynamics, the authors show that these effects are driven by the complex interaction between ions and the surrounding hydration water. This provides a clearer understanding of how salt environments influence biomolecules.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The challenge of accurately extracting information regarding the effects of salt concentrations on protein structures from Small-angle X-ray scattering (SAXS) data.

**Main result.** The study demonstrates that the complex effects of salt on the SAXS of BSA arise from the interplay between ions and hydration water, providing a unified picture of protein-ion-water interactions.

**Method.** The researchers combined the newly developed FMAPIq approach with explicit-solvent all-atom molecular dynamics simulations.

**Model / system.** The physical system studied is bovine serum albumin (BSA) in protein solutions at various salt concentrations, involving the interaction between the protein, ions, and hydration water.

**Key observables.** Small-angle X-ray scattering (SAXS) profiles and protein-ion-water interaction dynamics.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Salt concentrations and the role of hydration water.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study utilizes explicit-solvent all-atom molecular dynamics simulations to model the environment.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of salt effects on proteins, describes the application of the FMAPIq approach and molecular dynamics simulations, and concludes with a general picture of protein-ion-water interactions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Salts are an integral part of the environment for living systems and, therefore, understanding their effects on proteins and other biomolecules is of fundamental interest. Small-angle X-ray scattering (SAXS) of protein solutions can provide valuable information on salt effects, but extracting this information has been a significant challenge. For example, SAXS data of bovine serum albumin (BSA) at various salt concentrations were fit to three different spherical models. Here we combined the newly developed FMAPIq approach with explicit-solvent all-atom molecular dynamics simulations to show that the complex effects of salt on the SAXS of BSA originate from the interplay of ions and hydration water, leading to a general picture of protein-ion-water interactions.

</details>

### [Conditional Generative Models Enable Targeted Exploration of MAX Phase Design Space](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27709v1)

**Authors:** Jamie Swain, Cyprien Bone, Matthew T. Darby, Ewan Galloway, Keith T. Butler  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27709v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable

**Summary.** The researchers developed a fine-tuned large language model called CrystaLLM-pi to navigate the complex chemical space of MAX phases. By using specific conditioning vectors, they successfully targeted regions likely to produce stable MXenes, significantly increasing the discovery rate of new materials.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Efficiently exploring the vast compositional space of MAX phases to identify new, stable candidates for MXene production.

**Main result.** The CrystaLLM-pi model, using a two-dimensional conditioning vector, doubled the generation rate of novel stable structures and produced candidates with DFT-validated stability.

**Method.** Fine-tuning a large language model (CrystaLLM-pi) on 6,179 MAX phases and employing a conditioning vector based on MXene derivative count and A-site binding energy.

**Model / system.** The system involves MAX phases (M_{n+1}AX_n), which are layered ternary carbides/nitrides that serve as precursors to MXenes.

**Key observables.** Energy above hull (E_hull), MXene derivative count, and A-site binding energy.

**Important parameters / regimes.** E_hull < 0.050 eV/atom for stability; two-dimensional conditioning vector.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes that the learned patterns from the training set of 6,179 phases are sufficient to generalize to out-of-sample structures.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of MAX phase discovery, describes the CrystaLLM-pi architecture and training data, details the conditioning method for targeted generation, and validates the results using DFT stability calculations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

MAX phases (M$_{n+1}$AX$_n$), precursors to MXenes, span a vast compositional space, motivating efficient computational screening for synthesisable candidates. We employ CrystaLLM$-π$, a large language model fine-tuned on 6,179 double transition-metal MAX phases, and demonstrate its ability to generate out-of-sample structures consistent with known experimental trends. Using a conditioning vector with two dimensions (a statistically derived MXene derivative count and a surrogate for A-site binding energy), the model was able to target MXene-favourable regions of phase space for generation. Specific condition vectors double novel stable structure generation rates versus unconditioned baselines. Of ten compositionally novel candidates, five exhibit DFT-validated stability ($E_{hull} < 0.050$ eV/atom). This work showcases the potential for autoregressive generative models to explore targeted materials' spaces, offering a scalable framework for accelerated discovery in compositionally complex systems.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.27240"></a>
### [Confinement-Connectivity Coupling Enables High-Efficiency Piezoionic Transduction](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27240v1)

**Authors:** Tofayel Ahammad Ovee, Daniel Kroeger, Jean-François Louf  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27240v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable

**Summary.** The paper presents a new way to design hydrogels for bioelectronics by using a specific pore structure to prevent ion dissipation. By controlling both connectivity and confinement, the researchers created a material that generates significant electrical power from mechanical movement, even enough to trigger nerve responses.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Piezoionic hydrogels suffer from low output efficiency because rapid, symmetric ion redistribution dissipates the charge gradients needed for signal generation.

**Main result.** A confinement-connectivity design strategy using a mesoporous PVA-glycerol-CB[5] network enables high-efficiency piezoionic transduction, producing up to 180 mV and 9 mA and driving electromyographic responses in nerves.

**Method.** The researchers implemented a layered Negative-Neutral-Positive architecture in a supramolecular mesoporous network to simultaneously increase pore fraction and reduce pore size.

**Model / system.** A supramolecular poly(vinyl alcohol)-glycerol-cucurbit[5]uril (PVA-glycerol-CB[5]) mesoporous hydrogel with a layered architecture designed to regulate ionic transport.

**Key observables.** Peak voltage (~180 mV), peak current (~9 mA), and electromyographic responses in the mouse sciatic nerve.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Pore fraction, characteristic pore size, and the coupling of pore connectivity and confinement.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces a design strategy for hydrogels, describes the implementation of the mesoporous network, demonstrates the resulting electrical output under compression, and validates the device via biological interface testing.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Piezoionic hydrogels offer a route to mechanically driven bioelectronic interfaces, but their output is limited by rapid, symmetric ion redistribution that dissipates charge gradients. In biological electrocytes, efficient signal generation arises from the coupling of ion selectivity with spatial confinement that regulates transport. Here, we introduce a confinement-connectivity design strategy for piezoionic hydrogels, implemented through a supramolecular poly(vinyl alcohol)-glycerol-cucurbit[5]uril (PVA-glycerol-CB[5]) mesoporous network with a layered Negative-Neutral-Positive architecture that simultaneously increases pore fraction while reducing characteristic pore size. This architecture constrains ionic redistribution while maintaining a large mobile-ion reservoir, enabling deformation-driven charge separation. Compression generates peak outputs of ~180 mV and ~9 mA and elicits synchronized electromyographic responses in the mouse sciatic nerve without external power. These results establish confinement-connectivity coupling, rather than bulk conductivity, as a materials design framework in which coupling pore connectivity and confinement governs piezoionic transduction.

</details>

### [Contrasting Effects of Functionalization in Binary and Medium-Entropy MXene Coatings for Corrosion Protection](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27114v1)

**Summary.** This study demonstrates that the effectiveness of MXene-based anti-corrosion coatings depends heavily on whether the MXene is a simple binary structure or a complex medium-entropy alloy. While oxygenation helps protect against corrosion in standard Ti3C2Tx, it actually degrades the performance of more complex multi-metal MXenes by interfering with their ability to form protective oxide layers.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how surface functionalization (oxygenation) and compositional complexity (binary vs. medium-entropy) affect the corrosion protection of MXene-reinforced epoxy coatings.

**Main result.** Oxygenation improves corrosion resistance in binary Ti3C2Tx MXenes but impairs it in medium-entropy (TiVCrMo)C3 MXenes; the latter performs best in its pristine state due to its ability to form protective Cr/Mo-rich oxides.

**Method.** Experimental techniques include EIS, SEM-EDS, AFM, XPS, and XRD, complemented by DFT calculations and Electron Localization Function (ELF) analysis.

**Model / system.** A nacre-inspired multilayer epoxy coating reinforced with either binary (Ti3C2Tx) or medium-entropy (TiVCrMo)C3 MXene layers, applied to C1010 carbon steel substrates.

**Key observables.** Impedance modulus (|Z|), coating resistance (R_coat), charge transfer resistance (R_ct), interlayer spacing, and adsorption energies of O2 and H2O.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Surface terminations (P- vs O-), number of coating layers (3L vs 5L), and elemental composition (binary vs medium-entropy).

**Figures summary.** Figures likely show SEM cross-sections of stratified layers, EDS elemental maps, XRD peak shifts for interlayer spacing, XPS spectra for chemical states, and EIS Bode/Nyquist plots showing impedance changes.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of steel corrosion, describes the fabrication of multilayer MXene-epoxy coatings, presents characterization of morphology and chemistry, compares electrochemical performance of different MXene variants, uses DFT to explain the mechanistic differences in oxygen adsorption, and concludes with design principles.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Developing scalable and environmentally benign anticorrosion coatings is critical for protecting steel infrastructure in chloride-rich environments. Here, a nacre-inspired multilayer epoxy coating reinforced with four MXene systems is investigated. This architecture forms a dense lamellar network that increases diffusion tortuosity and introduces electroactive surfaces for ion interactions. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) confirms that the multilayer design increases coating resistance from ~103 to ~108 Ohm/cm2. A clear performance hierarchy was observed: P-(TiVCrMo)C3 > O-Ti3C2Tx > O-(TiVCrMo)C3 > P-Ti3C2Tx. Density functional theory (DFT) calculations reveal that P-Ti3C2 strongly adsorbs O2, indicating higher surface reactivity, while oxygen termination stabilizes the surface by partially passivating Ti sites. In contrast, P-(TiVCrMo)C3 exhibits strong adsorption of oxygen-containing species due to its multi-metal electronic structure, promoting the formation of protective oxides. These results highlight the delicate balance of surface chemistry, electronic structure, and compositional entropy in designing next-generation MXene-based anticorrosion coatings for marine and industrial environments.

</details>

### [Critical temperatures and critical currents of wide and narrow quasi-one-dimensional superconducting aluminum structures in zero magnetic field](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.27822v1)

**Authors:** V. I. Kuznetsov, O. V. Trofimov  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.27822v1>

**Summary.** This study reveals that narrower superconducting aluminum wires have lower critical temperatures and current densities than wider wires of the same thickness. The authors suggest this is caused by depairing centers on the wire boundaries and observe that the structures can behave as hybrid SNS junctions at certain temperatures.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating why narrower quasi-one-dimensional aluminum structures exhibit lower critical temperatures and critical current densities compared to wider structures of the same thickness.

**Main result.** The researchers discovered that decreasing wire width leads to a decrease in Tc and jc, likely due to the increased influence of depairing centers on dirty longitudinal boundaries. They also identified two distinct temperature regimes for the switching current, including the formation of hybrid SNS junctions.

**Method.** Electrical measurements of voltage-current characteristics and resistance-temperature transitions were performed in zero magnetic field using various measurement circuits and low-noise analog equipment.

**Model / system.** Quasi-one-dimensional superconducting aluminum thin-film structures (thickness 19 nm) with varying widths (0.27 um and 0.48 um) fabricated on a silicon chip.

**Key observables.** Critical temperatures (Tc), critical switching currents (Isw), critical retrapping currents (Iret), and normal-state resistance (Rn).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Dirty limit regime (electron mean free path ~9-9.6 nm), Ginzburg-Landau coherence length (~0.11 um), and London penetration depth (16 nm).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The reduction in Tc is attributed to the depairing effect of magnetic atoms or vacancies on the longitudinal boundaries of the narrow wires.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows resistance vs. temperature plots for different measurement circuits; Figure 2 displays V(I) curves and switching current temperature dependence for wide structures; Figures 3 and 4 show temperature dependence of switching current for narrow structures compared to theoretical predictions.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of Tc discrepancy in asymmetric structures, describes the experimental setup and fabrication of Al wires, presents measurement results for Tc and critical currents, discusses the theoretical modeling using Kupriyanov-Lukichev and GL theories, and concludes with a proposed mechanism involving boundary depairing.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We measured the critical temperatures and critical switching and retrapping currents of wide and narrow thin-film quasi-one-dimensional superconducting aluminum structures of the same thickness in zero magnetic field. For the first time, we found that the narrower the structure, the lower the critical temperature and critical current density in the structure. Probably, the influence of depairing centers that are on dirty longitudinal boundaries of the structure, is the stronger than the narrower the structure. It is found for the first time that, in most cases, the temperature-dependent switching critical current in both structures is approximated by two functions. At temperatures below the temperature corresponding to the bottom of the resistive N-S transition of structures, the switching critical current is described by the Kupriyanov-Lukichev theory. At temperatures close to the top of the N-S transition, the switching current is linear with temperature and coincides with the critical Josephson current. At these temperatures, Josephson SNS junctions are formed in structures.

</details>
